The Red Page
The Sentimental Bloke
I Everybody knows or ought to know The
Sentimental Bloke. He is the most typical
Australian that has ever mooched into Aus-
tralian literature. In C. J. Dennis’s Book-
block Ballads we had the pleasure of meet-
ing him; and now in The Songs of a Senti-
mental Bloke (Angus and Robertson,
Sydney. 3s. 6d.) we renew a treasured
acquaintanceship. As Henry Lawson says
in his enthusiastic foreword, The Senti-
mental Bloke is “more perfect than any
alleged ‘larrikin* or Bottle-O character I
ever attempted to sketch, not even except-
ing my own beloved Benno.” Well, we
should say that Benno and The Sentimental
Bloke, whose other name is Bill, are
cobbers.
Here we have the complete life-history of
The Sentimental Bloke and his Doreen. We
meet him first in “A Spring Song,” when
The young leaves is shootin' on the trees,
The air is like a long, cool sioig o' beer,
and Bill is yearning after his “ideel tart.”
Then we get “The Intro.” to Doreen, whom
Bill has made immortal.
’Er lurk
Wuß pastin' labels in a pickle joint.
A game that — any’ow that ain't the point.
And when Bill* “tried ter chat ’er in the
street,”
The way she tossed 'er 'eod an' swished 'er
skirt!
Oh, it tons dirt!
Poor Bill!
Me! that ’as barracked tarts, an' torked an'
larft,
An' chucked orf at ’em like a phonergraft!
Gorstrooth! I seemed to lose me poio'r o’
speech.
But, 'er! Oh, strike me pinkl She is a
peach !
The sweetest in the barrer! Spare me
days.
I cam't describe that diner’s toinnin’ ways.
The way she forks! ’Er lips! 'er eyes! 'Er
hair!. —
Oh, gimme airl
For :
... .Square an’ all, no matter ’ow yeh start,
The commin end of most of us is — Tart.
“I wisht yeh meant it, Bill,” sighs Doreen,
and speaks of higher things.
Yes, 'ighcr things—that wus the wap she
spoke;
An’ when she looked at me I sorter felt
That bosker feelin’ that comes o'er a bloke,
An’ makes ’im melt;
Makes 'im all ’ot to maul ’er, an’ to shove
'ls arms about ’er... .Bli’mef But it’s love!
But there are limits to Bill’s expression
of the poetry of young love.
The sparklin’ sea all sorter gold an ’ green;
An’ on the pier the band — O 'Ell Doreen!
All Bill can say is “O ’Ell Doreen!”
—and that is the real poetry of passion.
Whati more delicious tribute to her fascina-
tion could any Doreen want? “O 'Ell 1”
is the quintessence of desire, the precipitate
I of all the world’s poetry.
Bill takes her to the play, and there dis-
covers :
Wot’s in a namet Wot’s in a string o'
words t
They scraps in ole Verona wiv the'r swords,
A»’ ncvei' give a bloke a stray dog's chance,
An' that’s Romance. -
But when they deals it out wiv bricks an
boots
In Little Lon. they’re low, degraded broots.
Wot’s jist plain stoush wiv us, right 'ere
to-day,
Is “ valler ” if yer fur enough away.
Some time, some writer bloke toitt do the
trick
Wiv Ginger Mick,
Of Spadger's Lane. ’E’ll be a Romeo,
When 'e’s bin dead five ’undred years or so.
Already that prophecy has been fulfilled.
Ginger Mick, as we learn from recent reports
from Gallipoli, is fighting at the Front. His
“plain stoush,” when dealt out to the Turk,
is rewarded by the Victoria Cross for
“valler.”
And, after the unhappy episode of “The
Stror ’At Coot,” Doreen sings to her Senti-
mental Bloke, “beefin’ it out real good,” and
Bill’s heart melts. “You’ll ’ave to meet my
Mar, some day,” she says. And so he sees
it through. It was harder than he expected.
Mar called him “Willie”! Then the day
draws near. He is “hitched.”
“_4n'— wilt — yeh — take — this — woman — fer —
to be — „ ,
Yer — iceddid — wife?’’ ... .Oh, strike me!
Will I wot r
Take ’ert Doreenf 'E stan’s there arstm
me!
As if ’e thort per’aps Id rather not!
Take 'ert 'E seemed to think 'er kind was got
Like cigarette-cards, fer the arstin'.
Doreen, we feel, deserved her Bloke. For
when Bill stayed out all night,
< She never magged; she never said no word;
But sat an’ looked at me an’ never stirred —
I That 'urt look in 'er eyes, like some scared
bird
Sullenly Bill asks: “ ’Ow is a man to
gnard agen that look?” Wise Doreen brings
him no reproaches: she brings him beef tea!
Doreen will keep her Bloke.
Then comes the dramatic change in Bill’s
fortunes. He goes on the land. And then
“The Kid” arrives. When the nurse tells
Bill,
I drops into a chair, an’ jist see “ ’Ell!”
It teas a pray’r. I feels bofe crook an’
glad ....
An’ that’s the strength of bein’ made a dad.
But there ia more in it than that, as Bill
discovers later, when the kid gets a little
pain inside:—
I’m sent to talk tweet muffin' g to the fowls;
While nurse turns 'and-springt ew'ry time ’#
'owls.
So we leave the Sentimental Bloke, a success-
ful fruit-farmer, settled for life. a cheer-
ful but commonplace end for Bill. He still
remains sentimental; but in his last out-
pouring, “The Mooch o’ Life,” he becomes
almost sententious. Doubtless prosperity is
dangerous to poetry ipmd Bill must be get-
ting older. It is sad to see old Bill
Watchin', in the sundown of yer day,
Yerself again, grown nobler in yer son.
Somehow that doesn’t seem the same Bill;
he gets almost pious, “learnin* to fergive”;
still, he can “take the ’ole mad world as
’arf a joke”; and, after all,
Livin’ an * lovin’ —so life mooches on.
But if the Sentimental Bloke is married
and done for, his cobber remains—Ginger
Mick:—
'E ’awks the bunnies when ’• toils, does Miok.
And Ginger $lick, as readers of recent
issues of this journal know, has gone to the
war. He has all his adventures before him;
he has still to meet his Doreen. And, as
G. J. Dennis knows Ginger Mick as well as
he knows Bill, we shall doubtless welcome
Ginger Mick in another volume. We wish
him luck.
The Songs of a Sentimental Bloke is the
most typically Australian book published for
a decade. Its humor, its sentiment, its genu-
ine humanity, are expressed with feeling and
an assured poetic craftsmanship. C. J.
Dennis is not only an Australian poet: he
is a poet. The only flaws in his work are
his few lapses into the literary: throughout
Dennis’s absorption in the diction of Bill is
admirably sustained. Certainly the diction
and spelling will limit what should bs a
minor world-classic to the one continent
where the Australian language is understood;
but for the over-seas and the polite reader
there is the author’s delightful glossary.
“Bli’ me,” we learn, is “an oath with the
fangs drawn,” “strike” is “the innocuous
remnant of a hardy curse,” and “knock-
down” is “a ceremony insisted upon by ladies
who decline to be ‘picked up’!”
There must be many a Sentimental Bloke
and many a Ginger Mick at Gallipoli to
whom this volume, forwarded with the love
of their respective Doreens, will come
smelling of eucalyptus, or maybe, of Spad-
ger’s Lane. It is the most welcome bit of
Australia that can be exported to the home-
sick heroes at the Dardanelles.
The decorations, by Hal Gye, though a
daintily carried out piece of fantastic
humor, seem hardly necessary. Certainly
the Sentimental Bloke would not under-
stand th'eir meaning or their humor. Den-
nis needs no illustrations to such a delight-
ful piece of humor as “Pilot Cove,” which
almost insists on being quoted in full. In
these three stanzas we see the pilot core
more clearly than any artist could present
him: —
“Young friend,” 'e see....Young friend!
Well, spare me days!
Yeh’d think I t cus 'is own white-’eaded
boy —
The queer ole figure, wiv 'is gentle ways.
“Young friend,” 'e see, “/ wish't yeh bofe
great joy.”
The langwige that them parson blokes im-
ploy
Fair tickles me. The way 'e bleats *n’
brays!
“Young friend," 'e sez....
“Young friend,” 'e sez....A queer ole pilot
bloke,
Wiv silver 'air. The gentle way 'e dealt
Wit? 'er, the soft an’ kindly way 'e spoke
To my Doreen, 'ud make a statcher melt.
I tell yeh, square an’ all, I sorter felt
A kiddish kind o’ feelin’ like I’d choke....
“Young friend,” 'e sez
“Young friend,” ’e sez —on’ then me 'and *s
grips—
“I wish't yeh luclc, you an' yer lady fair.
Sweet maid.” An’ sofly wiv 'is finger-tips,
’E takes an’ strokes me diner's shinin’ ’air.
An’ when I seen 'er standin' blushin ’ there,
I turns an' kisses 'er, fair on the lips.
“Young friend!” ’e sez.

“Masetto” objects to the musicalities of
Louis Stone’s Betty Wayside : “We are told
that the hero, the happy possessor of a
genuine ‘Duke’ —drops his beloved fiddle and
ia struck with horror at the idea of having
to have the back removed to allow of the
sound-post being righted again. Any ama-
teur knows a sound-post can be set quite
easily with an appliance for the purpose.
Then the heroine Betty, who plays divinely
on the piano, appears at a concert to per-
form a Beethoven concerto, and, after a
spasm of nervousness, ‘becomes. unconscious
of the audience, being intent on the need of
keeping in touch with the conductor’s beat.’
Did anyone ever see a solo performer play-
ing to the beat of a conductor? The execu-
tant always sets the tempo, and the con-
ductor indicate* for the purpose of keeping
the accompanying orchestra together. Then
the story informs the reader that the fair
pla/er does well, ‘and the orchestra
applauded in the traditional manner by tap-
ping their instruments with their bows.’
String-players don’t testify their apprecia-
tion that way. It is the music stand they
bit. Fancy striking the neck or scroll (the
least vulnerable part) of a violin with the
bow!”
A New Australian Novelist
This page apologises to Charles Garbage,
the adjudicator for Australia of Hodder and
Stoughton’s recent prize novel competition.
The Pioneers , by Katherine Susannah Prich-
ard ( per Dymock’s Library), is a splendid
Australian novel. It does not even remotely
resemble Garbage. This is the story of Aus-
tralia’s past, of the rough days of the early
settlement in Victoria,, of the birth-stain and
its removal. It.is F. McCubbin’s painting of
“The Pioneers,” in Victorian National Gal-
lery, set to fiction.
In the beginnings of free immigration to
Australia Donald Cameron and his young
wife take up a selection outback in Victoria.
Cameron is a stern, dour man, who prospers
by hard work, and ultimately finds himself
the richest man in the district—he who, in
Scotland, had labored just as hard “without
so much as a plot of sour earth as big as
y’r handkerchief to call my own.” Left
alone soon after her arrival on the selection,
his wife had befriended two convicts escaped
from the Island (Van Diemen’s Land) ; and
this act of sympathy influences the lives of
the Camerons and of the convicts and their
descendants. Stern, rough days those first
days were, with adventure and villainy and
heroism in plenty. Thad McNab followed the
simple profession of blackmailing. He it was
who received escaped convicts and helped
them to their freedom, to reap his reward,
when they had become prosperous, by taking
toll of their riches under threat of the ex-
posure of their past to the police. This prac-
tice of villainy he occasionally varied by
pretending to shelter the convicts and then
denouncing them to the police for the rewards
offered. The consequence was that McNab,
though hated, was feared by the whole of
the inhabitants of Wirreeford, for he had it
in his power at any moment to have them
transported back to Port Arthur.
The feud between the dour Donald
Cameron and the cunning Thad McNab is
the motive of an interesting and well-
worked-out plot. Thad gets hold of young
David Cameron, smarting under his father’s
harsh treatment of him, and the boy runs
away from home and gets mixed up in cattle-
duffing. And at the end Thad McNab gets
his revenge, and though David and the girl
he loves ultimately escape-his clutches, the
•nd for several of the characters is deepest
tragedy.
Of Dierdte, the daughter of one of the
escaped convicts, a lovable and gentle man,
all that need be said is that she is a courageous
and delightful heroine. But the story is no
mere love-story. It is a broad envisagement
of a fine and sturdy epoch of our early days.
The character drawing throughout is genuine
and honest. These people live. And the
Australian environment is capably but unob-
trusively indicated, treated -as a background
for the passions and bravery of the charac-
ters, without ever encroaching upon the
action, and without purple passages.
The author’s style is sincere and workman-
like. She tells her story straightly; and she
just sufficiently indicates the necessary
psychology of her varied characters in what
is, after all, a novel of incident. But there
Is no piling up of improbabilities; her plot
is firmly based and its development un-
hurried. Though no work of genius, The
Pioneers is a genuine piece of our early his-
tory, with its moral in the person of the
young lad who is the son of David and
Dierdre. Mrs. Cameron had already had a
vision of that fulfilment of her* son’s love for
Dierdre:—■
“They had set tt seed in the country that
would bear fruit in the union of the two. All
the labor of their pioneering had not been
in vain. Donald Cameron had done what he
set out to do, though his last days had been
darkened with disappointment, the bitter
sense of disgrace and the futility of all the
long years of toll. But his name would go
on, she realised, and his children’s children
would talk with pride of their grandfather
who had come from the old country, a poor
man, and had made a great name for himself
in' the new land....
“ ‘O God,’ she whispered breathlessly, ‘we
broke the earth, we sowed the seed. Let
theirs be the harvest —the joy of life and the
fullness thereof.’ ”
And when the young lad’s time comes to
carry on the work, his grandmother tells
him: “You ought to be a great man, Dan,
because four nations have gone to the mak-
ing of you. Your four grandparents were
English, Irish, Scottish and Welsh. They
have quarrelled and fought among them-
selves, but you are a gathering of them in
a new country. There will be a great future
for the nation that comes of you and the
boys and girls like you. It will be a nation
of pioneers, with all the adventurous, toil-
ing strain of the men and women who came
over the sea and conquered the wilderness.
You belong to the hunted, too, and suffering
has taught you They may talk about
your birth-stain by and bye, but that will
not trouble you, because it was not this
country made the stain. This country has
been the redeemer and blotted out all those
old stains.”
William J. Locke
William J. Locke repeats his popular
prescription in Jaffery (Lane, per Dy mock’s
Library). -What sort of a book is Jaffery?
“It is a modern high romance of love and
pity, of tears iridescent with laughter, of
strong and beautiful erring souls; it is at
once poignant and tender; it vibrates with
drama; it is instinct with calm and kindly
wisdom.” No; it is not that kind of book;
that is the book that one of the characters in
Jaffery writes, and naturally it is univer-
sally hailed as a work of- genius, and, in
consequence, becomes a best-seller. So often
a work of genius immediately becomes a
best-seller, doesn’t it? According to William
J. Locke, the public has the critical faculty
of instantly spotting works of genius; though
in his own case, and that of writers like
Hardy, Henry James and Meredith, despite
the perspicacity of the critics, it took the
public some time before it was aware of their
genius.
Is Jaffery this sort of book? “This vivid,
virile opening!.... The people are lustily
drawn. They have a flavor of the sea and
the breeziness of wide spaces; a deep-lunged
folk From the first it goes with a rare
swing, incident following incident, every trait
of character presented objectively in fine
scorn of analysis. There are little pen-
pictures of grim scenes faultless in their
definition and restraint. There is a girl in
it... .etc.” No; it is not that sort of book.
That is another book that another character
in Jaffery writes—also immediately popular,
also an obvious work of genius. Two master-
pieces in literature flung at us in one novel
by prodigal William J. Locke!
No, made confident by his assured popu-
larity, this clever writer gives us not one
Beloved Vagabond, but two—and marries
them. The absurd improbabilities of his
plot, involving murders, the theft of the
manuscript of a literary masterpiece, won-
derful adventures on board a tramp steamer,
and a fraud worked upon the wife of an
author that could only be possible were she
an imbecile, are handed out to his readers
with the assurance of a novelist who knows
just how much his public will accept. There
is, for instance, one scene in the hold of a
tramp steamer where in a gale the cargo has
shifted, and unless it is made secure the
vessel will be lost. Of course, the two
“Beloved Vagabonds” have shipped on this
tramp, the male as a deck-hand, though he
is a rich war-correspondent, and the female
as cook’s assistant. Of course, they aren’t
married yet—that would be too dull. And
naturally when.the male “Beloved Vaga-
bond” is ordered with the other hands to
descend into that awful hold of slithering
cargo—“twenty men in semi-darkness, scarce
able to stand, fighting blind, mad forces of
half a ton each” —the female Vagabond
descends, too —into that “helpless scuttering
surge of men and vast wooden cubes.”
“Most of the men had torn off their upper
garments and fought half-naked.... Soon the
heat became unbearable, and I, too, tore off
jersey and shirt. Liosha (the female Vaga-
bond) joined me, and we worked together
without speaking. (She was, of course,
dressed as a deck-hand.) Her long, thick
hair had come down, and she had hastily
tied it in a knot, just as you might tie a
knot in a towel, and she had thrown
off things like everybody else, and only a
flimsy cotton, sleeveless bodice, or whatever
it’s called, drenched through and sticking to
her, made a pretence of covering her from
her waist.”
Those are the delicate details that William
J. Locke insists upon when he is dealing
with titanic forces. Jack London could have
made his readers see such a scene. Con-
rad would have made his readers feel it.
Locke merely utilises it as a background for
his readers’ sentimental interest.
A delightful and fantastic tale, too agree-
ably written for criticism. Each character
is merely labelled by a few crude traits; and
the psychology of the two heroines is help-
lessly vague. Doria is either an idiot or a
nice girl; and Liosha’s love-affairs are frankly
incredible. But as long as Locke provides
us with plenty of excitement, tinged with
sufficient sentiment, and can continue to
furbish up new variants of The Beloved
Vagabond, Locke’s enormous public will not
mind. And, after all, Locke writes delight-
fully ; there is little that is slovenly in his
style. But, this novel is no “modern high
romance of love and pity”; there is no “trait
of character presented objectively in fine
scorn of analysis”; there are no “little pen-
pictures of grim scenes faultless in their
definition and restraint,”
“The object of this volume,” says W.
Gordon Henderson, in his foreword to
Foiled / The Enemy in our own Land
(Australasian News Oo. O.A. Is.), “is to
convey to the average citizen, in narrative
form, some Idea of the system on which the
Kaiser’s spies work.” And he asserts that
to-day the German Secret Service System
is as well organised as ever it was in Aus-
tralia. If this be so, then W. Gordon Hen-
derson has chosen the wrong way to warn
us of our grave danger. His palpably sen-
sational detective-yarn of a noble Sydney
detective foiling the German villain again
and again, with all the devilish ingenuity of
The Man Who Stayed at Home, at once
discredits the seriousness of his foreword.
Detective stories of this class are hardly
likely to “put us on our guard against the
Secret Service agent” in our midst.

Self-Loading Shotgun, 12 Gauge
The Winchester Model 1911 Self-Loader is a wild-fowl gun par excellence. The
recoil ejects the fired shell, feeds a loaded shell from the magazine into the
chamber, cocks the hammer and leaves the gun ready to be fired again. In wild-
fowl shooting, the game often comes fast and when least expected. Shoot a
Winchester Self-Loader and you can always be prepared for a series of shots; for
the loading and firing of this gun are controlled by the trigger finger. As the re-
coil of this gun is used to operate the reloading mechanism, the “kick” of heavy
loads is minimized, making the gun very pleasant to shoot. It is a hammerless,
two-part, take-down, five-shot repeater, that works well with all standard loads.
The working parts being made of Nickel steel, make it strong and safe.
Ask your dealer to show you the Winchester that’s

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Serenade.
Could I that sickle moon but grasp
I’d reap the glories of the skies,
And planets pour into thy lap
To match the splendor ;of thine eyes,
The splendor of thine eyes.
With star-dust from the Nebulae
.And Pleiad gems I'd deck thy hair,
And gossamer from the Milky Way
Thy gracious lily limbs would wear,
Thy lily limbs would wear.
Far Saturn would provide the ring,
Orion would thy girdle be,
The Southern Cross thy scapula
To keep thee true to me, to me,
To keep thee true to me !
QUEENSLAND.
JAMES A. PHILP.
The Lovers.
She jested at their youth ;
And, looking slily,
I saw the boy and girl
Love-making shyly.
The passing ferries went
In lighted splendor;
The sundered waters sighed
In whispers tender.
Soft was the wind’s caress ;
The engines, throbbing,
Blended in harmony
With music sobbing.
Absurdly coy was she,
And he ungainly.
We from our snug retreat
Could see them plainly.
Yet flashed within their eyes,
Like dawn’s bright breaking,
That great, surprising light
Of love awaking.
She jested at their youth....
But I, in sorrow,
Wished for a moment I
Their youth might borrow,
That I, .with heart uncloyed,
Could feel the tingle
Of warm blood beating high
When fingers mingle ;
Know the sweet frenzy of
Those first embraces,
The new-born ecstasy
That earth displaces;
Seeing no perjury
In red lips pouting;
Take love at simple worth.
Truth past all doubting!
She jested... .and I laughed,
Jesting at sorrow.
Would, for an hour of love,
Youth I could borrow!
N.S.W.
HAMER.
Happy Valley.
I know a lovely valley that is open to the
South—
Lirl, come with me! Oh come with me!
, there’s magic in the place!
The City has its fingers on the corners of
your mouth;
And the mantle of the City makes a shadow
on your face!
The road down Happy Valley is like a
woman’s scarf;
It is pink within the hollow and pink upon
the rise!
Girl, come away ! Oh, come away ! —I want
to hear you laugh,
And I want to see the sunlight iu the won-
der of your eyes.
The air of Happy Valley holds a hundred
little tones :
The wind is off the ocean and there’s music
in its note,
And rinkle! rinkle! rinkle! runs the river
o’er the stones;
And I want to hear an echo from the glorv
of your throat.
The hills round Happy Valley are all dotted
o er with cows ;
And there are sheep upon them, here and
there a browsing horse;
And I want to see the fingers of the wind
upon your blouse,
And I want to hear the rustle of your skirt
amid the gorse.
The sky o’er Happy Valley is so very blue
and clear!
A lark is high within it, just a little trilling
speck!
And I want to take the melody and place it
to your ear,
And I want to see a sunbeam put an arm
about your neck.
The hunger of the City and the terror of the
noise !
Girl, come away! Oh. come away, and tread
the road with me,
The road down Happy Valley that goes
Winding ’mid its joys,
And we’ll follow and we’ll follow till we
find the open sea !
MAORILANI).
BOYCE BOWDEN.
The Owl.
A mouldy owl sat in his nest that stank
Of bones and scraps of carrion rotting rank
And croaked: “He’s damned! He’s ouito
beyond the pale,
That godless, outcast bird, the Nightingale !”
N.S.W.
JOS. Q. H.

NO HURRY, OF COURSE.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
/•’or £ I (lx. 6(1. per annum you can have The
Bulletin sent regularly to you every week for a

Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects,
and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say,
3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or A ’ewspaper- Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood .that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, OCTOBER 14, 1915.
PATRIOTIC FUNDS AND THE LITTLE
AUSTRALIAN.
Twice daily a strong man staggers into The
Bulletin office beneath the weight of corres-
pondence urging an editor, distracted by
poets and a printer, to write at white heat
about (1) the necessity for the prompt
expenditure of all patriotic, relief and other
funds raised for the benefit of wounded and
sick soldiers and the dependents of those
who have been killed; and (2) the immedi-
ate adoption of 765 schemes for the special
benefit of the children of the fallen. And
when The Bulletin reflects on the possi-
bility of the number of men who have yet to
be killed and be wounded, and thinks of the
changes that often take place in the public
mind, and contemplates the probabilities
associated with national and private cash-
boxes, it finds itself with no very great
enthusiasm for any system of spending these
funds in which prudence is not given reason-
able weight. Certainly it is desirable to
have every legitimate call made upon the
funds promptly met; but it is equally unde-
sirable, in view of the special circumstances
and the uncertain liability, to regard the
funds as things to be got rid of as speedily
as possible. The progress of the war and
the frequent casualty list and the despatch of
more men to the Front suggest indeed that
it would not be inappropriate if the pumping
of money into these funds was considered as
of equal importance to the pumping of money
out. Therefore unless there is reasonable
evidence that the funds are not being dis-
tributed with a fair hand, without parsi-
mony, among those individuals for whose
succor they were established, The Bulletin
is not prepared to throw more than half a
dozen stones about, and it doesn’t think any-
body else ought to. There are two things in
particular tc recollect —that the war is still
in full blast, and that these funds differ
materially from those raised for the relief
of sufferers by a flood or a mine disaster or
a shipwreck. When floods subside or a
mine caves in or a steamer is collected by D.
Jones, the extent of the disaster and the
needs of the sufferers can be determined in
a day or two - . But if mines were blowing
up every day and floods took place seven
times a week, and the sea was snatching
ships away every evening, the situation
would be complicated, and that is just what
is happening now.
* * * *
So far as the children of the fallen are
concerned this paper has no doubt that one
of the most earnest wishes of the Austra-
lian people is that adequate provision shall
be made by the nation for bringing up the
fatherless youngsters. In a very special
sense they are the care of the Common-
wealth. But all children are the care of
the Commonwealth; and it seems to The
Bulletin that the multitudinous schemes it
has received, suggesting that the children of
dead soldiers should be regarded as of a
class apart, and given special privileges and
opportunities and medals and all that sort of
thing, represent a .waste of time. Selected
at random here is a scheme seriously ad-
vanced for adoption by the State: —
1. That the child of every fallen or wounded sol-
dier shall he given a certificate or medal, which shall
he held in trust by the schoolmaster. (It is explained
that exhibition of these things would have a fine
moral effect on all children.)
2. That the school teachers shall he directed to pay
special attention to the education of these youngsters
as a compensation for their loss.
3. That after leaving school the youngsters able to
produce medals or certificates shall have preference
of employment.
Such schemes are hysterical. To distin-
guish in public policy between the children
of a soldier who has fallen and of one who
has come back from the wars to hang up
his gun and get work would be ludicrous.
Treatment of the child of either of these
people differently from the youngster of the
citizen who had been prevented from getting
into khaki because of his flat feet or his
false tooth would be to clothe a Biblical
threat with unnecessary realism. And, any-
how, the children of Mrs. McWringer,
washerwoman, whose husband went West
in an atmosphere of mystery, have done no
harm. So, in lieu of playing about with
anaemic schemes of primogeniture in disguise,
suppose,the alternative be adapted of look-
ing on, the little Australian in the mass and
doing the best Australia is capable of, so
that he may have a fair chance? To The
Bulletin this seems the more enlightened
method; and by way of a start it moves
that the be adopted:—
That -the people of this Commonwealth direct
their multitudinous Parliaments to, —
(a) Knock off pawning the kid.
(b) Cut out of the last will and testament of the
nation that part which bequeaths the kid great
hunks of debt and p.n.
(c) Rescue the kid from the frequent slum, where
he is rendered physically and morally unfit.
( d ) Make the soil of Australia available to the
youth of Australia, so that the sons of soldiers,
wowsers, millionaires and politicians who charge a
fee for making patriotic speeches may have an equal
chance in something, at any rate.
(e) Turn down the foreign import and train the
Australian kid to manufacture the things his silly
old father has been dragging out of ships.
and pledge themselves to fire any or every Parlia-
ment that balks at the job.
* * * *
The conspicuous merit about this is that
it indicates a series of steps which can be
taken at once without preliminary riot or
25 years’ wrangling by caucuses, Lib. or
Lab.; it represents a practical method of
doing the little Australian a good turn ; and
it shows a means by which the awful crowd
of parasites who are now loafing on the
youngster can be exterminated. Unless Aus-
tralia gets to work at this business of giving
the kid a fair deal, it looks as if one of the
first serious undertakings of the rising gen-
eration will be to erect a large monument on
which its opinion of the Old Man will be
chiselled in good, plain Australian. And
The Yell, which circulates morning and
evening editions in Gehenna, will contain a
full account of the proceedings, so that the
Old Man may be made aware of what his
children really think of him.
THE DELICATE ART OF LYING.
Those mighty lovers of truth, the Greeks,
who could not stamp the image of a monarch
on a coin without showing in relief the wart
upon his majestic nose, held that lying was
a fine art. Old Homer placed Ulysses,
the prince of deceivers, on a higher pedestal
even than Achilles, the tremendous fighting
leader of the Hellenic allies. The man who
could lie through to victory was a hero equally
with the man who by sheer strength and cour-
age could cut his way through with a sword.
The explanation is probably concealed in
the fact that the Greeks as a nation were
from earliest antiquity surrounded by power-
ful peoples infinitely lower in culture and
civilisation than themselves, and against
whom they could only maintain themselves
by long-sustained trickery and deceit. The
Greek sword was sharp, but the Greek hands
were few, and while the sword was never
found wanting at utmost need, the well-
framed lie was the shield and buckler of all
that was best and highest in the world.
Something of the same kind was true of
every rising race and people, and as a result
the modern world fought its way by slow
stages to light and truth, clinging to its faith
in lies with a passion of apology which is
the outstanding paradox of progress. Diplo-
macy, the art of international lying, is con-
doned and secretly admired, and the lesser
diplomacy of protective falsehood has been
allowed to find its way into every corner of
political, commercial and social life. In
the armory of truth the lie-is the readiest
weapon—not the strongest, not the surest,
but the lightest and most easily handled.
* * * *
In looking at the great world of nations
we are guided by colored maps and the
cabled remarks of those who hold the reins
of office, but we know nothing surely. The
very men who themselves handle the fine-
edged falsehoods most readily are so often
deceived that clever guesswork as to the
motives behind actions and >a sub-
conscious instinct for the likely thing are the
best that even keenest statesmen can achieve.
When the great Napoleon had run the full
gamut of diplomatic trickery and -was pre-
paring for his last campaign, his brilliant
brain evolved an idea so novel that its suc-
cess seemed certain. lie told the absolute
truth about his plans. One cold, strange
man in England who had watched him for
years with narrow eyes concentrated on a
single purpose literally believed him. The
result was Waterloo. Wellington’s amaz-
ing guess that truth was truth saved the
kings of Europe and made Freetrade, the
Kaiser and a number of other things pos-
sible. Napoleon’s one attempt to deceive
by plain dealing had failed; how could an
attempt to undeceive by similar means
succeed? Seasoned statesmen like Beacons-
field, Gladstone and Salisbury told the
truth hesitatingly, as if speaking lies, and
spoke the delicately-framed falsehood as if a
high sense of honor and responsibility had
forced them to get down to the bedrock of
plain fact. And so the game goes on ; and
Europe, clinging pathetically to its ancient
traditions, grants itself absolution for its
falsehoods that it may proceed to complicate
them with still more subtle prevarication.
* * * *
All this is very irritating to foolish per-
sons like ourselves who want to know things
for the mere sake of knowing them; but
diplomacy becomes maddening when it is
employed outside the international sphere and
invades the humbler chambers of our domes-
tic politics. It may be that Gladstone
cannot speak the truth about European
relations to Smith or Jones, lest Bismark
should overhear what he says, or lest Jones
should go and blurt it out to a pal who will
tell the Sultan of Turkey; but there seems
to be no good reason why Fisher and
Holman should hedge themselves with simi-
lar evasions and fortify themselves with pre-
varication and dark sayings when dealing
with plain matters affecting our own back-
yard and rubbish-tip. Yet if in the inter-
national sphere things are involved and diffi-
cult to understand, they are made a
thousandfold, more so in the ordinary realm
of home politics. We can be sure of
nothing. Our blind guess at the polls puts
this man or that in a position of national
trust, and whether he proves worthless or
shines at his job is almost as much a matter
of chance as the result of a horse race.
What every elector feels that he requires for
his guidance is to be present in his astral
body at one Cabinet meeting where, unseen,
he might watch and hear and form a definite
judgment with regard to the Ministers
present. But even if he possessed such
occult powers he would probably find that
the subtlety of diplomacy hides the real
motives and aims of those in office in their
place of council, and that to get at the bed-
rock truth he would require to be with the
knots of twos and threes who compose the
Cabinet in their still more secret private
conferences; and even then he might be
readily enough deceived. The Party Govern-
ment swindle is in large measure responsible
for the secrecy, caution and want of candor
which mark the path of the politician ; but it
is also in some degree a legacy from the past
and a reflection of the cunning obscurantism
which nations employ in their dealings with
others. To the small party politician the
party is the nation and its opponents are the
enemy; and the same forces which prevent
him from ever becoming a true patriot com-
pel the caucus-bound lawmakers to adopt a
secret diplomacy which wearies and disgusts
the public.
* * * *
The intense belief in the danger of speak-
ing the truth on the part of those in
authority has kept us in blindness during the
whole of the present war. We first learned
that Britain had joined in the struggle from
an American source, and since then the
crumbs of information officially thrown to
us have been of the meagre sort calculated
to give an entirely erroneous idea of the
nature of the fighting. Hundreds of times
we have heard of trenches recovered of the
previous loss of which we had no informa-
tion ; again and again we were told that all
was well, when later cables made it only
too clear that all had been ill. English
statesmen have spoken of the tremendous
gravity of the situation and have hinted at
disasters unthinkable at the very time when,
after the report of a series of minor victories
for our arms, we were assured that the
enemy was at his last ditch. The whole
thing has been confusing in the extreme; and
not one-tenth of the deception practised upon
us could be justified on the ground of public
safety. Of course, certain information was
rightly withheld through the fear that in
giving it publicity it might help the plans of
the enemy entrenched at Mosman and St.
Kilda, but the stories of the desperate cir-
cumstances of our troops in the early stages
of the war, of the defeats we suffered and
the reverses we sustained should not have
been withheld. In the main the censorship
has been a grim and foolish farce, calculated
to weaken our fighting arm and damp our
patriotic ardor. The people as a whole have
been treated by the few in authority as if
they were enemies and aliens, and the only
explanation which Can be given of the course
pursued is that it is in line with things done
in the past and in keeping with the devious
processes of diplomacy which statesmen love
and their small hirelings ape.
* * * *
The whole diplomatic idea belongs to an
order of things which is utterly foreign to
Australia. In this country we neither love
lies nor believe in their efficacy. We still
can admire Achilles ; we more and more
detest Ulysses. As for the half-information
about the war, which under the censorship
has become practically false information,
those who have been guilty of deceiving us
have already learned in part their folly.
They will yet come to know still more com-
pletely that they entirely and grossly misun-
derstood the Australian temper and character,
and that under the stimulus of the open
knowledge of defeat we should have done
more and done it more readily. It may be
our misfortune —and perhaps our sunshine is
responsible—but we refuse to fumble in
darkness while there is light available.
Devoted as we are to many pastimes, the
European game of Blindman’s Buff is not for
us. We refuse to have bandages over our
eyes, and the bandage-makers great and small
who take their cues from overseas will be
wise if they learn it in time. In a word,
Australia is about full up of lying, and a
number of people who practise it are going
to get kicked.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Need for a Spring Cleaning.
It would be as well for Commonwealth Minis-
ters, even if only for the sake of their own
political hides, to realise that an enormous
number of people in Australia are getting
mighty restless. The military machine is
creaking horribly. It has been running for 15
months now, and should by this time be
going without friction. Pearce, who is
standing behind the starting lever, professes
to be “greatly pleased” with things. If
this accurately represents the state of mind
he is in, the only possible commentary is
that he is wearing the wrong kind of spec-
tacles and is handicapped by not seeing the
things which come within the vision of the
average man. It would have been absurd
to expect an absence of muddle in this busi-
ness. But if things keep on as they are at
present, and unless the Government is able
to enforce a very marked improvement in the
details of administration, it will find the task
of convincing the electors that it is competent
to discharge the extended authorities asked
for by the Referenda, much more difficult
than would be the case in other Circum-
stances. It is no use being blind in that
eye; and this paper tells the Government
without hesitation that it has embarked upon
the campaign heavily burdened by the amaz-
ing ineptitude of military officialdom, con-
doned with sedulous persistence by Minis-
ters. The very dogs in the street are barking
about it. Even in such a simple matter as
the transport and landing of the wounded,
the Department still seems unable to bestir
itself to intelligent action. Only the other
day a contingent of wounded Sydney men
arrived in Port Jackson to find they could
not be put ashore because some official had
not been told they were coming, or some
individual was unable to arrange for a taxi-
cab. And so it happened that while
hundreds of the relatives and friends of these
men were waiting for them, they were kept
out in the harbor aboard the hospital ship
until next day, when the Department found
itself able to move. Then there has been
the case of wounded men coming back in a
hospital ship with dozens of empty cabins
and the men obliged to sleep in any old place.
And the medical inspection has been all up-
side down, and mobilisation orders have
been issued at random, and chaos rules in the
system of paying wives their allowances, and
so on and so forth, all irrespective of the
atrocious things that have taken place and
still are taking place in connection with the
camps. As for the financial side, everyone
who has a nose to smell by is firmly holding,
his breath. The public is very patient, up
to a point, and this paper is loth to use-
hard words at this '&tftge of the proceedings;
but the Ministry will be acting wisely if it
bends its collective back to the job of tidying
things up, for the certain alternative is that
something will go off bang.

The Beginning and Middle and End of It.
All this frantic argument as to whether
compulsory service for defence is Socialistic
in principle or anti-Sosh in intention, or is
connected by marriage or otherwise with
any particular formula in politics, is weari-
some. When a citizen is awakened in the
middle of the night in a burning house and
has the choice between being cremated and
reaching the street in ssec., the business on
hand demands that he shall ignore the
Police Offences Act and reach that street,
whether his pyjamas make the trip or not.
In the same spirit it would be a blessed
relief if these agitated controversialists,
would keep to the crux of the whole thing,
which is whether a nation can defend itself
most effectively, and with the greatest
economy of life and misery, when its man-
hood is ready for the job and appears with
its sleeves rolled up when the whistle-
blows, or, as an alternative, can it do lust
as well when a few men are ready and the-
rest are holding meetings to consider whether-
they ought to go round and see the shift;
boss? The problem hinges on the one point:

Preference to Partisans.
Andrew Fisher decorated the party joss
with a tasteful garland of straw the other
day. He was fobbing off a Sydney lntu
viewer who wanted to know something about
the High Goinmissionership :
A Labor Government is in power and we con-
sider it, right that a man representing the 1 uhlig
party should hold the position.
In other words, political partisanship is the
proper test of fitness for a post of high
responsibility in the service of the Mate.
Two excuses may be offered for his theory.
First the very raw one that a Government
ought to reward political services to the
party. This opens np beautiful possibili-
ties ‘ If it is right for the Government to
reward the political partisan, it must be also
right for the political partisan to give re-
wards for the same virtues. In other words,
the High Commissioner, being rewarded tor
being a Laborite, will be also doing the right
thing in rewarding those with whom he
comes in contact for being Labontes. in
the second place, the Government may otter
the excuse that a Labor man natiuaily
knows best how to interpret the wishes ot a
Labor Government. In that case, ot
course, the Labor Government will not do
its successor the injustice of appointing the
High Commissioner for any longer period
than it retains office itself. If it thinks it a
proper thing that Labor in office here should
be represented by Labor in office in London,
then it must concede that the Liberals have
an equal right to appoint a Liberal partisan
to represent them when they get back, as
they may some day. So we shall be unding
the Commissioner concerned not about hold-
ing Australia’s end up in London, but about
keeping his party in power and himself m
office. A more improper proceeding than
that cannot be conceived. What Australia
needs is the best man for the yob. It is
no more necessary that he should be of one
special brand of politics than that he should
be a Particular Baptist or a vegetarian. It
it is necessary that he should be a political
partisan, then it is equally necessary that
the holders of a thousand other offices in the
State should be partisans; and that would
introduce a system of corruption and a mass
of turmoil which even Andrew Fisher might
_not care to be responsible for. If Fisher
wants the job in London, well and good. But
for Heaven’s sake let him avoid the hypoensy
of pretending that he takes it in pursuance
of a political principle.

The Follies We Have Fed On.
The position of the Empire in regard to
national service, regulation of private profits
and many other important war-factors shows
how hard it is for a nation brought up m
the belief that it is inherently superior to
any other,- and in careless ind:" erence to
the ideals and customs of the rest of the
world, to reform itself in a hurry. British
“liberty to do as one likes,” British freedom
as compared with “Continental slavery,” the
ability of one Britisher to whip. four for-
eigners, with ancient silly old sayings about
one volunteer being equal to 10 pressed men,
and the quoting of England’s great wealth
as a proof that in everything she is supreme
—continual harping on these and kindred
strings has evolved dangerous characteristics
in the average Britisher. It is only fair to
say that in this he has been encouraged by
the bulk of those capable of swaying thought.
Politicians, the press, novelists, magazine
writers, educated people for the most part
have done their best to foster the concep-
tion. No better method of blocking the
acceptance of any scheme to better the status
of the nation or the individual could be found
than to label it “Continental.” Despite the
wonderful results achieved by German
“socialistic” legislation in peace as well as
in war, the Britisher has consistently sneered
at such things and refused to see the neces-
sity for a large nation in a small land having
more regulation than a few stray black*-
fellows in a continent. When the war
broke out, the average Britisher, still tilled
with this sense of superiority, naturally sat
back and waited to hear of the downfall 1 f
the big German army when attacked by the
small British Expeditionary Force. Since
then, little by little, and in spite of strenu-
ous endeavors on the part of the authorities,
the truth has been slowly dawning, and it
is now realised by a large number of badly-
disillusioned people that, after all, when it
comes -to war we are not magically pre-
eminent. Brave our men are; but their
opponents are also brave. Ours hang like
glue to their positions; but so do their
opponents. In every way our men sacrifice
themselves for their country and their man-
hood’s pride; but so does the enemy. The
enemy couldn’t stand the bayonet, it was
printed, and ran from it every time; but so
far his running hasn’t moved bis lines
much backwards. The poor old Turk—
what was he to stand in the road of the
Allies? But he still stands there. The
big warship meets the little warship, and the
little warship sinks, whether she flies the
Union Jack or the German emblem. And
so ok Irresistibly the painful truth has
been borne home, that to win we must not
trust to ideas of an illusory superiority, but
that victory depends on men and guns and
munitions and organisation and science and
self-sacrifice. And now the primary trouble
is to make a people, taught for years that
disorganisation and haphazardness and indi-
vidualism constitute the higher policy, behove
in a few minutes that organisation and thor-
oughness and Socialism are necessary and
right. ' Can it be’ done? And if not, what
will the harvest be? These are the questions
of the hour, and the hour is perilously late.
The Soldier and a Scheme to Get Some
Unearned Increment.
Thebe are indications of a sudden anxiety to
embark on schemes of land purchase for the
benefit, so it is said, of the men who carried
a gun. That land settlement should be pro-
moted cannot be questioned; but that it
should be done upon the lines proposed is to
confess the failure of past land policy. At
present events go something like this: Cer-
tain persons become possessed of the earth;
the State borrows and spends millions on
the construction of railways and roads and
bridges, thus giving improved, access to vari-
ous parts of the aforesaid earth; the
improved access gives to the earth there-
abouts an added value. Land that was
worth a pound or two per acre is quadrupled
in value by the State’s loan expenditure.
Then the State, coining to the conclusion that
the number of owners of earth should be
increased, rushes in and buys pieces of earth
from the persons seated thereon, paying
them heavy compensation. Thus the public
borrows £5 to improve the value of Smith’s
land. Having improved the value by £5,
it next buys Smith out, borrowing another
£5 to do it. It now attempts to sell the land
to Jones, who has to pay the £5 which the
State paid Smith, and also the interest on
the £5 the State borrowed originally, plus
various charges and etceteras. Every trans-
action of this kind makes the next one
more difficult, increases the drain of interest,
and in nine cases out of ten creates a class
of people who, finding it impossible to pull
through, have to be spoon-fed by the general
taxpayer; and he frequently dips deeper into
the loan bowl for the purpose. Of course,
besides being all grossly unfair, it can lead
to nowhere but disaster. If the argument is
that the soldier deserves a farm, the answer
is that there is- no special reason why he
deserves a farm any more than a suburban
grocery or a tanyard. Let him have his
reward, -by all means; but in dealing with
the lands' of Australia let us not hang, a
heavy debt round his neck, and a heavier
debt round the necks of future generations,
while leaving them both to struggle with the
real land problem —the problem, of unearned
increment —which their fathers industriously
shirked.
The Prevailing Dust Storm Behind the
Trenches.
Opponents of compulsory service against
armed aggression were busy all last month
explaining (1) that they were opposed to
compulsion; (2) that they were not opposed
to it if there was also compulsory conscrip-
tion of wealth. This means, if it means
anything, that they are only opposed to
compulsion when there is not enough ot it.
Provided they have compulsion in large
hunks their dislike of the same. thing in rela-
tively small quantities will disappear. All
of which is about as reasonable an attitude
to take up as that of the citizen who objected
to public authority compelling him to connect
his drain with a sewerage system before the
brotherhood of man had arrived. I he
problem that is up for consideration now is
not the readjustment of the whole social and
economic system, but the ways and means
with the material on hand, and the processes
that can be immediately invoked, of throwing
off an enemy who is clutching at the national
windpipe. Men are being killed in thousands,
the world filled with woe and misery without
parallel, the very existence of nations threat-
ened by a tiger which sprang upon them in
the dark. To let the brute claw and tear
eivili-ation to pieces while the people he
wants to swallow are debating whether they
will agree to socialisation of national
resources argues a preference for disputation
over life. - As the phrase is being used now
in the middle of terrible conflict in which
the only thing that counts is brute force,
“conscription of wealth” is a mere, catch-
cry, and the people who are using it most
frequently in Australia know that it is so.
For all practical purposes conscription of
wealth is with us. Any day the Govern-
ment can call upon the people of Australia
for any sum it may require. It is even now
calling on them for as much as 6s. m the ±
of their income. It is demanding of them up
to about one-third of the wealth of which
they die possessed. Its powers in this direc-
tion are illimitable. But to wrangle about
whether this is so or not—to engage in
heated debate about whether the fetate
should or could adopt a particular system ot
finance, and how it is all going to be done
and all the rest of it—and to do all this
while an international burglar and garrotter
is struggling to settle the proposition with, a
Jack Johnson, is merely an evasion of social
responsibility. Social theories can be experi-
mented with, submitted to tests, amended in
the light of experience. But when a homi-
cide comes along with a bayonet the theorist
can only conclude that the first thing to do
is to sit on the maniac’s head ; for unless the
theorist gets there first he won’t have any
theories left to trouble about afterwards.
The Occasional Wastefulness of
“Economy.”
The argument used to be that John Bull
could not afford to adopt a scheme
oc universal military training; that the
cost, a few millions a year, would
lead to beggary; that if the nation
sanctioned any such departure from
British precedent it would certainly become
involved in war through a Foreign Minister,
made truculent by the strength behind him,
speaking disrespectfully to another foreign
Minister or a Crowned'Head. Therefore, ran
the conclusion from the argument, considera-
tions of economy, and the preservation of
peace called for nothing more expensive or
provocative than an army strong enough to
repel any enemy who might suddenly descend
on Muddleton-by-.the-Mud. It was a beautiful
theory with three large holes in it: (1) that
a war forced upon a nation with an army
inadequate for immediate action would lead
to colossal expenditure; (2) that the absence
of ail adequate army was a direct incentn
to a possible enemy to unifee war; ami v>J
that Muddleton-in-the-Mud need not neces-
sarily be attacked to cause national trouble.
It was because the German saw there. -was no
armed Britain ready to spring on him unit
he himself sprang at Belgium, who had prac-
tically disarmed herself. It was because
John had been afraid to find a moderate
amount of cash every year that he had no
effective force, and has now, in order to get
an army, to foot a bill of staggering size,
in which one day’s expenditure dissipates a
year’s false economies. The leap in the
spending has been like -this :
1913 .. ~ £197,492,969
1914 . , 660,473,533
1915 '. . . 1,590,000,000
1916 (estimate) 1,825,000,00-0
To these later figures must lie added the
enormous reduction of earnings and an ap-
palling, loss of life,, though we have only just
begun to do what would have been done in
the first days of. war had an effective army
been instantly available. There is little com-
fort in all this for the people who foretold
what must happen ; but the dreadful conse-
quences of sloth and slackness and devotion
to old shibboleths are sharp reminders to the
rest of the world that, though unreadiness
to repel aggression may be cheap enough for
a time, it is a mighty expensive thing in the
end.

The Murders in Armenia.
The Armenian horror, which is only begin-
ning to be known in all its frightfulness,
seems to eclipse everything else when mea-
sured by the actual number of gallons of
blood shed. Eight hundred thousand men,
women and children have, it is said, been
slaughtered since May. While the German,
by backing the Unspeakable and leaving
immense territories entirely at his mercy, is
directly responsible, the real trouble goes
back much further, and England has to take
her share of the burden of shame. Things
happened somewhat in this fashion. When
Russia had smashed the Turks and mopped
up the mess they had made in Bulgaria,
England stepped in and balked the victors
of their reward. The Bear retired growling,
and England propped up the Turk with
financial pillows and told him to be good.
He wasn’t good ; and before many years had
gone he was killing Armenians in bulk with
greater enthusiasm than he had ever killed
Bulgarians. It was then England’s move.
Russia had fought one hard war to suppress
massacres by Turks, and, as England had
interfered, the job of settling the Armenian
difficulty devolved upon her. If at that
time Salisbury had taken the Ottoman
Empire by the slack of its pants and hurled
it out on its ear for good, the feat would
have had the solid applause of all Europe.
But England funked, did nothing, and the
Turk gathered the general no'Ay- that he was
licensed to murder in Asia as long as he kept
his knife out of anything European. how
the Unspeakable has found an ally of like
tastes, who has loaded his gun and sharpened
his axe for him, and he is putting up a great
fight at one end of his dominions while he
is slaughtering whole villages of peaceful
citizens at the other. And until the Turk is
pushed right out of Europe nothing can be
done about it. The U.S.A. have said, or
are going to say, to the Turk that if he
doesn’t stop Woodrow Wilson will be quite
cross with him; but Achmit and Abudul
and Ibrahim and people like that don’t know
Woodrow from a bar of soap, which; consid-
ering the permanent soap famine in the
Turkish bath-tub, is saying a good deal.
Armageddon.
The declaration of the Germans that the
present Balkan mix-up is uue to their deter-
mination to snatch Egypt from our hands
recalls ancient prophecy. Although the state-
ments of the prophet people, as reported to
us, are a trifle vague and hazy, and couched
in language which might mean one thing it
stood on its head and another if looked at
sideways, there seems to be a general con-
sensus of opinion among those who have
examined them from every possible angle
that the Armageddon of the nations, long
foreshadowed, is to be fought somewhere in
the narrow plain between Galilee and the
ancient Samaria, with special trimmings in
the way of great cavalry charges and the
leading of forlorn hopes on the slopes of
Mount Carmel. The real interest of the
thing lies, of course, in the fact that if the
Germans forced their way overland through
Asia Minor it is at this very point that the
Allies might be expected to make their great
stand for the defence of Egypt. Egyptian
Pharaohs in ancient times again and again
brought their hosts to this point to defend
their land from northern attacks. Like wise
strategists they fought the foe as far from
their own borders as possible, and even when
they were forced to retire southward it was
a very tired and dejectdd pursuer who at
last came to the desert frontier of then-
realm. In point of fact, for several thou-
sands of years no invasion of Egyptian terri-
tory was' successful. The German has no
less tough a job in front of him if he intends
to pass that way now ; and as the prophets
have it that in the last great battle good is
to wallop evil to a dead finish, the outlook
is bad for the Kaiser. Before the curtain
rings down, Peace is also to last for a thou-
sand years, which is good hearing for those
who oppose conscription, but no safe basis
on which to make our defence arrangements.
In any case, the prophets were notoriously
weak in geography, and those who interpret
them would be better engaged in growing
potatoes.

Thus George Reid, talking at the London
Guild Hall: —
In Australia there was not an individual black,
white or tan — who did not. swear by Kitchener.
And George wants to represent White Aus-
tralia to the end of the war on a screw of
nothing a week! Observe, first, the order
of the colors—the black first, the mere -white
in the middle, then the tan—the lofty Asiatic
occupying the position of rhetorical em-
phasis at the end of the phrase. What a
cheerful view of our Commonwealth some of
George Reid’s hearers must have taken
home from the Guild Hall! The average
Englishman was long in the habit of think-
ing of this continent as a piebald place unfit
for the habitation of decent white people,
and it was mostly to correct that erroneous
view that people like G. Reid were appointed
to eat dinners, and make speeches m London.
Evidently the person with the eyeglass tor-
gets. In his earlier days cheap nigger went
hand in hand with the Freetrade he loved,
and now in his befatted age he imagines that
he really was sent away to represent a coun-
try as cursed with variety of color as ms
own policy would have made it. Rut Ins
policy failed ; the White Australia dream is
being realised, and all that is left for the
survivors of the anti-Australian party to
do is to sit in a corner and dream of the
mongrels that we might have been. But we
do wish they would be quiet in their sleep.

“R.R.”; While the exportation of Austra-
lians continues a cable states that 200 Eng-
lish immigrants are on the way from England
in one ship for Australia. We are exceed-
ingly English in this matter. The call of
the new countries sent the best of the British
out to South Africa, Canada, Australia and
the U.S., where they promptly became South
Africans, Canadians, Australians and repub-
licans of America. England, with its rotten
Freetrade notions, forced on a foolish people
by a few exploiters of cheap labor, promptly
filled their places with Russian .Jews, Ger-
mans, Hungarians and Polaks. Now we are
sending out our best and bringing in the bad
citizens who run away from England when it
is at death grips. But England was never
as foolish as Australia. When it lost the
best and replaced them with perilously near
the worst, it did not pay the fares or portion
of the fares of its immigrants, and we do.
No Australian State should spend another
copper in bringing in these undesirables; for
the man who leaves his country in fear of
conscription in time of war is a deserter in
face of the enemy. And these are many.
Here in my little Melbourne suburb there
were three Australians working as trades-
men’s carters—mere lads. They have gone
to the war, and their places have been
filled by three men from England, men of
from 25 to 30, dull as oxen, but not so dull
that they didn’t know enough to make for
the best place on earth. It is fine encour-
agement to recruiting, this spectacle of the
runaway Englishman calling the Australians
“fools” because they take up the job he
deserts. But the real fault is with the
British Government, which does not immedi-
ately enact conscription and prevent the
emigration of these eligible men.

In the United States it is now usual to
find the hotels of- remote villages built to
10 and 12 storeys, in imitation of the
awful structures of New York and
Chicago. A building which might have
spread over an acre of otherwise useless
ground is set up absurdly on end for no
decent reason, and its builders describe the
monstrosity as progress. Sydney sins in a
somewhat similar fashion. It is true that
the space in George-street, Pitt-street and a
few other localities is valuaole. but there is
surely plenty of room for the business area
to extend without invading the top storeys
of the atmosphere. Having regard to the
fact that the streets nearest to the centre
are so narrow and badly shaped that they
must at some time be re-Constructed, the
piling up of mountains of brick and stone is
specially absurd. Alfred Deakin brought
back the advice from America that nobody
should erect buildings intended to last more
than 50 years. The experience there has
been that all growing and expanding towns
are re-built within that period to meet new
conditions and new needs. Long before 50
years are over N. S. Wales will either have
fallen dead through an overdose of crude
HoLMAN-ism, or some big, sane person will
have taken a mattock and opened the closed
ports of the North and South. In either
case, skyscrapers in Sydney will be ridi-
culous. The broad, far-spreading building of
few storeys, with wide, open rectangles in
the middle for light and air, is the sort of
thing that suits our climate and conditions.
As for the idea that people will only do
business within the toss of a chocolate cream
of the main post office or the central
railway station or the ferry-boat land-
ing, or some other picked spot, the futility of
such an argument has been proved again and
again. If a shop large enough and good
enough to attract the best custom were
erected to-morrow in Woolloomooloo it would
draw fashion and beauty to its counters. In
fact, one or two big business concerns have
gone even further from the centre, and are
steadily drawing the crowds after them. The
notion that all the city should be hoisted in
lifts is elevating only to some insane pride
in novelty at the expense of common-sense
and mechanics and economics and every
other blessed thing that really counts.
The cutting off of the Saturday afternoon
delivery adds intensity to the long week-end
sleep in which the Post Office indulges.
From midday on Saturday until the follow-
ing Monday all postal and telegraphic con-
veniences practically cease to exist, and
things become as if Rowland Hill had
never lived. The carrying of letters which
have been prepaid is such a happy-go-lucky
business anyhow that a day or two lost
entirely out of the calendar could make
little difference; but the long tele-
graphic sleep is a nuisance. It is true, of
course, that some central offices are open for
the despatch of wires, but unless sent to
other central offices there is nobody at the
distant end to receive them, and so
the apparent benefit is a delusion. Yet
with all its “economies” and disregard for
the needs of the public, who may urgently
want a doctor, a five-pound note or some-
thing that only a telegram can swiftly pro-
- cure, the service does not pay its way and
is a burden upon the general revenue. What
is wanted is not less j efficiency but more;
not a cutting down of conveniences, but
additions to theni. To obtain these things
a Postmaster-General who doesn't care a
hang about votes should be . appointed—not
a mere figurehead, but a live man with an
axe. He will have some heavy chopping to
do before he gets through, but his bush work
will be worth while. Observe the sad, heart-
broken fashion in which the average postal
official sells you a penny stamp, and you
will understand at once where the lack of
economy lies.

The Fight that is Not a Picnic.
Of course, it looks very grey, and the end
is far off; but, after all, it never looked par-
ticularly cheerful, and the present urgent
call for more men would have been made
long ago had it not been for the fatuous con-
spiracy to bandage the eyes of the nation
against the light of truth—a conspiracy of
which, by the way, Tiie Bulletin has had
some further experience during the week. All
the talk on which the Empire has been re-
galed about the enemy weakening, and shoot-
ing his bolt, and starving, and being in the
midst of a financial crisis, and all the rest
of it has been just so much humbug. He
hasn’t weakened. On the contrary, after a
year of war he was able to strike more
heavily than at the beginning, and is now
embarking upon a fresh enterprise of great
magnitude. With the Bulgar helping him,
and the Greek and the Roumanian standing
by as spectators of Servian martyrdom, it is
not at all improbable that the enemy will
soon be astride of Europe from the North
Sea to the Bosphorus. In a sense, this
movement is an indication that Germany
recognises failure in the West. But it is
one which involves special perils for
Britain, and is only made possible by very
pronounced success in the field against
Russia, and by exceptional cunning or pre-
varication in what passes for diplomacy. On
the broad facts as they stand to-day, it looks
suspiciously as if the Allies had been fooled
in the Balkans with great success—a circum-
stance contributed to in a very great degree
by the unimpressive character of the cam-
paign against the Dardanelles. The failure
of leadership exhibited there has only been
partly redeemed by the magnificent bravery
of the soldiery. Had the Allies spent more
time in considering themselves than in talk-
ing about the German, and had they, instead
of habitually under-rating the enemy, struck
the Turk with quadrupled force, they should
have been at Constantinople months ago, and
the Balkan armies would now be looking the
other way. But it would be as absurd to get
cold feet now as it was idiotic at the begin-
ning to anticipate victory except at the end
of an exhausting struggle. The fight is for
the only things that matter—life and liberty.
The great need is for men to carry it on, and
Australia can send them forward with abso-
lute confidence in two things—one, that if
the Allies hang to the enemy long enough
they will surely lick him; the other, that,
unless they do, victory will be his, not ours.
Albert Farthing, M.L.A. for East Mel-
bourne, who recently declared his intention
of enlisting, has had to defer his “date,”
chiefly owing to a Farthing-Worrall case in
the courts. There has been no alteration in
the main Farthing intention, however.
The Vaughan Government in South Aus.
is accountable for a remarkable piece of
political strategy. The Railways Standing
Committee is elected from the members of
both Houses on a kind of mutual understand-
ing. For the Leg. Council Wilson was the
Labor man, but the party resolved to super-
sede him by Jelley. The Liberal majority in
that House savv no reason why the old hand
should be dropped, and voted Wilson in
ln the resulting row, he has been
practically expelled from the party, but
clings to his place on the Committee. So
tha Government has appointed a Royal Com-
mission to report on a proposed new rail-
way; and behold! .Jelley is on it, and Wil-
son is not, and the other names bear a strange
resemblance to those of the old Committee,
whose chance of getting any work at all to
do this year can be guessed at. Charges of
unfair dealing are freely thrown about; but
Vaughan sits tight on the ground that the
Opposition began it, by going back on the
honorable understanding that each party
shall accept the other’s nominee.
From an old visitor: —
The end is at hand of the Randwiek (Sydney)
Asylum for children. Although Henry Parkes in 1881
passed the State Children Relief Act, declaring the
State policy to be the boarding-out of helpless, de-
pendent infants instead of cooping them in insti-
tutions, the privately-endowed Randwiek home was
able to go the opposite way for 34 years, and has
now yielded only to the inexorable demands of war.
Occupying a valuable site, and representing
six figures in improvements, the institution
with its 800 beds has for years past had
only a handful of inmates. With the best
of attention, the best of systems and the best
officials, children find it hard to wipe off the asylum
brand in after-life. The boarding-out system has
its faults and abuses, but it is the proven correct
policy, and was the “modern” method all the world
over when adopted in N. S. Wales. Many prior
attempts to close up Randwiek have been defeated,
and desperate efforts were made to save it even from
the wounded soldiers, the trustees offering the
unused half of the building to buy off extinction.
As a last resort the Leg. Council was appealed to;
but even that champion of conservative methods
couldn’t do anything but obtain good terms for the
trustees. Randwiek was generously endowed, its
benefactors including Catherine Hayes, who lavished
money on it.

“Kodak” visits the Labor party’s School
for Snobs:—
The deserted Government House that overlooks
Farm Cove, where the flagship swings to the tides
when it is at home in Sydney, is an intensely solemn
Sundry Show; but it has the advantages of being
soon over and free. The writer and the artist dis-
covered it on a recent Saturday afternoon, three
years after an inoffensive Gov.-General had been
chased out to provide them and the Woman Who
Had Seen a Lot of Trouble with 10 minutes’ enter-
tainment.
After a preliminary inspection of the carved stone
crests outside, and a burglar-like reconnaisance
through the window of the ballroom, we stepped
guiltily into the hall and pretended to be deeply
interested in the painted heraldic advertisements
screwed to the walls high up.
The Woman Who Had Seen a Lot of Trouble
looked sadly at one of the uniformed guardians of
the vice-regal sepulchre; and presently the oldest
guardian started off like a clockwork toy in front

Admission Free.

of us, saying in a phonograph voice, “This way, if
yer please.”
“Oh, thenk yer!” muttered the Woman Who Had
Seen Trouble, and we trailed on behind and passed
through the first pair of glass doors.
“This ’ere’s the ’all. Up there’s th’ pitcher of
Queen Vietorier, and there you see th’ pitcher of ’ls
Royal ’lghness. There on th’ opposite wall, sir, you
see th’ pitcher o’ Governor Bumpkins, and that’s the
por-trait of Governor Slosher, sir.”
By this time he had his little audience rotating
rapidly. The painted faces of the starred and rib-
boned dead stared down indifferently at the absurd
rabble of intrusive dervishes, who danced with dusty
feet on the hall carpet.
“That’s all ’ere, sir. This way, please 1”
So from the hall, full of dignity and mellow after-
noon sunlight, to the ballroom, with the red-covered
dais staring out at the brilliant garden and the grey-
blue cruiser beyond. The click of an electric light
switch, and the pink-satin water-lilies of the big
chandelier glowed softly, showing up the quality of
two big portraits by old Joshua Reynolds. One
was Queen Caroline, the Bad Girl of the Family.
But the official phonograph was in too much of a
hurry to allow us to linger amongst the shrouded
furniture and size her up.
“These doors slides back, an’ th’ next room is used
when there’s a crush.” (When will there be a
crush again?) “Yes, sir. This way, please.”
Out flicked the lights, and we slunk after him till
we brought up like frightened slieep or intruding
dogs in the centre of a great, gloomy apartment
haunted by the ghosts of ten thousand heavy meals
and garrisoned by portraits of Governors long since
dead and gone. They were all in full uniform, these
potentates (I wondered if they slept in full uni-
form), and they seemed to have contracted the habit
early in life of standing in noble postures in front
of shadowy pillars with one hand resting lightly on
a table whereon was laid for signature a tremendous-
looking document or charter with pendant seals. The
grave has devoured the splendid originals; but their
portraits remain to appal posterity.
The Woman Who Had Seen Trouble sniffed ner-
vously and tried to appraise the massive furniture
while we elevated our chins and stared back at the
Excellencies.
“This way if you please, sir.”
The little string moved on through a back passage
from which soared a narrow, familiar, gossipy-looking
staircase into “th’ Guvnor’s orfis, sir!” Straight as
a sword from the pleasant garden without, a beam
of light fell across a flat, deserted desk, where, no
doubt, past office-holders put in some of their spare

“Double doors, yer see, sir. Useter hold th’ Cab-
inet meetin’s in ’ere. And this ’ere’s the waitin’-

sir, in ’is private clothes.” So that’s how Belmore
Markets got their name! And while we were still

portrait-painter (he has been sentenced to the wait-
ing-room for it), a little side door opened smartly
behind us and we heard a voice say, “And that’s
the way out, sir!”
He was right as usual, confound him! It was
the way out, and we obeyed him speechlessly in turn.
The Woman Who Had Seen Trouble was our rear-
guard. No doubt she cherished to the last womanly
hopes of being shown the best bedroom; but it was
not to be.
While we lingered beneath the old grey walls, a
little dazed by the sudden sunlight, she looked back
over her shoulder much as Lot’s wife must have
done, with one hand playing nervously about her
lips.
“I don’ know!” she whimpered. “It’s grand
an’ all that, with the pitchers; but I’d like to see a
few live toffs about and ! ear th’ pianner goin’!”
And that’s just what’s wrong with the place.

“Potash and Perlmutter” tell each other
every evening that Boris, the refugee book-
keeper, causes them as much trouble as if
he were a relation. The loud yell of delight
that goes up from each successive audience
at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, is a slight indi-
cation of what modern society suffers from
its kith and kin. The end of the world will
probably come about through some strong-
minded soul starting a massacre of first
cousins.

of Saturday, October 10, and the official mov-

There are seven reels of them, and great
should be the rush of the young and un-
travelled when the vision machine starts
whirring.

Dad Wayback and his family are show-
ing at the Palace how they do it up-country,
and the confirmed congestionists of bloated
Sydney are thoroughly enjoying the per-
formance. So far no application has been
made to Attorney-General Hall to fix the
price of admittance to the show below the
cost of its production by Dad; but, as that
is the accepted procedure in the city, the
Waybacks may expect trouble any day.
