The enterprise of Branscombes, Ltd.
which resulted in the establishment of an
open-air theatre and cafe in Sydney, is win
ning its due reward. Since the opening per-
formance by the Violet Dandies the well-
laid out Garden-show in Bayswater-road has
caught the public fancy and given an assur-
ance of a gilded summer season. Novelty is
the whole of it in Sydney, and the Brans-
combes have the article.
$ $ $
Edward Marshall, a cheery artist from the
great country which makes deadly things 1
for others to play with, came to Sydney
Tivoli on Saturday. The young man works
casually and rapidly in colored chalks, and
picks some of his models from his audience.
His turn goes easily, and he has a disarm-
ing trick of rolling up his rapid sketches
and tossing them with a smile to his vic-
tims. Mary Law pulls down the house
regularly with her brilliant and sympathetic
fiddling, and Ella Caspers exchanges her
notes of gold for avalanches of applause
and something at the treasury. The Le
Boy Sisters —they are the modern, long-
frocked variety of sistern —take their first
burst of melody seriously and slowly, which
seems rather injudicious when there is so
much first-class stuff about; but a subse-
quent relapse into pleasant and unassuming
ragtime atones. Mary Elizabeth, Betty
Donn, tenor Goldspink, Edgar Warwick’s
burlesquers and some others also inhabit the
bill, to the great advantage of that docu-
ment.

Warrigal and the rest of the bushranging

cleared for “The Midnight Mail” at Sydney
Adelphi before this is printed. As a thriller
the train-wrecking melodrama is claimed to
exceed all speed limits.

** * *
Australia, which has done much to deserve
many insults to its sense of humor, has done-
naught to deserve “Nobody’s Widow,”
tedious farcical comedy in three foolish acts,,
played at Melbourne Royal last Saturday.,
The grains of truth essential to all whole-
some dramatic compounds are wanting im
“Nobody’s Widow.” Leading characters are-
as obtrusively false as the situations the-
dramatist puts them into. Human nature-
gets no show whatever, and though some of
the dialogue has a cheap American smart-
ness, the bulk of it is the. merest piffle.
Muriel Starr, a mock widow, comes to stay
with her young friend Grace Palotta at a
villa in Florida, where there is an extraor-
dinary house party. Two of Grace’s guests
are her sleepy-headed fiance (Frank Stir-
ling) and the Duke of Moreland (Charles
Millward). The other four and a stage-
negro servant speak about 20 lines between
the five of them. They are remarkable only
for their habit of going out of the room
when Roxana (Miss Starr) and the prepos-
terous Duke are about to converse together.
They are the most witless, motiveless minor
characters in the world of fiction. -Readers
needn’t consider them dead for the purpose
of this narrative. The wretched shadows
were never born.
A PRESSMAN’S FAREWELL TO HIS
FAVORITE SHOW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The war reports from East and West
Bring news that ought to make me gay,
They tell me that we get the best
Of Turk and Hun from day to day ;
And yet in spirits I am low—
On Saturday the Strollers go !
I have it from the stable straight,
The winner of the Melbourne Cup ;
It’s over, bar to celebrate,
Immediately the number’s up.
I ought to beam with joy, but no—
On Saturday the Strollers go!
The Spring blooms blush and beckon me;
The sun-kissed water winks again ;
The Summer Girl steps gladsomely;
All nature seeks to entertain.
And yet, I scowl and say, “Oh, blow !
On Saturday the Strollers go!”
N.S.W.

Muriel Starr affects to regard the Duke as
dead, owing to her having caught him kiss-
ing somebody called Susette on the day when
she married him. He was disguised as a
Mr. Clayton at the time, and nobody save
themselves knows that her supposititiously
dead husband is a real live duke instead of
a spurious Clayton. This strange American
ignorance on the part of their mutual friends
remains undisturbed to the end of the chap-
ter. Nobody, save themselves, ever knows
that widow “Claytmi” is the Duke’s wife.
When they “throw off” at one another in
company the company never tries to fathom
the meaning of their throwing-off. A dozen
times the preposterous British peer reveals
his identity with the deceased Clayton, but
nobody attaches any importance to his slips
of the tongue. Hostess Grace Palotta and
the other creatures of impossibility are con-
sistent only in their practice of leaving the
Widow and the Duke together. Then the
pair of humbugs talk of how the man wants
to live with the woman and the woman is
determined not to live with the man because
she caught him kissing Susette. Their con-
versation is wearisomely repetitional. their
actions are the merest make-believe. Finally,
in the third act, a long interview
occurs in Muriel Starr’s dressing-room, and
thus a strong odor of bedroom drama (farci-
calised) is imparted to the discussions.
Incidentally Muriel has obtained a divorce
(Lord knows how) from the Duke of many

* * * *
Maybe the loose back hair, the dressing
jackets, toilette mysteries and other bedroom
comedy belongings will preserve “Nobody’s
Widow” from the cruel fate it merits.
Maybe not. The present writer, for one,
wouldn’t raise a finger to save the poor
thing from swift damnation. Muriel Starr
and Millward are not nearly light and slick
enough to make the best of their unreal char-
acters, nor is Grace Palotta happily placed
as a young and foolish American woman.
As for Sydney Stirling, he is required to
impersonate chronic tiredness, and circum-
stances are very favorable to that. Two
interior scenes are nicely done by George
Upward.
At Her Majesty’s (Melb.), where the comic
operas are reviving, the second week of
“Paul Jones” is as good as the first for busi-
ness purposes, and better in its artistic re-
sults. The concerted musical numbers go
more harmoniously and the voice of the
prompter is not heard on the seashore. But
time and tide will not wait for “Paul Jones”
after Friday. The giddy old Excellency of
Estrella Island will no longer be in danger
of h—l fire for calling his nephew a f —-1, and
Malaguena will cease to look rather too large
for her outfit. The good French opera is
to be taken off, that “The Dancing Mistress”
may be put on.
Also another change of bill is threatened
ac Melbourne King’s, where honest Dan
Bailey, the pride of Silver City, continues
to deplore the skunkishness of the local
“nobs.” “They sit at my gaming-table and
get a square deal,” says that aching widower
and devoted parent, “but they don’t ask me
to sit at their dining-tables and get a square
meal.” So Dan bribes the electors to dump
him into the U.S.A. Senate, which is the
rugged old person’s notion of “Playing the
Game.” The forthcoming change of pro-
gramme will be “Bonnie Mary of Argyle,”
where the wild haggis blows and the pibroch
builds its nest.

Gregan McMahon’s Repertory Theatre
will awake from its long slumber at the
Athenamm Hall on Saturday, the 23rd.
Three short comedies —“Lonesomelike,”
“Hiatus” and “The Convict on the Hearth”
—will be produced. The last-named _is the
lcvely little study of life in unfashionable
society which the Repertorians treated un-
commonly well a while ago.
Newcomers at Melbourne Bijou on Satur-
day were the two Gaffs, singing and talking
comedians, and Maud Miles, a plump young
songstress who has lately been appearing
with J. C. Williamson Ltd. Hamilton and
Barnes, the effusive pattering duet, are
reviving sundry jests of uncertain age, and
the Goolmans, Lawrence Johnstone, Carl
Merlyn and F. Sidney and Co. continue their
efforts as before.
** * *
Adelaide King’s, with a monopoly of
Variety, has a jolly, mixed bill, largely
American, with Kipp and Kippy juggling
and Bollinger and Reynolds on the wire.
The Royal is about to reopen “The
Rosary,” and the Tivoli has “His Natural
Life,” with a happy ending—Elwyn Harvey
as Sylvia, the picturesque Fitzherbert as
Frere, Pat Scully the parson, and Roland
Conway the convict. Brewster Jones
organised a fine Town Hall concert, and the
Commercial Travellers another on more
popular lines. Two strong dramas appear
on the film, the savage “Sea Wolf” of Jack
London at West’s, and the Puritanic “Un-
afraid” at Wondergraph, which house is
about to commence its open-air show too.
Dandies and other signs of summer
in the offing.
Another very promising young Australia
has laid down his life in Gallipoli. This
was Alan McKinlay, of N.S.W. Department
of Agriculture. Graduating in 1913 from
the Government’s Hawkesbury College, he
won the Farrer scholarship, and was looked
upon as likely to follow in the footsteps of
that famous wheat breeder. He joined the
Ist Field Artillery brigade as a gunner,
fought at Krithia, and by an act of con-
spicuous gallantry won the D.C.M. But
while he dodged the Turkish bullets he
succumbed to illness soon after receiving
the decoration.

McDouall Stuart’s journey across Aus-
tralia in 1862, from the Gulf at Glenelg
(S.A.) to the shore of the Northern Terri-
tory, is periodically recalled by the death of
one of the men who went with him and
whose names are engraved on the pedestal
of his statue at Adelaide. Billiatt, who
settled in England, still remains, but the
last survivor in Australia, Stephen King,
died last week, at 73. He was quite a
youngster on the big journey, was after-
wards a surveyor under Goyder and a trans-
continental telegraph-layer under Charles
Todd, and put in the rest of a busy life in
the Survey Department of South Aus.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“0.K.” : The war killed George Edwardes
last week. The musical-comedy king had the
ill-luck to be in Germany when the big trouble
started, and the Hymn of Hate made his life
so comparatively hard during his detention
that he never recovered from the effects.
Edwardes was still quite young when he took
over the management of London Gaiety in
1875. He saw the opening for a new form
of entertainment —a cross between comic
opera and Gaiety burlesque—and he pro-
duced it for all it was worth or a bit more.
The first musical comedies he exported to
Australia by arrangement with Williamson
and Musgrove, just 20 years ago, were “A
Gaiety Girl,” “In Town” and “Gentleman
Joe.” No subsequent imported co. of the same
class was as clever or stylish. Decima Moore,
Blanche Massey, Florence Lloyd, Madge
Rossell, Maud Hobson and Grace Palotta,
not to mention some pretty girls in minor
parts, were a bunch of charmers. Of the
men Monkhouse drew the most salary, but
he made less of a success here than Louis
Bradfield and Fred Kaye. Leedham Ban-
tock was one of the comedians and Granville
Bantock conducted the orchestra. Bantock,
long time of Australia, is their brother.
Charles Ryley, too, returned to Australia as
a member of the co. Of all the companies
that Edwardes ever formed the one that
crossed the equator in 1895 was probably
the strongest in talent and good looks; and
he must have formed many, seeing that he
was responsible for more than 70 original
and costly productions in his rattling good
time before the war.
“Busk” goes on the wallaby from Beer-
sheba unto Dan :
Mention by “Norbell” (B. 9/9/’l5) of Dan Barry
once forgetting to put on his black gloves when he
played Uncle Tom is a reminder that in coon and
other colored parts gloves are nearly always used —-
to prevent soiling the ladies’ clothes. Fred Belton,
who is still remembered by old-stagers in Sydney, was
a favorite of Chas. Kean’s, who left him an Othello
costume. Belton, rather hard up, sold it to Tither-
adge. There were fleshings for the arms and hands
in chocolate color. This Belton used to relate that in
the stalls of a Boston theatre a gentleman beside him
started conversation. Belton cut him short with,
“Sir, I want to listen to the play.” Afterwards to
his poignant regret he found it was Longfellow!
As an Edinburgh manager Belton telegraphed to
London for a principal panto, girl to take the part
almost at a moment’s notice. He was horrified by
receiving something that looked like a witch. But
at night! She was a thing of beauty ! Planche re-
lated how, behind the scenes, he saw the aged Madame
Vestris cowering and shivering on a pedestal as
Apollo. Up went the rag—and she ivas Apollo!
George Vandenhoff, as Clifford in “The Hunchback,”
had only one rehearsal with Charlotte Cushman as
Julia. “This is the ugliest woman ever I saw,” he
said. “Uglier than Macready.” But at night on
came a vision of beauty in golden curls, and he never
had such rapture in acting. Reverting to Othello.
There is the legend of the man who blacked himself
all over to get into the spirit of the thing. Ira
Aldridge, a fine Othello, was a real black. His son
performed in Sydney and Melbourne, but only in
dad’s comic parts, such as Mungo in “The Pad-
lock.”
Somebody in Melbourne suggests a
matinee performance of a popular drama for
charitable purposes, with the leading people
acting as supers and scene-shifters, and the
supers and scene-shifters acting the leading
parts. The leading ladies and gentlemen
of the company don’t seem to catch on.
Apparently they have some anxiety about
giving the show away.
Stratford-on-Avon has had its little joke
—it has been advertising itself extensively
as the “safest place in England.” Having
formally and officially annexed Shakspeare,
how could even the Huns do anything to
injure his birthplace or his tomb!
“Yung” : Final results of the eight-months’
ballot for the most popular “movies” actress,
conducted by a Yankee picture-show maga-
zine, give Clara Kimball Young 442,340
votes. Mary Pickford, who was leading
right into the straight, passed the post 5000
votes behind. Alice Joyce finished in the
ruck, with Mary Fuller and Blanche Sweet.
The winner will be best remembered in this
corner of the world for her appearance in
“Hearts in Exile.”
“Fax” : Talking of “Ma Mie Rosette” and
“Paul Jones,” nobody remembers the revivals
which happened at Her Majesty’s (Melb.)
some V2i years ago. Harold Thorley was
the Henry of Navarre, and Florence Young
the most unsuitable of Rosettes. An imper-
sonation of rustic maiden simplicity is quite
out of her line. Hugh Ward iriayed the halt,
maimed and one-eyed Colonel, and Celia
Ghiloni was the catty lady. Florence Young,
Ward, Celia Ghiloni and Maud Clietwynd
appeared in “Paul Jones,” and Pat Bathurst
had one of his best parts in it. It seems
longer ago than 1903.
“T.C.”: Actor Eric Maxon, who is now in
New, York, has lost the first finger of his
right hand —so mendacious Rumor tattles.
Whether the digit was taken off by a jugger-
naut Noo Yark tram, or was bitten off during
a passionate love scene by an over-tempera-
mental leading lady, is not yet known. Maxon
was representing the Hun spy in “The Man
Who Stayed at Home” (played here by Cyril
Mackay). One night an excited German in
the audience threw a phial of vitriol on the
stage, but only succeeded in doing another
injustice to Belgium by burning a hole in the
Brussels carpet.
Hobart is being supplied with a new
theatre. Ever since Zeehan ailverfield
started, E. J. Miller, a South Australian,
has been selling it furniture and sundries.
When Wilhelm went mad, Zeehan collapsed
like a bubble. Miller didn’t waste any time ;
he hired a train, put his stock aboard, bought
an old store in Hobart, dumped his goods into
it, and had a clearing sale that made the old
town talk. Meantime he was making up his
mind what to do with the ancient store when
he had done with it. Possibly because the
site was opposite the old Theatre Royal he
decided on a picture-show place, with bil-
liard-room and other attachments, all bigger
and ahead of anything the Speck has so fai
had. The building is now being pushed
on, and Miller has gone to the U.S.A. to look
round for extras in the way of fitments.
A son of Herbert Flemming, who was
well known on the Australian stage, ami
whose wife was even better known in the
musical world as Minna Fischer (she is now
married to George Clutsam, the M.L. com-
poser), has been killed by Ilun bullets. This
victim was Captain Herbert Flemming. A
brother of his is still at the Front.
“ Jwosp” : Hugh J. Ward is in the United
States, and by a singular coincidence which
must have given him much pain, several
N’Yark journals ran a yarn lately headed
“An Australian Hero.” This modest cap-
tion covers an even more modest description
of the excellent work done by Ward in. the
interest of patriotic and charitable funds.
It is also mentioned that when Ward left
Sydney for ’Frisco he was “accorded an
official salute as he passed the forts.”
Nothing seems sacred to these Yankee news-
papers.
“Fax”: How fitting was the end of Hal
Cohen’s musical career! Plump Hal Cohen,
Melbourne’s loveliest amateur operatic tenor,
was the pet of all the ladies. One would see
him in the audience at a Town Hall concert
with a pretty married woman on either side of
him both trying to keep the dear boy warm.
He was the tame pussy of “smart” society,
exceedingly fond of himself, though not arro-
gantly conceited. His leading virtue was
kindness to his mother, it was said. His
tenor voice all this time was rather absurdly
throaty and heady, for he had no chest notes
to speak of in his upper register. But all
the girls who like their heroes to be soft and
smooth were “awfully gone” on Hal Cohen,
or Cohn. Also, Marshall Hall extolled the
plump boy’s voice, and finally he was sent
to England. There he discovered that he had
a baritone of a sort, not a tenor voice at
all. He sang under the name of Colonna,
without winning a swift renown. Things, in
fact, might have gone ve.ry hard with him if
a love-sick heiress had not come along with
thousands and thousands a year. She thought
herself lucky when the gentle Australian
young man asked her to share them with
him. So he nowadays purrs, in the lap of
luxury and promises to die fat. His career
turned out just as a fortune-teller might
have predicted.
A Melbourne critic, finding fault with
Leslie Holland’s way of buffooning in “Paul
Jones,” said that he wasn’t as amusing as
W. S. Percy had been in the same part. But
Percy never played the part of ridiculous
Don Trocadero. It was Fred Leslie who
skipped around in the Don character, whilst
Percy was figuring as Petit Pierre the Insect
in the same cast.
Australia awaits anxiously the publica
tion by the N. S. Wales Government of a
full, true and particular account of the
great wheat-growing enterprise it entered
into when the world was nine months
younger. There was vast expenditure on
motors and ploughs, and men were thrown
into the breach without waiting for casual-
ties ; heaps of machinery (imp.) were pur-
chased and hurried to the several scenes of
action that the crops might be promptly
harvested. But there isn’t a blessed crop,
and the whole mighty concern is a heap of
stricken hope and p.n. and withered reputa-
tion. How much money has gone down the
awful sink is so far a secret, but the figures
are big enough to make the patriotic Gov-
ernment which imported a shipload of
machinery to handle the wheat that wasn’t
there wake up in the night and wipe a cold
sweat from the back of its joint and several
neck. Wilted corn three inches high covers
thousands of acres.
HER SUPPORT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She was not more than eight years old,
The subject of a thousand pars;
She acted kiddies pert and bold
With Judson’s Combinated Stars;
And not a pro.
In ail the co.
Drew half as much per week as Flo.
Her. hair was ginger, and her nose
Turned up in blighting haughtiness.
She beat her parents, I suppose.
Her egotism was not less
Than that of Pugh,
An actor who
Played Hamlet back in ’B2.
“And do you like to act, my dear?”
I asked her when we talked apart.
“Oh, yes,” she answered with a sneer,
“But with this push it breaks me heart.
I’ll ask you what
’S the chance I’ve got
Supported by this bumming lot?”

The Judge put on his cap of black,
The fatal words he spoke;
And as the pris’ner left the dock
A sigh, half-stifled, broke
From one—an actor, long-haired, thin—
Who brooding sat the court within.
“The poor wretch just removed has won
Your pity, I infer?”
A man who sat beside him asked.
“Not so; my envy, sir,”
In thrilling accents cried the mime—
“ They’re going to bury' him in ‘lime’!”

MISSING.
[Fois The Bulletin'.]
Never a word spake the soldier,
Silent and still,
llifle to shoulder
Guarding the hill.
Soldier, thy task is o’er,
Passed is the host.
No more the cannon's roar
Reaches thy post.
England hath pressed ahead,
Stern as of old.
Where she hath left her dead
England hath hold.
Red is the rusty stain
Dark on thy steel,
Grasses have grown again
Crushed by thy heel.
Comrade, watch on, watch on,
Mid springing clover!
God for his benisou
Give thee good cover!
Never a word spake the soldier
Crouching so still,
Rifle to shoulder -
Guarding the hill.
MAORI LAND.
* * * *
“Boyd Cable”: I find that my pals who
are out of this business —and feminine pals
particularly—are much less anxious for
details of the slaughter than they are to
hear about the eating and sleeping and
living. Of course, these things vary a heap
according to time and place and the “tacti-
cal scheme” of the moment. When there is
no big scrapping match on, life (apart from
the intrusion of shells, etc.) is fairly com-
fortable. We fl’m speaking of an artillery
division in Flanders) get our lations with
great regularity, and have our meals at more
or less constant hours. The rations are (1)
fresh, (2) preserved, or a mixture. At
present, and for some time past, we’ve
been on two-thirds fresh and one-third
preserved. “Fresh” means frozen meat,
bread and potatoes; “preserved” is tinned
meat (“Macconnacliie” meat-and-vegetable
ration or bully beef) and biscuit. In both
(1) and (2) are always included bacon,
cheese and Yam. Although all these things
are good and nourishing—always excepting
bully beef, which is the American red bully,
flavorless, salt as brine, and utterly uneat-
able except by starving men —they all get
deadly monotonous, and we should find it
hard to live if it were not for the Parcels
Post and the extras sent to us, plus an
occasional vegetable or milk or eggs obtain-
able locally at famine prices when one moves
back from the actual firing zone. The guns
are rather badly off in this way—worse luck,
as I’m on that job—because they remain in
action for months on end. On the other
hand, we frequently sit tight in one position
for weeks together and get a chance to make
ourselves at home in decent billets and dug-
outs, and so have the pull over the foot-
wallahs, who do a couple of days in the fire
trench, a couple in support, and then several
days in reserve or rest; and are thus always
on the wallaby.
There is very little bivouacking or camp-
ing-out for artillery, mainly because ’planes
coming over can spot such camps and pass
the word of their map position back to the
guns and have them shelled to glory. For-
tunately this country of Flanders and north-
ern France is dotted thick with farms and
villages, and the troops billet among the
fragments wherever they can find a roof
sufficiently whole to hide them. If such are
not available we dig deep holes, cover them
with a solid roof of beams and earth, and
crawl down to dinner and doss. When there
is a strenuous disturbance on all the usual
household arrangements go by the board with
a rush. Then one feeds a bricky biscuit or
a slab of bully to an unwashed face from
grubby hands, sleeps with or without a
blanket in any hole or corner of a trench or
gun-pit, or crawls for a spell into a gritty
and grimy cellar under the usual brick-heap
that was once a house. Under normal cir-
cumstances these things might be uncomfort-
able, but in all the uproar and upheaval and
blood and murder and sudden death of an
action, one is so dashed, devoutly thankful,
ai:d more or less amazed to be left alive and
in one piece, that the minor discomforts fade
into nothingness.
* * * *
Victoria’s Criminal Investigation Depart-
ment was never a very swift animal, but of
late it is following too closely the example
of its head, Chief Sec. John Weary. The
“heroine” of the Sale mystery, the missing
girl Jessie Stirling, disappeared on August
10, and the police were informed 10 days
later. One would have expected them to get
on any possible trail in hot haste. But
no: they inspected the locality thought-
fully, then considered the matter delib-
erately, and on October 4, nearly two
months after the girl and man had vanished,
they took two black trackers to the place
where the pair were last seen and to where
the girl’s dress-basket was found. Such
faith in the aboriginal is touching.
But it makes the job too easy for Brother
Binghi; they should have waited a year
before laying him on. the scent.

“H. 0.”: A characteristic of Billjim in
Gallipoli is a fixed determination not to
allow his leg to be pulled, and a rooted belief
that someone is always trying to do it.
General , who was being piloted by
Colonel , looked down into an eight-foot
trench in which a number of Billjims were
doing vigorous pick work, and, apparently
with the object of making himself a good
fellow with the boys, inquired : “Well, what
“BUSINESS AS USUAL .”
CUPID: "Well, I can’t say that the war is interfering with ME!”
are you men doing there?” Work did not
cease, but a reply was promptly forthcoming:
“What the has that got to do
with you?” The staggered Colonel an-
nounced that it was a General who had asked
the question. This allegation struck Bill-
jim as being unworthy of investigation, so
he merely observed, without looking up, that
“if the blanker has been in the army long
enough to become a Geneial he ought
to well know what they were
well doing !” There being no answer to that
in the wide world, the inspection ended right
there.
* * * *
“Silas”: Like a lot of other'people my
friend Piper sees German spies in his sleep,
and is determined to get the world out of
their clutches. He is a small, energetic
man (to me he always suggests an enlarge-
ment of the soldier ant, the little red devil
who can lift a 15-stone man if the 15-stoner
bestrides him aright). Besides being a
patriot he is an inventor, and his latest
appliance is for destroying German airships
and aeroplanes. The scheme is a simple
variant of the common or Fifth of November
rocket, powerful enough to carry up a man
in place of the usual stick. You simply
shoot your rocket at the aggressive Zeppelin.
The attached hero does the rest. An expert
of unusual perspicacity commented : “Yes, but
how does the man come down again?” My
friend simply hadn’t thought of that. He
went away, and returned within a fortnight
with an improvement on his device in the
shape of a parachute, to break the fall of the
man on the rocket. The passenger carries
a bomb which he hurls at the airship when
near enough. With a model it works admir-
ably ; and still the military authorities are
cold to Mr. Piper. Not a man among them
is. willing to take the trip. “That’s what a
man has to light against,” Piper confides to
me—“pro-German influence !”
* * * *
Eminently sensible remark by Judge
Docker at the Anglican Synod at Sydney :
The Church needed, from time to time, a new
reformation. He was opposed to everything- savor-
ing- of superstition. Vestments—what were they?
To his mind a mere nothing. He would prefer to
see a simpler dress assumed by the clergy, and even
by his Grace the Archbishop, in the same way as
the soldiers of the Empire were putting aside their
finery and dressing in the modest khaki.
Quite so. Now._ suppose his Honor puts
aside his official finery by way of setting an
example in consistency!
( A case which filled the Sydney Central
Court most week was the bad smell
that followed a squabble amongst the officers
the big Railway Workers’ Union. Dan
O Sullivan guided the fortunes of this body
w ““e it grew from a few hundred members
to 18,000, and amassed a cash balance run-
ning into five figures. lie was a capable
and energetic secretary, who got very large
concessions for his crowd ; but unfortunately
for himself he opposed the One Big Union
idea in uncompromising fashion; and when
the members of the union, after polling 7000
votes in favor of amalgamation, as against
a few hundred in opposition, were informed
that the Registrar of Trades Unions had
pronounced the vote ineffective, the angry
majority turned on O’Sullivan. Previously
invincible, he went down in the ensuing elec-
tion of officers by over 2000 votes, and George
Bodkin, for five years the union’s chief
organiser, got his job. Naturally the fol-
lowers of O’Sullivan were mad at his sum-
mary dismissal, and a charge followed
against the victorious Bodkin —concerning an
alleged fraud in connection with the pur-
chase of railway tickets for organising tours
in 1912. However badly O’Sullivan was
treated, it is good to know that the allega-
tion against the other man broke down
thoroughly.
* . * * *
“Carfax”: A Melbourne Socialist has
been exhorting the democracy to recognise
the inalienable “right to be lazy.” This is
a timely reminder of one of the privileges
the people of Australia are fighting for, and
one that has been overlooked by citizens,
calling themselves Socialists, who vehem-
ently denounce military -service. If the
Hun gets on top the Liberty to Loaf is
going to be mislaid for ever. The inspired
advocate of laze puts it this way :
Everybody has a right to be lazy, and when the
working class get a wholesale reduction of working
hours they will be in a better position to discuss
many vital problems.
Hear, hear! We will then have time to
consider whether getting up before 10 a.m.
is injurious, and also whether perspiration
ou the forehead should not be regarded as
prim a facie evidence of invalidity, and
therefore.of a right to a pension. Also we
shall have time to give serious attention to
the problem whether the right to laze shall be
recognised in the case of beer-carters and
wives. For my own part I believe that not
only have men a right to loaf, but they have
also a right to stand on their heads, to shift
their chins rapidly up and down, to swing
on a gate and to chew straws. This agita-
tion to reduce working hours is itself a
negation of man’s fundamental rights. De-
mands that we “Awake!” come from jackals
who would oppress the proletariat by keep-
ing us awake. Down with them, I say. and
let us usher in the Golden Age of Snore.

beautiful perch on a roof when last I wrote) or
some other elevated and unhealthy look-out. And the
batteries also get their share of the distribution of
shell favors. The Germans usually keep a number
of observation “sausage balloons” in the air—l can
count six at the moment from my observing post
and although these have to rein back far enough
from the firing line to be reasonably safe from our
shells, and so cannot spot a battery’s actual posi-
tion, they are near enough to get on to the gun
flashes. Having got this they direct the fire, and
our friends pepper away on the off chance of their
luck being in and their shells being on the battery.
Usually the bulk of their shells do no more than
excavate pits in some unoffending cornfield, or pile
in a series of new shapes the heaps of brick and
mortar that were once houses. But they do manage
to puncture a gunner now and then, or to wreck a
house that has been revived from its prostration and
had its tottering roof and doddering walls shored
up enough to make the place habitable again as a
billet. And even if they don’t do much actual and
physical damage they are a disturbing factor in the
daily routine. The proportion of hits may be small,
but somehow that would be cold comfort if one
stopped to work out the law of averages when a
string of 6- or 8-inch high explosive shells is heard
approaching at the gallop and raising a fiendish up-
roar sounding something like a regiment of runaway
electric trams, each fitted with a steam whistle
shrieking its lungs out.
* * * *
New prayer of the Young Turkey Party,
adapted from the Church of England litany :
“From Enver, hatred, malice and all un-
charitableness, good Lord deliver us.”
From a Headquarters officer at Anzac: —
The Bulletin’s regrets (17/6/’l5) that for lack
of enough reinforcements soldiers were being
“patched up” and sent back to the firing line are,
in some ways, quite sound; but if hundreds of our
chaps hadn’t been patched up and sent back here we
would have had a different tale to tell of our last
really wonderful night march and attack. Hundreds
get merely flesh wounds, but can’t stop here on
account of septic. They are sent away to Malta and
Alexandria for a few weeks, and come back far
fresher and brighter than we poor devils who have
been here all the time. Men who have been here
since the beginning are weak, whereas a man going
away, after getting good food and rest, in a bayonet
rush far outstrips the trench-dweller who has kept a
whole skin, but lived on “bully and biscuit.”
A thing to be proud of is our fellows’ wonderful
stamina and lack of “nerves.” This is most notice-
able when alongside other troops. Their sense of direc-
tion is also strikingly good. The other night, the
objective being known to all, our chaps marched for
six hours, never fired a shot although under heavy
gun- and rifle-fire all the time, but used only the
bayonet, yet were never “bushed,” although moving
in five-feet scrub of wild holly and honeysuckle, with
dozens of deep ravines, nulias, etc., to get over.
Other troops, on the same job, got hopelessly boxed.
“Maphsu”: Someone has blundered —
who’s going to pay? Sergeant-Major Lamont,
killed in action at Gallipoli, left a wife and
eight children to serve his country, with the
assurance that, if the worst came to the
worst, his family would be cared for by a
grateful nation. Yet see what has hap-
pened.. . Two ladies called on Mrs. Lamont
and found she did not even have wood or
coal for cooking purposes. Out of gratitude
(let us not call it charity) these ladies
bought her some, as well as a few pound i
of meat and potatoes. This for the wife of
a man who made the supreme sacrifice.
What a tragedy is here! And what an
example for those who are still wailing
against conscription !

From “Vagabond,” still from his Observing
Post in Flanders :—•
On this Western Front, so far as the infantry is
concerned, there’s “nothin’ doin’.” But this doesn't
mean that nothing is being done to ’em. The rifle
is having a spell off in the routine trench warfare,
but the guns on both sides keep busy and do their
best to save fellows dying of boredom by killing
them off more expeditiously with shrap. bullets and
high explosives. Every now and then, when some
mysterious spirit moves, the guns get work and
systematically set about making life a misery and
death a lottery in the trenches. The German
gunners are usually the first to break the peace, and
hurl death and disaster into our trenches, blowing
bits of them in and other bits out. Our guns imme-
diately join the merry dance and set to partners by
shelling the Hun trenches opposite. It is all highly
unpleasant to the infantry, who can do nothing but
crawl into their holes and try to pull the holes in
after them.
But the unpleasantness also extends to the gunners,
particularly in the persons of the Forward Officers,
who have to inhabit a front seat in the areas of
shell-exploding performances, and who, moreover,
must usually have their seat in the top of a tall
tree or on the roof of a burst-up building (I had a

From J. W. A. Bradford, formerly editor
of Quorn Mercury and Port Lincoln
Kecorder (S.A.) :
The report of my capture and internment at
Constantinople was exaggerated. After a debate
with a shrapnel shell and an argument with three
Turks I spent six weeks in a Cairo hospi-
tal, and arn now fighting a typewriter at
the Zeitoun Headquarters. The ’writer starts
from scratch; I’m handicapped with a gammy
leg and an almost useless left hand. To ex-
cuse The Bulletin (1/7/' 15), I might add that
my name has, through some regrettable error, been
in at least four official casualty lists as “wounded
and missing.” In trenches, hospitals and camps The
Bulletin [never mind that]. The average Billjim,
when he reads in the average papers of his “won-
derful heroism,” etc., merely yawns and lights
another fag.
“WITH A HEART FOR ANY FATE:"
“Oh, I can’t go to the theatre with you to-
night—I’ve got no clothes.”
“Well, it’s moonlight — let’s go down to
Manly surfing.”
Some months ago “Pica De Vinue” mentioned in
The Bulletin that I had started a soldiers’ paper
in Egypt. The start was as far as we got. Short-
age of paper was the trouble. The sergeant in
charge of the sanitary arrangements was an unfeel-
ing brute. He discovered where his paper had
gone, and forcibly recovered it. He might at
least have waited until after we had gone to press.
Two of my companions in the venture —Fry and
Featherstone (both S. Australians) —were subse-
quently killed in action, Chadwick was wounded,
and the others are either wounded or missing.
In spite of adverse circumstances, the Engineers
at the southern end of the Dardanelles occasionally
issue a sheet of sorts. Letters taken from dead
Turks, pamphlets dropped by enemy aeroplanes, and
local items of interest make good reading. But
there are many changes in the staff.
The British correspondents were surprised and
delighted at the physique of our first lot. One of
them said he saw a battalion which must have aver-
aged sft. lOin. in height. He was not far wrong.
In my crowd—-“the Terrible Tenth” —there were
plenty over 6ft. long. I am 6ft. in my boots, and
in my section of 13 men there were four as tall as I
am, or taller. Once a guard from our company
escorted two prisoners to Cairo. The “birds” were
6ft. 3in. and 6ft. lin. respectively, while the four
men of the escort averaged a trifle over 6ft. Our
neighbors—the 11th, from Queensland, where the
climate is so bad—-were just as hearty sons of Anak
as ourselves. Most of the Turks and Territorials
are children by comparison, though There are excep-
tions.

“Boon Boon”: The Chadbands run in all
shapes. A Grenfell (N.S.W.) resident
recently organised a • boxing tournament
in aid of the local hospital; but the
committee refused to accept the money,
principally on “moral” \ grounds. Yet
a great part of the lucre that goes
to keep the local hospital -.open comes
from much more contaminating sources.
Some of it smells really unpleasant. What
does the Grenfell committee propose to do
about that?
“De Oriel” : Though you’d scarcely expect
to find it there, the most thickly populated
cemetery in Australia is the ancient bone-
yard called Dead Island, handy to Tort
Arthur (Tas.). This tiny spot, about 80
yards in area, is the resting place of about
a thousand prisoners. Space has also been
found for many civilians, officers and sol-
diers. The names of some famous regiments
are engraved upon the headstones. The old
grave of the penal sexton is vacant and
yawning—its intended occupant died and
was buried in Hobart. But there are oue or
two very old-timers left, and one of them
may find a use for it.
* * * *
“F.H.”: The Australian service rifle is
fitted with sights which permit only the
upper half of the field to be
visible at the moment of dis-
charge. The special “peep
sight,” which the Australian
rifleman uses in target-prac-
tice, permits a view of prac-
tically the whole field. While
the superior accuracy of the
“peep” has been proved be-
yond question, the military
objection to its use in war
has been that it is not ser-
viceable in a rough-and-
ready manner. The affair
has to be screwed to the side
of the rifle, find when raised
and extended makes a some-
what excessive projection,
and this is declared to ren-
der it impracticable for
hurried battle-action. But
the practice of trench war-
fare is scoffing at the theory
of office warfare. Since Billy
Khaki spends a large part of
his time at Gallipoli in
quietly potting or being
potted, there is no apparent
reason why the authorities
should object to Bill taking
his own “sight” with him.
Every one of the many ex-
pert riflemen who have left
or are soon to leave for the
Front learned to shoot with
a “peep” : and each of them paid up
to 275. 6d. for his appliance. Science
is to win this war in the long run;
and probably, in the long run, the
Australian Defence Department will
itself provide a “peep” for every rifle
that leaves this country. Meantime,
since Bill has bought his own “sight,”
and since it can be used without re-
moving the ordinary “sight,” why
not let him employ it in the trenches?
It can’t burst and injure Bill, and it
is only a light, trivial affair. • The
“peep” at this moment in my hand weighs
30z., and measures approximately 3in. x lin.
x lin. The Germans use this help. So do
British army snipers. Why not Bill —every
one of him?
* * * *
The Sydney clerks who have unionised
have placed some gumpaper on their torn
amicability and decided to federate. The
repair of the rent was a wise act, seeing
that it threatened to delay the progress of
a cause that deserves well and has a hard
battle ahead of it. The disagreement arose
between the Clerks’ Union of N. S. Wales
and the Federated Clerks’ Union of Aus-
tralia—the latter formed in Melbourne and
having the adherence of the brotherhood in
most of the other States. Now that the
foolish dissension is over, the crowd will
stand on the doorstep of the Commonwealth
Arbitration Court 5500 strong.
* * * i *
Maoriland’s railway workshops have lately
thoughtfully perpetrated a fine little piece of
work —an ambulance carriage for transport-
ing wounded soldiers to their homes. Twenty
beds are provided, 10 on each side. The
interior is finished in white enamel, and fold-
ing chairs and stools are carried for the
convenience of nurses and relatives. The
vehicle is steam-heated and can be kept at
any temperature, and there are hot-water
geysers everywhere. A special gangway
enables stretcher bearers to walk straight up
and lift a wounded man into his cot without
any turning. The car is running on the long
Christchurch-Invercargill section, and is a
credit to the railways of Maoriland.
South Aus., which claims to do more for
its soldiers in the social way than any other
State, has opened a Y.M.C.A. hall to seat
1000—with billiard tables and other attrac-
tions—at the Mitcham camp. The opening-
concert was provided by the “Cheer-up
Society,” a strong body which has started
also a branch at Glenelg for the Morphett-
ville recruits, and is rushing up an enor-
mous “hut” by the Adelaide railway station
for the men in town. “It is no good decry-
ing the public-house,” says editor Sowden,
president of the Cheer-ups,, “unless you can
say ‘Don’t go there, come here.’ ” Con-
tractors and workmen of all trades are
doing nearly all the building and fitting-up
of the Hut by working-bees.
THE LIBERTY ROSE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The cables are singing again to-day,
And the end of the war seems nearer ;
A hundred and twenty big guns, they say,
So it’s getting a trifle dearer
For the Potsdam bullies who set the tune
That is played by the watching sniper;
The Huns will be starting for home by June,
With old Death for their grinning piper.
The cables are singing again to-day.
And the heart in my breast gives answer;
Our champions have sprung from the chalk and clay,
And there’s work for dragoon and lancer.
I know, oh, I know, that the tide’s at flood,
And the guns of the Free are booming,
For there in the garden, as red as blood,
See! the Liberty rose is blooming!
N.s.w.
THE GARDENER.

GOOD NEWS FOR STOUT PEOPLE.
RAPID FAT REDUCTION WITHOUT
EXERCISES OR STARVATION DIETS.
“One great objection to exercises for fat
reduction,” says a noted health scientist, “is
the after exhaustion and discomfort they
generally cause those who adopt this method
of becoming slender. Yet superfluous fat
can be rapidly and surprisingly reduced in a
more simple way. (let a dram of quassia
chips and three ounces of cirola-bark extract
from any chemist. Put the quassia chips in
a pan and pour a cupful of boiling water
over them, then after they have stood for a
minute strain the liquid through a cloth and
add the cirola-bark extract. Rub this lotion
into the fatty parts of the body, hips, abdo-
men, chin or wherever excessive fat exists,
for about 10 minutes night and morning by
a circular movement of the hand. As the
preparation is absorbed by the skin it dis-
solves the fat tissues underneath, at the same
time tightening the loosened skin, and leaving
it beautifully smooth and firm.”*
THE BABGE OF THE UNFIT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A demonstration was held in Melbourne Town
Hall on September 30 by the Australian Patriots’
League, in connection with the presentation of badges
lo persons who had been rejected for active service.
The Lord Mayor presided. —News item.
1 wear tlie badge, the P.L. badge, awarded to the
Best;
It was the good Gaud Mayor himself who pinned it
to my breast.
Some men there are who’ve gone away to do their
little bits,
And others who at home must stay—the Physical
Unfits.
lin one of them! My dauntless heart is burning-
now with pride;
This badge is just a proof of how I might have
fought and died
To save the Flag that rules the waves and domi-
nates the seas,
Had 1 not suffered all my life from rickets in the
knees.
Few men get stars or crosses for the perils that
they woo;
This badge suggests the flames of II —1 1 wanted
to go through ;
A lot of chaps who passed the test have died without
renown—-
I won my decoration when the doctor turned me
down !
1 won it on my merits, sure! Apart from eranky
knees,
I've got a kidney trouble and a touch of heart
disease;
Of all the hundreds of “rejects” who danger would
defy,
Xo wearer of the hero’s badge is more unfit than I.
I've got the pull on some who fell amidst the
nameless brave;
Xo stone or disc will ever mark the “missing”
soldier’s grave,
But one day on my marble slab, to signify my grit,
Jhe League will place my well-worn badge—the
Badge of the Unfit.

ERNEST E. KEEP, President of the Vic.
Emplayers' Federation.
Although for several years lie exhibited
regularly at the Academy, artist Phillips Fox,
who passed out in Melbourne the other day,
was, like Rupert Bunny. Ambrose Patter-
son and others, more in sympathy with the
methods and ideals o! the modern French
school.. He was the first Australian artist
to receive a gold medal from the Salon des
Artistes Fra.ncaise, for a portrait of bis
cousin, exhibited in 1890. Also lie was the
first Australian (fleeted a member of the
Salon Nationale des Beaux Arts, at which in
IHI2 six of bis pictures covered the whole
of a Avail in one of the principal rooms. In
the Melbourne Gallery lie is represented by
his big “Landing of Captain Cook” (done to
order) and the charming “A Love Story.”
Fox’s death, from a very painful internal
complaint, occurred ten days after an opera-
tion, Avliich fie had insisted on delaying until
be had finished his portrait of Bigjolin For-
rest for the National Portrait Gallery.

Washington messages report that the Arabic case

THE HUN : "How much?”
The cable reports Major Tremearue killed
at the Front. 11 is father, Dr. Tremearue,
lived at the luxurious Mandeville Hall,
Toorak, formerly associated with Rupert
Clarke’s uncle Joe. The Major, when killed
in France, was with the Seaforth High-
landers. Born at Creswiek. he was educated
at Ballarat College and Geelong Grammar
School. He began a medical course at Mel-
bourne University, but turned to the mili-
tary. At 18 he had been lieutenant in the
Ballarat Militia. His first taste of the real
thing was in the Boer War, from which he
was invalided to England. Promoted cap-
tain, he was offered an Imperial commission,
but declined it; yet he was presently sent as
special-service officer with an Ashanti Expe-
dition, and became Adjutant of the West
African Regiment at Sierra Leone. Re-
turning to Australia in 1901 he passed ft r
major and was attached to the Queensclilf
Garrison. Soon Africa called again, and he
went to Nigeria in 1903 to help raise a mili-
tary police force. This time fie spent more
than seven years among the niggers before he
was invalided to London, where he wrote a
number of books, mostly on West Africa,
lectured at Cambridge, and in spare time
graduated M.A.

line leg-pulling time at the beginning of tin-
war. have had their pot put on at last. It

couldn’t very well take up a wholly hostile
attitude. One of the humorous side-issues of
tlie business was that llirsch’s agent in
Australia was prosecuted if not actually at
the instigation, at any rate with the warm
approval, of the Mehrten people i Merton is
the English of it). Though the Mertons and
the llirsehes co-operate when it's Germany
v the world, they are otherwise bitter trade
rivals. Generally speaking, the Mertons
represent the Gentile, while the llirsejies
stand for the Jew interest; and what even
the very wealthy Jew has to put up with
in a social way in Germany may be judged
from the fact that, though the llirsehes prac-
tically own the town of Halberstadt. where
their works are. they are not admitted to
membership of any club in the place.
From “The Oldest Inhabitant” :
“Hoff’s” manager, C. J. Hope, died last week
after a short illness. Many years ago he came from
South Carolina, U.S.A., and took a hand at the
Palmer gold-fields. Then he was gathering tin at
Glen lanes. Afterwards he became connected with
a printing business in Melbourne, and later he
joined “Hoffs, Limited,” in Sydney. In 1854 Isaac
Hoff was a tailor in Little Collips-street East, Mel-
bourne. In the first ’GO’s he combined with his
tailoring business that of bill-poster. lie did the
posting personally, and with bucket, paste and brush
filled a long-felt want. The business grew. This
writer in 1862 gave him bills to post. As he did
the sticking. himself we were sure to have it done
honestly. Prior to that the two theatrical printers,
Charlwcod and Son, and Shaw, Harnett and Co., did
the posting. In a few years the firm became Isaac
Hoff and Son, and as late as 1877 the firm was
“I. and J. Hoff, clothiers and bill-posters.” Now
the big business is owned by a limited company.
Maoriland' remembered an incident in
another war last month. On Sept. 7,
istio, Captain Swift and a lieutenant of tlie
Goth Regiment, with a few men, were recon-
noitring in the Waikato when they fell into
an ambuscade. The captain, lieutenant and
seven men were killed and 11 were badly
wounded. As the captain was dying he
handed his revolver to a private named
McKenna. “Do your best,” he said.
McKenna did well enough to get the V.C.
He afterwards joined the railway service
and later had charge of the Wanganui and
Palmerston North stations. His bit of
bronze is now a cherished thing in the Auck-
land Museum. One man who was in the
happening is still alive, T. Innis, of Ilawera
(M.L.). He was amongst the 150 men of
the Royal Irish who rescued the survivors.
Dean Talbot, of Sydney, found three par-
sons in a single field-ambulance service in
Gallipoli—Private Andrews, vice-president
of the Bush Brotherhood, Dubbo; Private
MacGregor, who has been mentioned in dis-
patches ; and another. In the trenches he
met Lance-Corporal Maxted, who was for-
merly rector of St. Oswald’s, llaberfield;
Sergeant Stott, who was Canon Stott, of
Newcastle; and a son of the Bishop of New-
castle. Talbot had the sorrowful job of
burying Digges la Touche, the scholarly
Glebe parson, who, as might have been ex-
pected, died an heroic death. And there are
others —including that Wollongong Congre-
gational parson who, after inspiring his
little crowd to enlist to the extent of .’>7
members, himself stepped un from the pulpit
into the camp:
"THE SENTIMEN-
TAL BLOKE.”
C. J. DENNIS on a
walking tour.
G. W. Smith, who crossed from Westralia
four years ago to see that the trains on the
Tas. Government railways keep up their
normal average speed of 12 miles per hour
and pay working expenses and 2 per cent,
interest on the cost of construction, has been
reappointed as Commissioner for a couple of
months while the Ministry tries to straighten
out a kink or two in the Act under which lie
was engaged. Then he is likely to get the
job for four years more. He has been look-
ing out for one worth more than £l2OO per
annum : but jobs like that are not plentiful
these days. For its part the Government
is not anxious to make a change.
When the Great Slay opened Maoriland
Defence Department found that, owing to
its supply of German-made carbons for
searchlights being cut off,- it would, unless
“something was
done,” have to go
rou n d with a
slush lamp look-
ing for the enemy.
In its hour of
pained suspense it
called on Easter-
field, Professor of
Chemistry at Vic-
toria College, and
he, in conjunction
with the Welling-
ton gasworks, is
now turning out
an article equal
to anything of the
kind ever made by
the Hun. Easter-
field is a meek
little man. but he
knows his job and
could have shown
the importing
swaddies how to
do without the
German years ago
if anybody had
asked him.
M. L. SHEP-
HERD, Perma-
nent Secretary,
P’ritne Minister's
Department.
China lias got into tlie Big Scrap. About
20 half-caste Chinese born in Maoriland
have enlisted, and are at Gallipoli or train-
ing to go there. The family of Sing, well
known at Auckland, has sent three sons,
and already two of them have been wounded
at Gallipoli.
The man who gave Australia its first
official introduction to the stars celebrated
his 00th birthday last week. The Tier.
William Scott was appointed Astronomer in
ISSG, and two years later H. C. Ilussell
joined him as assistant. The parson held the
job for six years, and then, for healtii
reasons, resigned, became headmaster of the
Cook’s River Collegiate School, and. later,
warden of St. Paul’s College. At 90 he still
enjoys good health, which is a curious cir-
cumstance, seeing that lie was continually
changing one position for another because
he didn’t think he felt very well.
“Chasuble”: The Salvation Army lost
last week, by death, an officer who had been
second to none in value. Brigadier McPhee,
chief headquarters (Melbourne) treas-
urer and accountant for thirty years—ever
since Major Barker began, on a paltry
scale, at Collingwood Flat, without even a
roof over him. McPhee, a particular friend
of mine, made me think of Barnum’s pro-
cession entering Pianketank. There was the
Queen of Beauty in her gorgeous car. a
throne, drawn by a host of elephants,
camels, ostriches, buffaloes, gnus, etc.,
attached by garlands, and magnificently
caparisoned. People said : “Surely the draw-
ing of the queen does not require all those
animals!” As a matter of fact, not one of
them pulled a single ounce! The pulling
was all done by an old horse, covered up.
Modest McPhee was the old horse of the Sal-
vation Army. I fancy I see his spectacled
phiz peering over the battlements of Heaven
and protesting that he never did anything,
lie and Brigadier Ilendy, designer and
carrier out of ugly but useful buildings,
have been the most valuable men in the
Ilarmy. McPhee was not much good as
fugleman on a platform. He couldn’t sema-
phore his arms and run up and down, but
was worth a shoal of the officers who figure
before the public. Is the Army’s business
capacity a marvel? Look to the McPhees
and Ilendys, able business men and women,
cheerfully accepting wages far below their
market value.
Carl Hansen, of Ballendella (Vic. >, is
one of the Viking breed. He has two sons at
Gallipoli, one of whom sent a nosegay of
red, white and blue flowers (poppies, daisies
and bluebells) from the battlefield. After a
message or two of that sort Hansen, senior,
had to go to camp. He had smelt blood on
the letters or the flowers from the trenches,
so he left his irrigation block to the younger
chips of the family, and shouldered his rifle,
too.
The Historical Society of X. S. Wales
has persuaded the Government to spend a
trifle in commemorating the work of James
Ruse, the first man to grow grain in Aus-
tralia. Phillip, hearing that Ruse had
farmed in Cornwall, offered him an acre at
Parramatta, promising him more if he could
make wheat grow. Ruse succeeded and
Phillip signed over to him .’5O acres —the
first land grant in Australian history. His
bones are at Campbelltown.

Out of the Shadow
into the Sunlight
A man steps out of the shadow into
the sunlight, and for the moment he
becomes the focus of all eyes. Con-
sciously or unconsciously the impres-
sion he creates, the mental photo-
graph, remains long after lie has
passed out of sight. And it is just
in this way that half the world forms
its estimate of a man, whether he
ivills it or no.
It is a question : Does the average
man realise how frequently lie
becomes the central figure ol the
moving-picture of every-day life?
—as he leaves the doorway of his
home in the morning, as he joins'
the waiting crowds at railway plat-
form or ferry wharf, or steps out of
his office at noon —-even as he turns
the corner of the busy street.
Literally lie steps out of the
shadow into the sunlight many
times a day —every day, and every
time he leaves an impression, favor-
able or otherwise, just as he will's it.

stands out of the crowd.

The war hasn’t done this co. much harm
yet. The chances are that, had it not come
to the rescue, more or less, the figures for
last year would have shown up pretty badly
on account of the widespread drought and
the- extreme slackness of things in Westralia.
The explanation of the comparatively poor
profits and dividends of the previous four
years lay partly in the large increase in
wages and other costs, but (since the ship-
ping public has had to make that increase
good) more in the co.’s policy of building
vessels that really* got ahead of the trade.
Further, as the co. had been paying a big
dividend on watered capital, it didn’t have
the money to pay for the new fleet, and it
fell pretty deeply into expensive debt. This
shows: —
1910. 1915.
Liabilities (unused tickets and
sundry creditors) £79,514 £362,695
Assets (other than steamers, free-
holds, etc.) 118,503 153,341
Surplus assets . . . . £§8,989
Excess liabilities . . - £209,354
The war mopped up the three new big pas-
senger steamers one after another, and all
of them are now under Government charter.
To this must be added the demand for vessels
for deep-sea work —a demand which gave
this co. a chance to also release some of its
big inter-State cargo ships on more profit-
able jobs. One way and another, therefore,
the co. did far better than it has done of
late, notwithstanding increased taxes and
other war demands. The position is, how-
ever, not permanently improved by these
things, except to this extent —that the com-
paratively big revenue now coming in is
allowing the co. to wipe out some of the
debt which has been a gobd deal of a burden
of late years.

The Bulletin declines to guess at some of
the figures that are necessary to complete
the last line. It would be an easy thing to
say how much the dividends would call for if
the capital kept steady; but it is on the
increase all the time, and as the co. only
pays out div. on new capital from the date
on which that new capital is paid in, and as
that date is unspecified, the exact amount
of the dividend is uncertain. As for the
bonus, it amounts to 5 p.c. rebate on most
purchases made by shareholders, with a
refund of sales commissions running up to
30 p.c.
The co. is one‘of the most progressive in
Maoriland, and is so much in the lead of
the co-operative movement that Australian
producers with longings for co-operation usu-
ally send across to it for literature to guide
them. The trouble mostly is that a co-
operative concern won’t manage itself any
more than a State socialistic concern or a
straight-out private enterprise concern ; and
the failure to recognise that fact is costing
the people hundreds of thousands of pounds.
Given good management, which mostly in-
sists on being well paid, there is practically
no limits to the possible successes of co-
operation. This concern has been steadily
moving on towards new successes; and now
if is co-operating with other co-operative
concerns to eliminate the wholesale profit-
maker. The movement in which it is so
active is one of the utmost national import-
ance. The simple fact with regard to the
country producer is that he has been so
shamefully bitten by the city sharks that life
on the land has been made dull and un-
attractive. Premier Holman, of N. S.
Wales, said the other day that the “poor
farmer” generally dies rich. It is a stupid
untruth; not only does the farmer who
sticks to farming generally die poor, but he
and his missus spend in their lifetime about
as much as Holman and his missus spend
in a twelve-month. And the reason they
mostly don’t spend money is that they
haven’t got it to spend, because of the out-
rageous bites taken out of their earnings by
various middlemen. At this present
moment, for instance, when wheat is (Js. 3d.
in London, it is very doubtful whether the
farmer could get 3s. in the wheat-growing
districts; and 3d. is to come out of that for
the wheat sack. In a hundred ways the
co-operative movement, by saving the
farmer, can help to save the country. It is
the greatest remedy for parasites yet dis-
covered. So Tiie Bulletin gives this
society its blessing and forgives it for not
supplying all the information this paper
would like to have in the balance-sheet.
The co. finances clips and crops on the
road to market, and as that takes a heap of
money its own liabilities are pretty heavy
Liabilities.

However, besides the capital (which is in-
tact), there are reserves running to about
£130,000; so that the shareholders’ own
funds amount to about £535.000, while all
sorts of liabilities total £675,402. The debt
isn’t therefore at all dangerous, especially as
(1) no less than £240,857 of the deposits
have a currency of from two to five years ;
(2) £157,026 is held in cash or on short call;
and (3) £459,451 consists of “advances
against . stock, grain, etc., and produce
shipped,” which is or ought to be money
that can be got in pretty quickly. The
financing appears therefore to be done on
very safe lines. -

The profit is well maintained considering
the shrunken income from foreign exchange
and the larger outgoings on account of taxa-
tion and war-relief funds. That the bank
will be able to keep it up seems doubtful,
though, in view of the shrinkage in ad-
vances, as shown hereunder :
Aug., 1914. Aug., 1915.
Notes and bills. . £5,440 £4,813 Dec. £627
Deposits . . . . 1,865,370 2,028,241 Inc. 162 871
Liquid assets . . 698,887' 1,053,357 Inc. 354,470
Advances.. .. 1,587,478 1,401,714 Dec. 185,764
Premises . . . . 57,521 58,829 Inc. 1,308
But if the figures indicate the likelihood of
smaller profits they also indicate a distinctly
stronger position. Of the total liabilities
nearly 52 p.c. are now held liquid ; and that
is a very unusual proportion for a Tasmanian
bank. Further, the shareholders’ funds
represent £1 for every £4 Ss. of the public’s
money in the business; and that is a posi-
tion which ought to satisfy depositors and
others that they haven’t anything to worry
about. It may be that the deposits are
swollen by money lodged more for safe cus-
tody than anything else; but even so, it is
evident that, when the clouds clear, the bank
will be in a position to do a further fair
amount of lending. Which is another way
of saying that on its present resources the
bank ought in normal times to be able to
increase its advance business and therefore
its profits.

Share capital is only £27,783, and liabilities
on all accounts amount to under £34,000.
That the co. can do such a business on such
figures and maintain a stock of over £05,000
is due to (1) the fact that it hasn’t

sive city property, and (2) the cash system
being vigorously encouraged. Customers’
debits on July 31 were only £9452. The
co.’s premises are leasehold, and improve-
ments, etc., now stand in the books at
£18,040, which amount is written down at
the rate of about £9OO a year.

This is the poor relation in the family of
Sydney Harbor ferry cos. It has three
troubles: (1) on a capital of £50,090 it has
freeholds valued at £34,594, and they cost
a lot in taxes, and don’t bring in much in
either land-sale profits or picnic revenue;
(2) the plunging in properties meant con-
siderable debt; and (3) the co.’s services
are mostly in keen competition with trams.
Compare the position with that of three
years ago:—
June, 1912. June, 1915.
Liabilities .. .. £14,374 £26,042
Ship property .. .. 45,685 57,319
Freeholds . . . . 34,894 34,263
Other assets . . . . 3,146 5,246
On the face of things there hasn’t been much
improvement there. But everything depends
on the virulence with which ship property
has been written down out of revenue to
counterbalance depreciation. The ship pro-
perty item has gone up £11,034, and this is
how the co.’s mosquito fleet lias changed :
Gone Off the List,. Added.
Me Mel. Lady I erguson.
Birkenhead. Ladv Scott.
Shamrock. Lady Edeline.
The vessels that have gone were ancient
arks. The Me Mel and Birkenhead were
sunk, and the Shamrock was sold. Even
allowing that some of the machinery in the
old boats was utilised, £11,634 is so far
under the value added to the fleet in the
three years that it looks as if a substantial
amount has been written off against depre-
ciation. Which means that the profit dis-
closed has been fully earned after allowing
for reasonable wear and tear. On the
other hand, it is plainly not a gold mine, and
there are no prospects of a brilliant future.

The co.’s revenue is now practically all
derived from its holding of shares in what is
generally known as the Brewery Combine,
though it isn’t a combine in the ordinary
sense of the term —it is a co. which actually
owns_ the. business, and has leases of the
premises it employs. This Combine co. now
pays a very steady dividend and apparently
makes a comfortable reserve ; so the Foster
co.’s surplus profit is really a bit more than
shows. Apart from the shares, the co. prior
to paying its dividend owned assets valued
at £BlB9, mostly sundry debtors and
balances owing on property sold, and its
liabilities total £435. Its future depends
on the Combine; and as that is a brewery
concern and not a pub-owner, the outlook
is fairly good, in spite of legislation against
“the Trade.” At the same time this is to be
remembered: if things slacked off, for any
reason whatever, there would be no assets
worth mentioning to divide, inasmuch as
by far the greater part of the capital of the
Combine (and therefore of this co.’s share in
the Combine) is represented by intangible
goodwill.

The dividend paid for the year wipes out the
liability to the cumulative pref. shareholders.
The improvement shown is apparently the
further result of. the saner policy adopted
by a new management two or three years
ago. Prior to that the co. tried to win
Melbourne Cups when it was only up to
cab-horse form; and floods and bad debts
completed the disaster. Even now it must
go warily, because it is heavily in debt, and
it has still given much credit to people in a
part of the Dominion where the farmers
have paid most extravagant prices for land.
This shows the position in some important
details: —
1913. 1914. 1915.

Other assets . . . . 311 241 197
Of the capital indicated above the bulk is of
course lost. It now rests with the share-
holders who have profitable business to
give the co. and with the management to
decide whether there shall be a further loss
or a recovery. They certainly can’t lose
much more and remain in the business.

On the available figures the dividend is not

be formed. All the same, a co. of this sort
which in four years pays away £5373 and
puts by only £922 ought to vindicate itself.
It isn’t the right way to attract clients, nor
is it the right way to make shareholders
forget that, while their paid capital is only
£lO,OOO, their liability is £40,000. Further,
the odious practice is pursued of lumping
most of the assets as “sundry debtors.” The
law should not allow a trustee co. to adopt
these methods.

AFTER FORTY YEARS!
[Fob The Bulletin.]
The report of the Victorian Railway Commission-
ers for the past year tells a sorry tale Not only
did the traffic receipts decrease by nearly £400,000
which was inevitable—but there was an increase in
working expenditure of £372,000. Then the capital
charges for interest were added to by £90,000, and
as a result there was a deficit of £B!,2,JjS6.
—News item.
Forty-odd years ago, eloquent members,
Grinding blunt axes and rolling big logs,
Urged upon Ministers no one remembers
Action “to save the State going to the dogs”:
“Give us a railway, and give it us quickly!”....
“Build us our line—for it’s certain to pay!”....
“Down in my district the crops grow so thickly
We must have a railway to shift ’em away.”
Jones, Brown and Robinson—all long departed—-
Hustled dead Ministers madly along.
Millions were voted; the railways were started—-
Forty years back, when things couldn’t go wrong!
Featly and neatly the money was borrowed—
Four per cent, interest, five per cent, too —
Nobody worried and nobody sorrowed ;
All bills were met with a glad IOU!
Drought was a rarity, War a mere vapor;
Also, Australia was “too far away!”
Each line would pay for itself^—upon paper—■
What did the experts (dead twenty years) say?
“Boundless resources,” “Our Future enlarges,”
“Ideal assets and build up the State”
“Working expenses? Pooh! Those trifling
charges—
Look at-the passengers! Look at the freight!”
Thus spake the experts. Some got up and snorted
l\hen it was hinted that they might have erred !
Engines, material, rails were imported.
rt hat ? Make them here ? The idea is absurd !. ...
Well, that s the story—and now for the sequel:
What do the present Commissioners say?
Expenses and earnings are slightly unequal,
But the lOU flourishes grandly—HOOßAY!

ilie recent annual meeting of Melbourne
General Motorbus Co. disclosed a lovely
state of discord arising from the intrigues
which had sickened manager Burnside of his
.lob long before he resigned. Chairman
Downs opened the animated discussion by
stating that some of the other directors
thought it wise not to print the whole of
the manager s report.” The suppressed por-
tion, if read between the lines, looks very
like a complaint, that Burnside was persist-
ently thwarted in his endeavors to get all
the co. s plant and rolling stock under one
root. Coming from England to manage the
amalgamated co., he had found it still divided
into the ‘Red” and “Blue” sections, amal-
gamated only in the Profit and Loss account.

ALUMINOFERRIC is used by the Sydney Water
Board, New South Wales Public Water Supply Depart-
ment, and many country Water Boards and graziers
for precipitating impurities and clarifying water in
reservoirs, cattle dams, etc. It is simply thrown
into the reservoir in the form of a solid cake, or
dissolved and added as a liquid. In three hours’
time the most discolored water will be as clear as
crystal. One pound will clear over 2000 gallons of
water. It is exceedingly cheap and quite harmless.
Sole agents: William Adams & Company Limited,
Sydney, Melbourne, Brisbane and Perth.*

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yards
of road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yard, and the cost of the spalls per cub. yard aver-
aged 2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub.
yard, broken and spread. Their last contract under
the handbreaking system amounted to 6s. 3d. per
cub. yard stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is. ;
total, 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd. — a saving of 2s. 3d.
per cub. yard. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow 16in. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusber, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.* [2
