The unprofitable “Red” line was (and is)
housed at West Melbourne, and the trouble
seemingly was seated there. A bust the
“Blue” line, with headquarters at Bruns-
wick, was earning a profit of £4301, the
“Red” ’buses were making a loss ot £:..).> l.
Disorganisation due to factional influences
on the Board has been the matter with the
co. up to now. When both fleets <>l . buses
are housed, petrolled and repaired in one
building, as manager Burnside proposed,
and all disturbing elements are stilled, the
co. should be a prosperous concern.
* * *
Zeehan (Tas.) feels better now that
Mount Lyell, after a lot of testing and
thinking, has taken an option over the Her-
cules, Tasmanian Copper and Primrose
mines. This group of mixed metal mines
has never done much good, mainly because of
the difficulties of treating its complex zinc-
iferous ores. The Tasmanian Metals Ex-
traction Co. started to put in smelters at
Rosebery and had a contract to treat the
Tasmanian Copper Co.’s ore, but it never
got beyond experimenting. The Hercules
and Primrose used to send ore to the Tas-
manian smelters at Zeehan, but the results
were not very satisfactory, and the smelters
died anyway. If the Lyell people take hold
of the thing they will no doubt make it go.
Hercules shares have been down about 7s.
lately, and some of those who think they
know have been buying them up, reckoning
that they are going to jump.
* *
Great Cobar created quite a surprise lasi
week when the twice-adjourned application
for suspension on the Cobar Gold and Ches-
ney leases came before the Warden. Though
three months’ suspension was granted, man-
ager Hogan Taylor announced that he was
in receipt of information to the effect that
unwatering could commence immediately.
This job will take about three weeks, and
then necessary work for a resumption of
smelting will "be put in hand, if no word to
the contrary is received from London. The
furnaces will probably be blown in about
the New Year.
C.S.A., Cobar, is being overhauled by
.T. R. Godfrey, formerly mine inspector in
the Cobar district, as a preliminary to
another effort to secure a railway. Mean-
while a slow (painfully slow) effort is being
made by the co. to erect a water-jacket fur-
nace to treat the richer ore on the spot.
G. Moore, a surveyor from the Great Cobar,
has recently run his professional eye over
the property.
* * * *
Reported that a Victorian crowd is about
to take over the Golden Lily mine, Long-
Creek, near Windeyer (N.S.W.). Unlike
many of the shows Vic. speculators have
been introduced to in N.S.W., the Golden
Lily has been a good little mine, and lias
turned out a lot of gold for the proprietors,
the Winter Bros. At one period, worked in
a rather desultory way, when its farmer-
owners could spare time from the farm, it
was crushing op nee stuff with its little five-
head battery. It may be only a has-been:
but some of the N.S.W. bonanzas Vic. people
have stumbled upon never were mines, and
never will be. Even a glorious past is more
refreshing than an inglorious future.
*■ * *• *
Wiseman’s Creek (N.S.W.) shows no
signs of joyous activity. The Mabel plant
is gradually leaving; the recent high price
of copper failed to tempt anyone to have an-
other go at the old Phoenix ; and North and
South Wiseman’s still slumber serenely with
all the oilier little copper and gold shows
that small parties have opened in the past.
Breakfast Creek, the Bendigo-owned gold
show between Wisemans and O’Connell, is
also under a cloud. The 10-head battery (part
of the old Lucknow mill, Orange) is hung
up, and only a couple of men are pottering
in a shaft designed to test the reef at a
depth. Yet there are many less promising-
bits of country than that between the
Phoenix and Mabel mines, though the ore is
bad for either amalgamation or cyaniding.
Old Phil Foran is still working away on
Copper Hill,. and his latest shipment to the
smelters assayed 25 p.c. copper worth £l3
per ton at the smelters. Considering that
this show has been worked in a most primi-
tive way by the owner for several years, a
windlass being the only machinery, there
must be fair ore to stand the cost of raising,
freight and treatment. Copper Hill is said
to be the first place in the district where
copper was discovered, by Dr. Stuart Robert-
son, many years ago.
* * * *
Aliclc Sanderson has got his Central Gold
Mines Co.—to acquire the Central and Cen-
tral Extended at Southern Cross (W.A.)
into being. Fred Tricks is manager, which
argues a lieavv interestedness of the Edna
Ma y crowd. The co. secures also the appli-
cant’s interest in the Frasers, Frasers
South, and South Extended, which gives the
new ownership the whole of the Fraser line
and the Maori Lass lease and plant, includ-
ing a 10-head mill. The mines which the
co. acquires have milled 344,776 tons for
£755,350. Early-day costs were as much as
an ounce a ton, and cyanide was unknown.
The ore body became hard below 120 ft., and
water heavy, and the later history consists
mainly of tributors’ returns of 1489 tons for
3783 tons. The Central main shaft is down
245 ft., with a drive north in ore said to be
of higher grade than that at 120 ft., below
which level the mine is virgin. The co. is
in 60,000 shares of £l, of which 30,000 are
contributing.
* * * *
Edna May Battler (W.A.) claims that
three assays made under the supervision of
Williams, manager of the Edna May, gave
soz. 7dwt., 2oz. 13dwt., and loz. IJdwt.
The first was obtained from a sample broken
underfoot at the 50ft. level, the second from
a similar one taken out of the 100 ft. level,
and the third was from the dump. A 100-
ton crushing estimated to average 2oz. over
the plates is about to be treated.
Edna May Junction lias committed suicide
and so added another to those that lie sleep-
ing in the Westonia burial-ground. Bome
of the Westralian shareholders are greatly
incensed at its action, and would if. they
could oppose its interment. However, it did
the best thing under the circumstances. By
doing away with itself now seripholders -will
receive about 2s. per share, whereas if it
had delayed the tragedy much longer they
would have got nothing.
A Bulletin man at Westonia
Edna May hasn’t done badly. So far it has
given nearly four times its subscribed capital to
shareholders, and its ore reserve contains a profit of
quite as much —a thing rarely seen in a quartz mine.
The “Deeps” has a first-rate chance of becoming- a
mine at depth, but the battery proposed by the con-
trollers is not wanted yet. Shareholders should put
up enough money to sink the shaft and develop
ore reserves. At a later stage, when there is a fair
tonnage in sig-ht, a mill can be installed. Mean-
while mine development is the scientific step to
take. As every mining man knows, it gives the
mine, as a mine, the best chance in the long run.
The probable trend of the shoot is the chief topic
of local discussion, and anyone who speaks out of
his turn runs the risk of being “plugged.” Some
argue that there won’t be much gold left in the reef
by the time it leaves the Edna May; others reckon
that the body will remain for ever in the “Deeps” ;
others again that the Central will get a look in
before the shoot goes much deeper. Contention No. 1
is blown out by the fact that there is nothing to
indicate that there will be any substantial falling-
off of values in depth. Now for the other two. The
Centralites claim that the pitch of the shoot will
for a certaint}' carry it across the corner of “Deeps”
into their show. The “Deeps” crowd scout this
theory and declare that the run of gold is main-
taining its normal and regular course and gaining
both in length qf ore and length of shoot as the
reef underlies north towards and-into the “Deeps.”
In support of this they point to the fact that in
the Greenfinch, in the Consolidated and even in the
Central itself all the shoots of gold pitch west.
Only further development can decide who is right,
but the odds appear to be overwhelmingly in favor
of the “Deeps.” To overcome any serious water
difficulty that may arise in the “Deeps” shaft, man-
ager Stuckey proposes to put down drill holes 30ft.
ahead, and by means of a powerful pressure pump
force the water out of the country rock and put
liquid cement in its place. The cement will be given
three days to set, and sinking will then be continued
through it and until another series of holes are
necessary. The method has succeeded elsewhere,
and may do so here. Development work at the No.
4 (300 ft.) level in the Edna May is at a standstill
and will remain so until the extra pumping plant
that is to grapple with the water difficulty is in-
stalled. This will perhaps mean another three
months’ wait.
The most disappointed co. on the field is the
Greenfinch. First it had a chance of acquiring the
Edna May arid refused; then it was successively
offered the Central and “Deeps,” but turned up its
nose at both. Now it is in very reduced circum-
stances. The rest of Westonia is made up of leases
that are mere speculative counters. Two or three
are not without value, but most are dreadful rubbish.
The market has been lively in Edna May
Deeps. Kalgoorlie and Perth crowds, who
won’t swallow the freakish Westonia field,
got a gruelling over Ednas. They have never
understood the Melbourne speculator, with
his small Share-register company and his
bullish belief in any concern on to which he
freezes. Per contra, the Westralian
measures up ore reserves, and then sails in
on the bull or bear tack. Hence his original
nip over Edna Mays and the rise in the price
of shares to over £9. Some Westralians who
were squeezed over Ednas wanted to get a
bit of their own back when they took to
specking Deeps. They might have succeeded
if no one else had joined in the game. But
an Adelaide platoon tumbled into the firing-
line—all old stagers at the game, who ought
to have known better. Now the hunt on the
market is for scrip to complete deliveries.
As with the Duke of York squeeze, a lot of the
intermediates are being winnowed out by the
adoption of clearing-house tactics, but there
will be a few left to dance to the piping of
the bull crowd. To make the jig the livelier
there. is a chance of the Edna May folk
agreeing to swallow the Deeps. If that be
not done, manager Stuckey’s report to the
Deeps Board on what water means in the
way of finance lias to be chewed over.
From a Melbourne man :
lie the Linden (W.A.) field (B. 30/9/’l5). From the
Olympic since the first return in 1913 the owners have
treated 156 tons from 100 ft. down (not 30ft., as The
Bulletin said) for a yield of £4OOO. The other
property referred to is presumably the Democrat, and
from this a crushing of 1881 tons, obtained at a
depth of 270 ft., yielded 61370 z. So the field can’t
be so very “badly handicapped by water in large
quantities at a shallow depth.’’
Westralia’s gold output still shrinks. For
the nine months ended September 907,6630 z.
valued at £3,855,512 were produced, which
is substantially below the figures for the
same term last year. It seems that 1915’s
total will be about 25,0000 z. below that of
J 914, and the lowest since the fields were
discovered.
*• * * *
Whim Well Copper Co. (W.A.) again
seeks reconstruction. This time it wants to
be a c-o. with a capital of £225,000 in £1
shares, which will carry a liability of 55.,
shareholders being offered share for share.
It also proposes to issue £52,000 worth of
7 p.c. debentures and £7OOO worth of non-
eumulative G p.c. debentures for the pur-
pose of satisfying present debenture holders
and other creditors. Of the ordinary shares
100,000 are guaranteed. Calls on these are
expected to realise £22,500, and after paying
various exes, the new concern will, if every-
one pays up, have £15,000, which is con-
sidered sufficient for a fresh start and the
shipment of. ore.

Chaffers (W.A.) is to be auctioned on
the 27tli inst. Neither Boulder nor Horse-
shoe —its next-door neighbors—are said to
be keen, and if they do not bid, and Lake
View and Star (which also adjoins) re-
fuses to make an offer, the “lot or lots” will
be split up among second-hand dealers.
Black Range West (W.A.) has a good
barracker in the local paper, which says
that the last crushing of 25dwt. per ton may
be taken as a fair average of the ore in
sight. The Nos. 2 and 3 levels have lately
been connected, and driving has been re-
sumed in what is described as a “fine body
of stone.” The sljow has been a long time
justifying expectations, and there is still a
strong doubt as to whether it will do more
than keep a few men in employment and
help the local store- and pub-keepers to make
a living.
A howl ascends from Jumbunna Coal,
Gippsland, because it has achieved a bank
debit during the winter months when trade
ought to have been brisk and profits good.
The co.’s record is good as a wage-payer,
poor as a dividend-getter. In 25 years
1,096,781 tons of coal have gone to the
market. Goodness knows what concessions
the State railways have made to help profits,
but wages have piled up to £535.906, while
shareholders have netted only £36,000 in
dividends. Of course there is an explana-
tion ; but it isn't the one the directors throw
on the table. Faulted and narrow seams—
that’s the chief trouble. It may have been
Bulgar tactics for men to strike when the
winter trade was in bloom; but as they
can’t put up an effective scrap during sum-
mer, they had to choose their fighting-
ground. Yet, with plenty of backs open,
Jumbunna should crawl on to the dividend
list again, even after yielding a bit more to
the workmen.
* * * *
Specimen Hill, Bendigo, has gone back
to 280 ft. to test the shallow ‘'round by winz-
ing in centre country. This is because the
intermediate below 1335 ft. has not panned
out nicely. The idea there was to pick up
the run of shoots that helped Virginia next
door. As 1280 tons of spurry stuff gave
Specimen Hill only 3060 z., it looks as if
the manager has done the right thing in
starting out on the upper plane. Still, as
the deep level is on the course of a slide,
there is always a chance of cutting into a
patch near the fault. But, anyway, Speci-
men Hill is a drab-colored show.
That ex-Bullfinch float, the Hansel Mon-
day. of Bendigo, comes on the call list after
having tossed a cool £lO.OOO into the Cars-
halton side-line. The co.’s best prospect is
at 484 ft. There a big spurry formation
tantalises the management by keeping just
under the pay line. If it were not for that,
Adelaide would simply skip out of Bendigo.
At Charters Towers the market is still
deadly, mainly because only two local mines
—Brilliant Extended and Papuan—have got
on the div. list this year. And the Extended
has been spending more than it earned for
the last three months, so shares are down
again. A couple of other shows are brighter.
The East Mexican, which is working a hang-
ing wall section of the Day Dawn reef close
to the New Brilliant Freeholds, reports 232
tons for £2758; and the Victory from its
last crushing of 98 tons got £998. Clark’s
is hauling about 30 tons a week, so it will
soon have another crushing. The last 176
tons gave £lB2l.
* * * *
Brilliant Deeps, Charters Towers, closed
its half-year £1938 in debt. In the six
months 3541 tons were crushed for £10,121,
or £2 17s. Id. per ton. In the deepest level
(No. 8) the crosscut has exposed three sepa-
rate reefs; but this splitting up entails
expense. A winze is down 90ft. below No. 8
level, and the reef for the last 20ft. of sink-
ing has been over 3ft. wide and is of fair
quality. The gamble some years since in
Brilliant Deeps was based on a big make
of 2oz. stone in No. 4 level, and a develop-
ment of a similar nature in No. 9 would
shake things up. It isn’t impossible. There
are 150,000 shares of ss. each, 100,000 con
tributing, with Is. uncalled.

BOTTLED IN FRANCE.
A Good Corn
Remover.
Those who suffer from corns and hard skin
on the soles of the feet w'll find, it a great
boon to apply a little Atla Resin to the corn,
which, when applied to the corn or hard skin,
and a little covering of cotton wool on the
the top to prevent it sticking to the stocking,
will remove the corn in a day or two. The
Atla Resin seems to act by getting thor-
oughly into complete contact with the corn,
and thus being able to penetrate it, and not
to act on the surface only. It is certainly
the latest cure for these troublesome and
painful structures, and. according to all re-
ports by those who have used it, oue of the
best.* [2

Sydney, October 11. 1915.
Aly Dear Arini, —
Race week had a stormy interlude on
Wednesday; but for the last day things
calmed down into one of those nondescript
occasions when furs and pneumonia bodices
parade together. Everybody seemed happy
—at the start, at any rate. The G.-G.
strolled round in his week-day clothes, as
incog, as a Governor-General who is wel-
comed with “God Save” can be. Lady
Helen’s favorite ostrich fluff was not in the
pm, his Ex. being a grass-widower for the
afternoon. The Strickland family, or one-
third of it, was out West counting the flocks
and willy-willies of the plains. Perhaps
that’s why local Society let itself go. An
impromptu cake-walk given by three fashion-
ably-dressed young things was one of the
most popular items of the day. When the
hand suddenly plunged into ragtime the girls
apparently found their feet bolting. And
they danced before the crowded grandstand
with much grace, too —one of them in par-
ticular. The apparition of a masculine
devotee of Bacchus ambling towards them
with uncertain step and a red handkerchief
iliove the maidens into entrenchments.
The Gordon girls—Dorothy and Jeanette
-were mildly sensational in freakish mus-
lins. One sister was in a Revue frock —a
Tou Ton allurement, decollete in ilie place
where the breeze blows through the top of
the lungs. There was a lively expression
about this garment which attracted the
"knots.” Sister Jeanette's white flounces,
edged with china blue, and supported by a
stagey get-up. were at odds with flimsy
lingerie that drooped below the short dolli-
sh irt.
Dead-white complexions are the thing;
but Saturday’s breeze gave some of them a
mottled-mauve effect, against which incessant
powdering was helpless.
Amongst the damsels of the neat and
spruce order was Dorothy Dangar. Her
check suit was in the almost mic-roseonic
pattern of ihe moment. A Queenslander,
Airs. Baldock. was up to the last instant in
a baggy glace garment that was tightly
shirred where ir didn't flop. The sleeves
were first cousins to a Lord Lisbon's Sunday
lingerie. Airs. Fred AlcAlaster was unob-
trusively gowned in navy blue. A few
ostrich plumes waved in the Holy of Holies
—that inner retreat in the men’s official
stand in which women are tolerated but not
encouraged. The tall Dora Alexander
ambled round in white embroidered muslin
in the wake of the recent Society Revue
crowd.
Some of the very short skirts showed quite
enough to let the spectators decide whether
the owners would stand up well in the
chorus. Hats were as small as tarts and as
large as Quaker tiles. The coatee that
waggles a small sparrow-tail came in.
Frobably it will go out with a rush, for its
loaded hem rap-raps like l’oe’s tapping fowl
uf sable hue. The buxom Enid Bayley,
quietly frocked in black, wore the round black
hat so dear to our Best People. They have
taken this pudding-basin millinery to their
hearts for some time, and wear it so deeply
embedded over one eyebrow that they all
look exactly alike.
The Allies' Consuls rested from the din of
patriotic shows during race week. Bel-
gium’s Consul AVatteeuw. the beau of the
diplomatic circle, was faithful to a pepper-
and-salt mixture in sac suiting. Like the
girls, he shows a neat ankle between the hem
of his nether garment and his shoes.
John Brown gave an eye-opening spread
at the Australia on the night the Derby
was run. For days before the invited had
been clearing their throats to sing:
John Brown's won it with Wallace Isinglass,
And his luck goes marching on.
But the owner of Wallace I. was disap-
pointed. Cetigne passed the judge’s nose
first.
Dr. Stewart McKay celebrated his racing
win of f IS, OOO with a dinner at the Aus-
tralia oil Thursday night. Three tables and
38 guests. The Tlieo Markses and Herbert
Markses and the Yivers and the Philip Alor-
t-ons and daughters and the Arthur Alac-
Arthurs and the beautiful Airs. Sep Levy
and a Aliss I’udney of whom mention is
made as “fair and frightfully smart” and
Aliss Nellie Mossong the fashionable hair-
dresser and noted good-looker. I’ve used
too many “ands” and no stops and I’m out
of breath trying to catch up with all the
beauty and fashion among the lucky 38 at
that lovely dinner. (One small roasting
Chick now costs 6s. 6d. !)
Railway-Chief Harper wound up his
three months’ holiday with a 10 days' stay
on Kosciusko. Before that he had “done”
F. and Honolulu, and come hack with a
mixed picture of wonderfully prolific vege-
tation and more wonderfully prolific politi-
cians. In Fiji the native at the moment
holds the balance of political power, and
e\ery corner has its stump orator on a
fruit-case in the midday dinner hour.
Having well braced himself up Air.
Harper has taken his coat off again at head-
quarters. Justice Harvey, with a young
law-sprig as his mate, was amongst the
Alonaro sndwhalls at the same time.
Poor little Robertson Kerr-Clark fell in
the recent fierce lighting in France. A
grandson of old Jock Robertson on mother's
side. ' Robin’s” father left him a handsome
slice of his N. S. Wales station property.
1 Ie Avas A.D.C. to Beauchamp, then on the
Rawson -staff, and later for a short time in
Islington’s menage in ALL. Finally he
showed us his pretty Irish wife during the
Chelmsfords' reign. She was Lady Beatrice
Aioore till the Australian persuaded her to
try Clark for a surname. He wasn't keeu
on soldiering in time of peace, though he
sometimes wore the fancy togs of a local
Scottish regiment.
The Bellevue Hill garden fete for sheepskin
waistcoats caught the brisk breeze that
chanced to be blowing that way on Friday.
But the Knox family, who lent their two
adjacent chalets for the affair, had some
luck. It wasn’t on the day, but the day
before, while preparations were merely pend-
ing. that the gale removed a marquee and
banged it down elsewhere in a crumpled
heap. Race-week weather was so uncertain
that a song of thanksgiving ought to have
tailed off the opening text by Lady Helen At.
Ferguson. A table of the woolly comforts,
with a man in khaki as shopman, did as
much business as a bargain-counter at a
summer sale.
“The Rustics,” who gave an outdoor
show, included two of the society mummers
in the recent “Revue” —Thelma Aladdrell
and Alonty Stephen. “The talent” was
lightly garbed in check cotton breeches and
the smock which English Hodge has (iu
moderation) east off for khaki. The new
Help. Haskett Smith, made a serious pro-
gross at the vice-regal heels. Airs. Harry
Finn and daughters Kathleen and Aloya,
with Airs. Collins, were the most attentive
waitresses the local appetite has encount-
ered for some time. Archdeacon D’Arey
Irvine's Best Hat. with wide curly sides,
attended the Best People. The flower stall,
sweet with the scent of lilac, was rushed by
all comers.
At the Conservatorium Peter Dawson is
going through the satisfactory phase known
as "an extended season.” On Friday rhe
baritone was in his best voice, and rained
encore numbers on an enthusiastic audience.
Too boisterous in the Pagliacci “Prologue”
and “Invietus,” Peter, whose lungs are like
a blacksmith’s bellows, gets very red in the
face when he lets himself go with a roar.
But his normal efforts —heard in Clay’s
"Sands o’ Dee” and Coleridge-Taylor’s
haunting “Eleanor” —are worth a long jour-
ney to hear. Annette George, the buxom
Airs. Peter, chirped pleasantly, with careful
enunciation. Ernest IToberts at the fiddle
opened the show with Frank Hutchens’s fore-
lock waving at the piano.
The Jap. parasol made a daring entry
into the gales of race-week. But it gave the
ladies in charge so much hard work to keep
it on earth that it retired for a rest-cure till
the atmosphere is less like a Balkan in-
surrection.
On the edge of starting for Randwick on
Saturday. Aliss Burdekin got a cable to say
brother Sidney Burdekin is missing some-
where at the Dardanelles.
Airs. Tony Hordern’s smart, mouse colored
coat and skirt was a mile or two away
from the freaks that were shown round the
Randwick lawn on Saturday. The breeze
had a nippy feeling in the shade, and the
Avell-tailored were It. Auntie Madden is
staying with her handsome niece.
Yvonne Charvin didn’t g t such a big
audience as her concert deserved. Bac-
night—any big race night—is a bad da'*-
for a musical show, except of the high-kick-
ing variety. If the hayseeds in town for
the Randwick party had been forewarned
that Beauty in fancy togs would make such
a brilliant display in the Town Hall, things
would have hummed. As it was. there were
too many cane seats visible in the big hall.
G.-G. Ferguson and his lady stepped
briskly up to take their places on the blue
carpet. Later on the Allies’ Consuls yawned
furtively once or twice in the same patch.
Some of the items were too heavy for an
audience which had spent the afternoon at
Randwick. The programme was mercifully
short, however. In spite of encores, the
G. was able to take a stealthy glance at
his watch at 10 p.m. and see the finish an
inch or two ahead. The pageant was the
thing. May Gibbs, who designs gum-leaf
babies for decorative'trimming, had taken
our beautiful Australian colors to her
bosom. Exquisite Christmas bells walked
with gorgeous waratahs. Flannel flowers
ami infantine wattle blossoms tumbled up
the steps to rustle into the organ seats.
Some of the wattle blossoms were so stuffed
out with crinkled paper that they rolled
along like the yolks of eggs supported bv
thin green legs. All the States were repre-
sented. N. S. Wales’ sponsors were shep-
lu rdesses in pale blue with "fleecy tunics.
'The damsels who showed us what Queens-
land really means were horned, but not
hoofed, as cattle maidens. They nobly
turned warm tan complexions, under their
horned headgear, to the glacial stare of the
great electric lights. Proceeds go to a
Battalion Comforts Fund.
Steady processions of the Wade twin-;
and pretty Marjory Hinder inspected the
progress of the latter damsel’s trousseau
. during the past few weeks. The wedding
occurred at Ashfield. The groom, who has a
brand new stethoscope, is a son of Opposi-
tion-Leader Wade. Airs. Critcliley Hinder,
the heroine’s step-mother, was easily the
best looking of the matrons, and thought-
fully garbed herself iu light and festive
drapery—quite unlike the average mother
who at smart weddings is prone to shed
tears of joy under the brim of sable millin-
ery or muffle her smile in deep shades of
royal-mourning purple. AVadc junior will
practise in Molong.
The hassocks in St. Andrew’s Cathedral
have been dusted with extra vigor of late
for the knees of wedding guests. Most of
the ceremonies have been fringed with khaki.
Ralph Pain, second-best man at the knotting
of Alabel Lewis, daughter of late architect
Mortimer Lewis, was in the usual fighting
millinery, as he goes to the Front forthwith.
The groom was Edmund A. Harris. The
Charles Alackellars, Joe Carruthers and his
wife, the Lethbridge Kings and a swarm of
friends toasted the bride’s health at
farmer’s. The moon is silvering on the
Alountains.
Next month A’iolet Wickham, eldest
daughter of 11. T. Wickham, will say the
usual thing to A. .1. Swan. Grandpa was
that Captain Wickham. R.N.. who was

ago. The old gentleman’s portrait hangs

aquiline features have been passed on to

man is a Sydney accountant.

—happy times when the Young Person ex-

John Dallas Campbell, only son of the
late squatter Campbell, of Condobolin, met
Dorothy Alunro, of Longueville, and a cer-
tificated marrier the other day. The bridal
plumage was tipped with a faint pink blush
—supplied by the lining. The foam of
white on top was soft as “carded wool.” as
the poet said. Or it may have been a shop-
assistant. I forget.
Killara is again burning with patriotic
fever. This time it is for Allies’ Day.
Stealthj- parties, who usually water their
lawns o’ nights, now make a bee-line for a
local hall to prepare for the great event.
The show occurs on the evening of October
23. Airs. Duncan Preston. Mrs. John Lem-
mone and Airs. A'ldworth *are chiefly respon-

The Jim Gordons, of Manar. have been in
toAA’ii for the races. Airs. Jim, who used

From Orange came the genial Jim Dalton
to have a look at the Horse and bowl his

A portion of Newcastle defences has been
called Fort Wallace, in memory of the late
Colonel Bob Wallace. His death, after a
painful struggle in front of the Scythe,
will be regretted here, though he was better
known in Victoria. The late Commandant
of the Southern State had his wife and only
daughter with him at the end. They got
hack from England a short time ago.
Queen A'ictoria Club is going to have a
party on October 2(1 in a sporting effort to
bag some gifts for its stall on Allies’ Da} 7 .
Barrister Bert Norris, who went a-soldier-
ing to Egypt, has been kept there on busi-
ness that mixes the law with military dis-
cipline. He is a judge-advocate or some-
thing of the kind.
Doris Buckland. a dashing country girl
from woolly Pine Ridge, is now polishing
brass work in the Coast Hospital. This is
understood to be an introduction to nursing
in Egypt.
Dr. Lucy Gullett will go to the Nile
with Constance Carson under her wing.
They are both on the trail of the damaged
kangaroo. Constance hopes to find a
brother reported missing, and unless forcibly
l>ievented slie means to help with the. nurs-
ing.

Ims a Christmas gifts afternoon for the fight-

to <> p.m. a committee, ravenous for your

iMiss ( larke. a daughter of Melbourne's
chief Anglican shepherd, is amongst the race-

Sister Hester Mac Lean arrived from
Kgypf on Tuesday by train. She leaves bv
rhe Moeraki for Wellington on Thursday, and
is meantime with mother at Darling Point.
In Maoriland she will resume lier job of
Inspector of Hospitals. Sister Hester was
in charge of the first batch of 50 nurses sent
from Maoriland—was a fortnight and much
tiffed in London, thence went to Alexandria,
where there are two hospitals manned by
M.L. doctors. She was afterwards at Lady
Dudley’s Convalescent Hospital Cottage at
Alexandria. Sister Mac Lean was Matron
of the Women’s Hospital in Melbourne before
she took the M.L. billet —which, by the way,
is one of the fattest in our hemisphere for
women.
Dr. Fiaschi. the 70-odd-year young medical
light and vine-grower, who is working at the
Front, stopped work for just long enough to
welcome a baby daughter. The little stranger
arrived in Pharaoh’s land, and the question
is will they call her Cleopatra or Benjamina 7
Present Mrs. Fiaschi was a nurse and is the
doctor’s second wife.
If the wastage of male life goes on at the
present rate the births of daughters will
become so unpopular that mention of them
will be censored out of the birth column,
while the arrival of the man child will lie
exploited in headlines and with noise of
loud timbrels. Boy twins "brightened the
home” (as the Americans would say) of Dr.
and Mrs. Adams, of Bose Bay. last month.
The lady is one of the Knox girls, and she
married Dr. Adams when he was on the old
Powerful man-o’-war. .Airs. Kelso King
also achieved the valuable son lately. The
stork’s beak-luggage was deposited in Mel-
bourne.
A note from Alexandria :
Colonel Watson and bis pretty wife, a Hordern
girl. have been staying- here. Mrs. Sweet-
land is helping at the No. 19 General Hospi-
tal. Airs. Fiasc-hi. wife of the Colonel, is staying at
the Majestic. Major Clongston, R.E., has been
wounded. Ilis wife has been acting as matron at
one of the convalescent homes, but has now gone to
see after her husband.
Splendid young- It. J. A. Massie is lying wounded
in the Ras-el-tin hospital. Everyone who knows
Australia’s fine bowler hopes the wound will
not interfere with his swerve.” Lieutenant Jim
Osborne is at the same hospital. He has been shot
through the groin. His mother (Airs. Fred) and
his sister Valentine passed through Port Said on
their way to England only a few days before lie
was wounded. He is doing well. Lieutenant
Johnny Newmarch, of the A.F.A., is also at Ras-el-
tin. He was shot through the back. It was a
dangerous wound, but he is getting on well. ilis
mother, father (Dr. Newmarch) and brother are all
here, and all working hard.
The small toques with high walls of tulle
survive the rush of wide gossamer headgear.
But they are too reminiscent of the trim-
ming on a ham-bone to be extra-becoming
to elderly complexions. There is a newer
species of the toque which is a rich relation
of the Dutch bonnet. I saw a pretty girl
at Randwick who had her head swallowed
tip in one of these skin-tight coverings. The
wind couldn’t stir it, though it shook the
large silk side whiskers which were like
the rounded arms of a windmill.
Flounces can be borne with equanimity
if you remember that you can’t sit down on
• hem without crumpling up their mid-Vic-
torian beauty. But when they’re scalloped
from hem to waist the lady inside looks as
if she ought to wilt away on a, horsehair
soft with a. white antimacassar as a hack-
ground.
The Polish Belief Fund’s lion. sec.. .Miss
Czaykowska, remarks in a soft whisper that
the new depot is in Hamilton-street, City.
Cases of garments for the shivering Polish
infant are being rushed thence to their des-
tination.
Laurence Godfrey Smith is slill pegging
away in London. Ilis’frantic desire to per-
sonally meet the enemy was turned down for
lack of the physique that thrives in the
trenches. So he helps the Censor to un-
ravel foreign correspondence.
The Town Hall cellar, which performs
many useful purposes in war time, will be
a depot for the receipt of custom on Flower
Day. Don’t start, Arini —it’s only another
of them! It falls on October 15. Any kind
of produce will he welcomed by Mrs. Munro,
the lady behind the counter.
Yours affectionately,
VAN DORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, October 11, 1915.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Despite murder and sudden death, the
annual insanity began at Caulfield on Satur-
day with the Guineas. State-Governor
Stanley showed that it was a less official
occasion than usual by appearing in tweeds
with a gent, help or two but no womenfolk.
Mostly they kept away from the official
crate and went looking for winners as ter-
riers hunt rats. The women for the most
part were nearly worried to death over what
to wear. After a week of biting winds, the
weather had turned on a dusty north wind
and a hot sun, thereby putting un a joke on
the furry rich, who staggered about like
asses in the skins of bears and such. The
encumbrances were far too valuable to leave
about, and chauffeurs had mostly lost them-
selves before the fool-wearers discovered
that they were likely to perish of their
armor. But the ones who had taken a
sporting risk and backed summer were
almost as badly off with their fashionable
footwear, which is “just too sweet” when
you try it on at home, but more like Hades
when you try to walk in it on a hot day.
The Fenner girl, in very full skirts,
threatened to topple forward at any minute.
.Mrs. Waters was in bright blue foulard and
spots, with a saucy veil hanging from her
black sailor hat, yo ho! But the wife of one
of the Baillieux, in black taffetas beautifully
shirred, was really the smartest racegoer
visible. The Herbert Power dame was
arrayed in black glace, while the clever
Brennan lawyer-girl wore tricky full skirts
of white cloth. It was joyous to see fat,
middle-aged dames in full “stuff” skirts and
fat sailor hats. The slim young ones in
silk frills suggested lampshades. One slender
girl had her gathered skirt held round her
hips with a cord. She made me think of a
nice umbrella that was stolen from me years
ago by a cat with green eyes. Another
extraordinary vision was in paddy-green
glace elaborately flounced. A baffling veil
hid her face. Mrs. “Alawa” Buchanan
looked fully six feet tall in a layered out-
fit of dark grey. Actress Eugenie Duggan
had on the neatest oufit of navy blue and a
small, putty-colored hat like a half-baked
tart.
Young Atherstone Archer, a grandson of
the late Judge Gaunt, has had a yarn with
George V. It happened this way. Young
Archer went to the Dardanelles as a private
in Colonel Wanliss’s command, which was
part of the First Expedition. At the Front
he was badly wounded in the head and sent
to England. George V. came visiting at
Ilarefield Park Hospital one day. and said
something polite to the Australians there
about a distinguished Australian —Rear-
Admiral Ernest Gaunt, a son of the Judge.
Atherstone’s convalescence in the hospital and
his relationship to the Admiral were made
known to the Presence, and H.M. did the rest
while the reporters took a copious note of
the occurrence. Mrs. Oakley Archer, the lad’s
mother, has for many years been principal
of our Trinity College Hostel, where the
woman-’Varsity students stay. The soldier’s
sister is doing the science course at the Uni-
versity.
Baronet Clarke’s pretty daughter Aimee,
otherwise “Possie,” is about to pair off with
a young British officer by the name of
Gerard. The gossips say he matches his
bride-elect in good looks, wears the proper
I ang-the-expense air, and has a sporting
( fiance of wearing the family title some day
when all the wars are over. Father Rupert
will probably get away from his military job
to hand his girl over.
Osborne House, Geelong, which was our
first Naval College, has been turned into a
military hospital for returned wounded.
Latest news of its first commandant, Cap-
tain Chambers, is about his ship, the Rox-
burgh. being torpedoed “somewhere in the
North Sea,” and he being given a Dread-
nought instead. Meantime the tall and
beauteous Mrs. Bertram Chambers, so well
remembered for her remarkable frocking and
yellow head, anxiously awaits her lord in
London, where she is flatting in Cavendish-
street. Her small sister, Miss Bertrand,
who came to Australia on a short visit and
remained a year, has changed her name since
returning to England. She married Lieut.
Archibald Monk, one of the Naval College
staff, who now rocks in the cradle of the
North Sea.
The solemn boom of the “Dead March”
reminded Melbourne last Saturday that
(’olonel Wallace was going to his long rest.
The dead officer will be missed, for he was
full of tact and a gifted arbitrator. A
military “inquiry” in his hands became
rather a pleasant function, and the result was
generally satisfactory.
The Monty Langdon widow, whose only
son Leslie has been posted as missing since
the famous Gallipoli landing, has been taking
a course of mountain breezes at Braemar,
with daughter Dorothy Campbell, who was
on the sick list for a time.
Mrs. Fairclough, of Toorak, is another
mother who is waiting and hoping for news
of a missing son. Her handsome boy Bob
was given a commission on the field, only to
vanish a day or so later. Jack Tovell, a
Brighton boy and an embryo barrister, has
not been seen by his mates since that day at
Sari Bair.
Helen Sexton, M.D., who was among the
first flight of women doctors in Australia,
and has been given the management of a
hospital in France, wears the title “Major” !
Our Trinity College has the gentle warrior’s
name on its war-service roll.
Frank Derham, a son and junior partner
of Tom Derham, of the ancient Melbourne
law-firm, has. a job as Major in Lieut.-
Colonel’s Grimwade’s new field-artillery
command. Two young brothers of Mrs. Der-
ham, Colin and Harold Bowden, are helping
to keep the guns tidy in the same brigade.
Their dad is John Bowden, who for a time
wrestled with Conservatism on the commit-
tee of the V.R.C. Captain Norman Derham,
one of the military censors (groans), belongs
to the same totem as the artillery major.
Presbyterian-parson Frank Rolland, who
is just the shepherd for the job, has put on
the chaplain’s stars and gone to Gallipoli.
For some time previously he had been toiling
Noorat, a space in the Western District
vineyard which is mostly sheep and scenery
and squattages owned by the Black family.
Justice Higgins’s only son, Mervyn, now
at Gallipoli, has a blend in his veins which
should not fail him at a pinch. He is
descended from an Irish grand-dad, that long-
deceased Baptist-parson Higgins who founded
the Australian family; on the other side his
grandpa was a Scotchman, fine old Principal
Morrison, of the Geelong Grammar School’s
early days. Young Higgins is a lieutenant in
the war-scarred Bth Light Horse.
Norton Grimwade lost a 20-year-old
son in the recent fighting. The boy was the
second of three, and the other two are busy
in the field. The eldest, Fred, has just got
his aviator’s certificate in England, and
Lionel, the youngest, is a middy on one of
the North Sea giants.
Three Maori officers. Captain Dansey,
Lieutenant Hiroti and Lieut. Hetet, who
returned by the Don’t-ask-me, caused a mild
sensation in Melbourne during the few days
they were being shown round. Mrs. Manson
and other Maoriland women gave a tea-party
at the Mia Mia, and the Athenaeum Club
blew up with a dinner.
Maybe to square accounts, Angliss, M.L.C.,
has fitted up a white and red motor-ambu-
lance and handed it over to the Defence
people. Colonel Cuscaden, acting chief mili-
tary sawbones, said a few fervent words of
thanks.
Mrs. Waters is packing her clothes, her
children and the nurses in readiness for a
forward move to Egypt. The Colonel hus-
band has written the glad news that he is
to be for some time at or near the Canal,
and the whole family is to advance on
Cairo. Sister Mrs. Walsh is in London,
where her children are devouring chops at
tenpence each, and generally making them-
selves expensive. Therefore the Walsh
menage intends to migrate to Egypt also,
and junction with the Waterses.
There is going to be a big jump in the
recruiting figures after harvest. Half the
Mallee boys are just holding on till they get
the precious wheat in, and then they’ll see
about getting the Hun out. As for this
harvest, by the way, it is still too early to
talk of records. October is a critical month,
and thus far it has been exceptionally dry.
Miss Ethel Hart, matron of the Found-
ling Hospital at Richmond, is engaged to be
married to Claude Felstead, a one-time golf
champion. * Claude, who is a son of the
Felstead. is at present trying to grow wheat
and things in Westralia. His farming
partner has enlisted, and when the coming
harvest is over, Claude will also enlist —but
not before he marries his Ethel. The
romance has been short and sweet. Miss
Hart was surfing with sister Mrs. Percy
Cook at Peterboro when Claude came down
to the surf and saw the Junoesque girl, who
towers nearly six feet in her bathing sandals.
And he knew at once without telling that she
must be his little tootums for ever.
Vera, the Frank Madden daughter with
the dreamy smile, is booked for a wedding
with a blue and gold-laced naval officer on
the , by name of Johnston. The
romance is popular with both families.
The Sam Warnocks are in mourning for
the youngest of their trio of daughters. The
bereaved mother, for whom everyone is feel-
ing very sorry, is an elder sister of the good-
loojking Mrs. Henry Cornish.
Dr. Hope and his wife have flown home
to Australia after a long three years’ flight
round the world. Unlike most travellers
they first “did” Australia from one end to
another, and then went further afield.
When they had spurned the whole round
earth with their tyres, they exchanged their
faithful chariot for £2UO and sailed home.
Mrs. Hope is furnishing a flat while her
husband is sorting out his sheep on a
N.S.W. station. The Hopes’ last English
rest was taken at Folkestone, within hearing
of the big gnus in France. A passing Zep-
pelin dropped a bomb in the garden of a
neighboring clergyman, and they left
hurriedly amid a shower of flowers and
weeds for Australia and peace.
Christmas tobacco has been rolling in plen-
tifully for the soldiers. But there is a
scarcity of pipes to burn it in. Some eager
matrons lately -ailed a meeting with the
intimation, “Come and bring your pipe (as
a gift).” The little function progressed
nicely in the tea-and-talk method natural to
the Sassiety brand of worker until a she-duke
with an arrogant nose rose in her silk stock-
ings and yawned: “Ai suppose clay pipes
will do for them?” Some of these absurd
women cannot rid themselves of the hallu-
cination that in making presents to the
troops they are doling out charity to uni-
formed paupers.
Frank Hardyman (secretary) and Mrs.
Major James (chairwoman) of the Over-
seas Club want to send out a whole ship-
load of bottled vegetables to the Dardanelles
immediately. The trench-dwellers, it
appears, are being attacked by something
like the distressing complaint known to
Australian bushmen as “Barcoo Rot.” The
only remedy, it is alleged, is a vegetable diet,
and the Overseas women are throwing down
their knitting and collecting preserved vege-
tables as fast as they can. Pass the pickled
cabbage, please!
There will be some fun for Sassiety dar-
lings in Cup week, even if it has to be dis-
guised with Red Cross badges. Mrs. Harry
Emmerton gives an At Home on Monday
evening, November 1, at her South Yarra
place, in the midst of a f@te and cafe clian-
tant in aid of the Red Cross. Visitors must
have the usual invitation pasteboard (larg-
est size and gilt-edged), but they will also
pay seven-and-sixpence to get inside. Amy
Castles, sister Dolly and brother George have
all offered their voices, and John Amadio
and Harold Elvins will be among the others.
Now for Adelaide: —
The University conferred degrees the other day,
and most of the men recipients were in khaki. An
exception to the rule was Dr. Bevan, the Congrega-
tional cherub, who took his D.D. in America and
his IiU.B. in London in 1866 ; so he is more than
a promising boy.
Dr. Bollen, of Woodville, is off to the Front,
wearing the major’s crown. The Moldavia took with
her E. W. Hayward, who is bound for Egypt’s Red
Cross, and one of the Talbot Smith sons.
News is through that Lieut. M. A. Davies has been
killed in France. His mother was a daughter of the
late' Maurice Salom, a one-time legislator, and his
father is a brother of Major Karri Davies, of W.A.
Every second woman you meet has a billycan in
one hand and a collection of small parcels in the
other. And she invariably wants to know why the
first woman wasn't carrying another billy and
another set of parcels.
Artist Will Ashton has sold his picture, “The
Luxembourg Gardens,” to the trustees of the N.S.W.
Gallery. This is the eighth of his canvases to find
a place in an Australian national collection.
The Navy League has arranged a special fneet-
ing on Trafalgar Day (October 21) to celebrate the
work done by the Allied fleets since the outbreak of
the war. The triumph is worth celebrating, and his
Ex. will be on hand to help do it.
The Chamber of Manufactures is enthusiastic about
the Minister of Industries’ scheme for establishing a
special department to afford facilities for research
work —that is, finding out how the enemy did it.
The idea is that a properly-equipped laboratory
should be set up, under the direction of a chemist,
and that experiments—which are often nothing more
than bad smells —be made with great care. It is
one of those noble, necessary, dull subjects in which
you probably .are not interested, so I won’t tell you
any more about it.
Brewster Jones’s symphony concert in aid of the
Red Cross Fund at the Town Hall on Thursday even-
ing deserved a better attendance than it got. But
what can you do! The Law won’t allow us enthusi-
asts to spring at wayfarers from behind and rush
them into the vacant chairs by the scruff of the neck.
The National Service meeting in the Town Hall
might have been better attended, and might have
been the text for more enthusiastic speeches. The
same thing might be said of every function bar the
Last Judgment. But that will, I understand, be
Compulsory Service.
Some- time ago, when Melbourne thought
the war would smash the Cup of 1915, the
Brighton matrons began to scheme a big
carnival bazaar for race-time. Now the Cup
is going to run over as usual, and the pro-
ject has got on their nerves. It begins on
Derby Day, and must struggle along as best
it can while all the State is out at Fleming-
ton. If you’re not going racing, attend it,
and you’ll probably see one prominent horse-
owner’s wife wringing her gloved hands.
A well-known sheep-farmer, whose wife
and daughters are working their fingers to
the bone over Red Cross sewing, is giving
his front teeth to the Cause. He takes
possession of the hotel bar in his township,
and when the thirsty visitor drifts in he
lures the stranger into a bet —he (the sheep-
man) will back himself to bite a tliripny bit
in halves. Practice has made the hero per-
fect, and he wins every time. I suppose
it’s magnificent and all that sore of thing,
but it makes my teeth ache to think of it.
It is killing, the way the Sassiety charity-
queen works her wonders to perform. A
bunch of matrons are organising a Patriotic
Dinner Party (hear, hear!), which is to
happen at the Savoy just after the Caul-
field Cup. The original idea of asking only
seven-and-sixpence a head has been squashed
flat and the feeding price fixed at a guinea.
Of course the dinner will have to be good,
and the caterers can’t afford to work for
nothing. But, never mind ! No doubt the
Red Cross will get something out’ of it. If
the guns fail us we will chew our way to
victory with serviettes tucked under ’ our
chins. How it must comfort the warrior
wounded to think “Somewhere at home Nice
People are over-eating for me!”
Veronica Ronald from Hay, Caused a
social sensation they say—No, no, no! I
forgot that it wasn’t a Limerick competi-
tion ! Anyhow, Veronica alighted at Mel-
bourne as a prelude to a long holiday, and
put up at the Alexandra Club in such glit-
tering style that this war-stricken city
dropped its collection box and cheered till
its hat was crooked. Visions like Veronica,
as she appears when in full sail, are very
scarce these times, and we felt much better
for sighting her.
. “Block” promenaders on Saturday morn-
mg w/re treated to an inspiring sight when
a well-known and dressy St. Kilda matron
burst’ into view with a live and uncomfort-
able-looking native bear, decorated with a
large pink bow, in her arms. The crowd
surged round the lady as though she were a
dogfight or a damaged motor-car, but she
contrived to furnish an imitation of a per-
son wondering in a composed sort of way
what all the bother is about.
Partly owing to the inclusion of over-ripe
articles of food, and partly for another
reason, workers have been engaged
unpacking and repacking the 20.0(1(1
billies-for-soldiers at the Alexandra Club.
Unchained cheese, fruit, portions of ham,
cooked chickens and similar highly explo-
sive goods were stowed in some of the early
billies —possibly under the impression that
they were to go only as far as Broad-
meadows. A complete examination followed
as a matter of course.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

MRS. GRUNDY IN TOPHET.
“D.D.” : In Colac district (Vic.) I struck
a case of a lad of 14 who, it was given as gospel,
milked 18 cows in a non-stop run of an hour and a
half. The cows were not picked, but taken as they
were bailed up. If anyone can put me on to his
better I’ll take the wonder on forthwith.
"Bad Australian” (from S. America) :
Wandering over the roof of the world in search of a
location for a railroad from Nowhere to the Next
■id foi the purpose of exploiting nothing more
tangible than the ever-confiding shareholder, I struck
a one grave under the shadow of a beetling crag.
(Hie crags don’t beetle much in Bolivia, as a
general thing, and that’s why I make a point of
mentioning the circumstance.) The last resting-
place was marked by a fantastic cross painted or
daubed in red ochre on the side of the crag Th it
and nothing more. My Quichua boys said the local
equivalent to dunno when I questioned them, but
they thought it was the grave of one of (he many
prospectors who had perished in that arid wilder-
ness I pushed on to a little Indian village where
the hospitable padre, a full-blooded Quichua, put
me up for the night, and after a dinner of roast
llama-meat and a rather fishy omelette of flamingo
eggs and a bottle of vino moscatel, and when we
had lighted our cigarillos of native tobacco rolled
m maize husks, I expressed my sympathy for the
luckless prospector who lay under the crag The
padre put me right. “No, Sefior, you are entirely
wrong. Ihe grave you saw was that of a bad man,
a bandido a hunter of the vicuna and the chin-
chilla, and he died from a surfeit of vicuna heads,
and Maria Motive! cursing God and the Holy
Saints, for which may he receive pardon!” The
vicuna is a cross between a fallow deer and a
t wart camel, with a tremendous leaning towards the
eei. Hie pelt is much prized for its fine silky
An t T he , head is regarded as a great delicacy
among the Indians of the Higher Andes. Hence the
untimely end of the man who had more of them in
his possession than was healthj'.

fL At Motion near Gennanton
* lle y e ' dl two notorious men were
shearing as pen-mates. One was
the Derwent Nugget; the other
Power, the bushranger. Both had
served long terms of imprison-
ment. The Nugget’s little trouble
was the murder of a picker-up
and tar-boy who had goaded him
beyond control. The Nugget
plunged the sheep-shears into
the youngster’s side. Power had
only been out a couple of years
after doing a score for highway
robbery. He was the protege of
two wealthy maiden ladies in
Wangaratta (Vic.), and supple-
mented their bounty by a little
shearing. When he would talk of
his exploits he always maintained
that Ned Kelly put his (Power’s)
haunts away to the police, and
so enabled them to capture him
while sleeping. His career was
picturesque. He never shot any-
one even in circumstances in
which others probably would have
been tempted to do it.

him some stinging-tree leaves. Then all he will

“Coot”: Several country districts in Vic-
toria are complaining of the increase of wombats,
and the Assembly spent some hours recently dis-
cussing whether the animals would take poison'. Mr.
McKenzie asserted that wombats would not take
poison, whereupon the officers of the Lands Depart-
ment retorted that they took it readily. In 1913
near Mudgee (N.S.W.) the wombats became such a
pest that the Pastures Protection Board offered 10s.
a head on the assumption that they were difficult
to kill or capture. In a few weeks the amazed
Board had 1300 claims sent in, and had to cancel
the bonus offer. Recently, at Mount Feathertop
(\ic.), my horse was stopped by a wombat stretched
across the track. Worn, wouldn’t move, either,
until I got off and prodded him with a stick.
Is it specimens of Australians
you re after ? Here are four that met casually to
dunk to the follow-the-King movement in a Gunne-
dah (N.S.W.) church the other day:

“Leo Leal-” : I don’t know how ’roos fared
ioi moisture in Westralian droughts, but I can
give some first-hand information re birds in water-
less country. In 1902 there was about as much water
in the far N.W. Victorian mallee as can be seen on
the face, of the moon. Yet small birds lived
through it in apparently normal numbers. Emus
and ’roos made for the Murray or down south.
Some birds can, I knowq do without water as long
as the dews are heavy, but some of the honey-eaters
and others are never found far from a bird pub.
Noticing that for several evenings they were flying
in a particular direction, I followed, anti came to a
bull-mallee with a hole in a projecting limb, around
which dozens of honey-eaters, caterpillar-eaters,
wood-swallows and other small fry were flying.
Investigation proved that the tree was hollow-, with
the lower end plugged up with mud. In this natural
reservoir water had collected during the rains, and
the birds used it as required. As evaporation was
negligible and leakage unknown, the birds had an
assured supply. Since then I’ve heard of other
similar cases. Moral for the thirst-stricken bloke
in the back country—keep one eye on the birds.
Ino ’: Forty-five years ago quanclong
trees were plentiful near Eugowra (N.S.W.). The
largest fruit that I ever saw east of Forbes would
be about as big as a small walnut, the stones
inside the fruit being' about the size of marbles.
Inside the stone is a kernel, white, solid and to the
taste much like raw mutton suet. The fruit that
grew down the Lachlan was much larger and better
in every way. It made good pies and fair jam,
and was quite a treat as a fruit where fruit was a
rarity at that time. I think the tree is about ex-

THE BIRTHDAY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
" <ll I remember when we first
Resolved to mark Brown’s natal day
By drinks, wherein his splendid thirst
Would have full scope to frisk and play,
Like a fat mermaid in the surf
Before the bold sun comes that way.
We fixed a list of pleasures sweet,
filled with delight and sopping wet,
So that in afterdays we’d meet
And look back with a mild regret
On this delightful night of ours,
And smile and sigh but ne'er forget.
Biown when in drink's a gentleman,
But when he’s sober he's a boi;e ;
So we arranged a splendid plan—
We’d start the jag at half-past-four,
1 hat when fond night embraced the sky
Brown's meaner self would nag no more!
We drank his health in whisky neat,
And he drank ours in bottled beer ;
Great, envious crowds stopped in the street
To see us drink and hear us cheer.
A cabman with a monstrous nose
Kept driving us from There to Here.
Shocked by the sound of falling beers
The sun blushed red and sank from si grit.
Said Jones with glee: “The mean cow fears
That if he stays we’ll make him tight, "
And there would be an awful row
If the old bloke stayed out all night.”
AVe let him go. We didn't mind.
If he was such a flaming nark,
Well, blast him! He would dam’ soon find
How much we all preferred the dark. j
Smith struck a match to take his place
Oh I we were having such a lark !
So we went on from hour to hour
Like large, intoxicated bees,
Flitting about from flower to flower,
A-drinking stout and eating cheese,
And scoffing steak in Dago joints,
With waitresses upon our knees!
Brown thanked us all in Market-street;
We shook his hand in Martin-place,
Where Jones was very indiscreet—■
He wept to see the cabman’s face.
(His nose annoyed him, so he said—
It seemed a glaring red disgrace.)
Then we beheld a fearful thing:
Ihe earth and skv went reeling round ;
Each paving-stove began to sing,
And yelled aloud without a sound;
Great buildings all about us leapt,
ihe cab-horse left us with a bound,
The moon was walking hand in hand,
The stars were playing twos-and-threes*
And over all the silent land
A brown tide rose by slow degrees—
It flooded out the oyster shops,
And lapped a|pund the policemen's knees.
It rose and rose and rose apace.
AVe greeted it with shouts of glee,
And showered prawn-shells on its face.
And then, too late, we turned to flee—-
It rose, and buried all the world,
And left us drowned beneath the sea.
Until in a tumult of hiccoughs and laughter,
It hurled us into the Morning After.
N.6.W.
J. J. S.

THE illustration shows in miniature the finish of the remarkable Rawson-
Moody All-Steel Fence. It is the only first-class fence in the world to-day.
Give your attention to the straining-posts. They require a depth of only
two feet. You do hot require to sink holes to considerable distances. The
straining posts are the strongest in the world to-day. They are made of high-grade
steel. See the patent slots, which allow the wires to strain through either way,
automatically helping in the self-locking movement which is a chief feature of the
Rawson-Moody fence. Take notice of the anchor-like stays and feet, which take
an octopus-like grip of the ground, giving tremendous stability and strength. The
standards and droppers, which can be fitted to a five-wire fence in as many seconds,
are also a wonderful patent. The wire-straining ratchets, another Rawson-Moody
patent, is a clever feature of the fence. One goes to each wire, and by its use
ties, staples, clips and wedges are done away with. The weight of this fence is
only three tons to the mile, as compared with 12 to lb tons of other fences. A
man and a boy can erect a quarter of a mile a day. Above all, the fence gives a
finely-finished appearance. A few chains enclosing the approaches to your home-
stead, and enclosing the house paddocks, will make you proud-of your property.
This can be done at small cost and little labor. Write for particulars of this
all-steel fence. The straining posts, standards and droppers, which are sold
separately, are made so that any good standard fencing-wire may be used.

"Pick : Certain breeds of monkey-vine
that festoon with fancy arches the fairy- bowers of
jungle-land are water-bags which in drought have
furnished liquid for many a billycan. The experi-
enced bushie with a thirst seeks a rough-barked
vine, and at high-reach mark makes an incision
in the central pipe of the vegetable hawser. lie
then severs the vine low and holds the William
beneath. Air pressure does the rest. The wattr
that trickles abundantly forth, if the right vine has
been selected, has a somewhat resinous tang, but
tile tea brewed from it is preferable to a can of
emptiness at “joe” time.
Lillybungev : Billjim, the jumbuob
baiber, has a vocabulary of his own, which is
ia t hoi mystifying to a “chummie” in shearing sheds.
hen applying for employment he invariably in-
quires if he can get “a pen,” “a stand” or “a cut.”
In a shed where the harboring is done by machinery
he always alludes to his handpiece as “a bog-eye.”
This is from its likeness to the lizard of that name,
a thick, dumpy fellow six or eight inches long,
with a blunt head and tail and big scales like a
crocodile’s down his back. The combs and cutters
aie invariably “the tod-,” and when comb and
cutter are screwed on the handpiece Billjim reckons
he has “loaded-up his bog-eye.” The old-timer who
only barbers with the shears is “queer” or “cranky,”
and his implements of destruction are “the blades”
of “the tongs.” The unfortunate quadrupeds he gets
his bit of dough for “peeling” arc “the woolics”
when he’s polite; when he’s not polite it would take
a column to say what he says they are.
_ * .. * *
Dinku m : The most elaborate— at any
rate, the noisiest—funeral ceremonies held by the
extinct Wirrulumba abos. were those following the
death of a dingo. They regarded that animal with
the most singular superstition, looking upon it as
one of their ancestors and in some way associated
with their origin and destiny—a belief, I think,
unknown elsewhere on the Australian continent. The
death of the dingo was the occasion for universal
mourning, and the Icidjee (wake) held over a chief
was nothing in comparison with the noisy send-off' a
wild-dog got.
Put O Riot”: Somebody or other asks
(B. 9 9/'l5) for something to block a dog from
eating eggs. Dose an egg or two with cayenne
pepper. Pierce the fruit as for blowing, half blow,
and season to taste. If the mongrel lives, he is
permanently cured. If he dies, he is cured still
more permanently. The only other remedy I know
is wire-netting*. Not for the dog, for the eggs. For
fear of the eggs becoming morbid through loneliness,
enclose the hens also. I presume that What’s-his
name wishes to act in advance of the egg-laying,
so if lie nets the hens he's right.
“Mulga’*: There’s one all-round fine Aus-
tralian tree that is overdue for'mention, and that is
the heefwood of Bananaland. When seasoned it has
no equal for lightness and durability, and when
worked up the grain and polish of it are equal to
anything the ■ sun smiles on. I know of several
horses that are being kept alive on the leaves of it
during the present drought, and it does not bind
them up like mulga, wilga or belah.' Bullockies in
Queensland will have nothing else but beefwood for
yokes if it is obtainable. And no wonder: for one
ox-pilot near the Balonne has had a set of beef-
wood yokes in continual use for over 20 years.

BAGMAN : “Prime Minister bin come this way next week, Billy. Big pfeller boss, eh?”
BILLY: "I dunno — I bin king all about this place.”
“E..T.G.”: Some time back I mentioned
that the so-called Afghan had a playful habit of
sewing dead goatskins on to live camel skins.
" liar coo” (B. 19/S/’l5) scoffed at me—which shows
what a fat lot he knows. Just 10 days after his
misinformation appeared I happened to be in
Duchess (Q.). A team of 4 0 camels, including
eight babies, was on its way to be loaded with copper
ore. The mother of one of the infants was lame.
It also had a raw wound on each side of the back-
bone. Now, it would have been easy to strap a
goat-skin over these wounds, but Abdul believed in
making sure the baggage on top could not slip. So
lie had the pieces of goat-skin sewn on. I repeat, in
“Barcoo’s” car, the goat-skins were sewn on. About
a million flies were getting drunk on a little blood
that trickled from underneath. Finally, as a
guarantee of good faith, if “Barcoo” will forward
his address, I’ll pass it on to the first Afghan I see
looking for an animal to skin.
“Pick”: I can enlighten “F.11.R.” (B.
16/9/’l5) re bark-stripping. The long, stringy tree
he found peeled to the branches had been handled
by a blackfellow. Binghi is an expert at the game.
With a tomahawk he rings his first sheet of bark
top and bottom; then using the upper ring for
toe-hold he rings again at arms-length overhead.
This manoeuvre he repeats until as far aloft as he
wishes to strip. He then shoves off the sheets
successively as he descends. The marvel about the
performance is the extraordinary length of time he
can support himself with his toes.
* * * *
“Mulga”: I’ll nominate the ironwood tree
of Western Queensland to be the hardest cutting,
the heaviest and most useless timber in the bush,
lhe axe that has stood the test of an old man iron-
wood should be sent to the Bushwhackers’ Museum
as a curiosity’'. The wood is too heavy to be used
for rails, while to put it up for posts would be
issuing an invitation to all the white ants in the
district to come to lunch. If it had an asbestos
covering it could not be harder to ignite, but it
has this to be said of it—once well alight it burns
with tl\e fierceness of seven furies.
* * * *
“Mulga”: What’s the highest tally of
posts known to have been put up by any wild
colonial boy with bar and shovel, or with shovel
alone, in a day? I heard of a toiler the other side
of Sunset who had put up 400 in spinifex country,
and was still going strong when the shovel caught
alight. But, disqualifying him for negligence, I’ll
give a start with 217 scored by “Bluey” Watson
in mulga country on Wild Horn Plains in the
Bollon district. He did that in a bush-whacker’s
day—from crowup to sundown.
“Mulga’’: On the sandy plains out Far
West (N.S.W.) there is a weed which Black Bro-
ther calls “pitchery,” and which he uses as a
substitute for opium—or “mooki,” as he calls it.
He claims that it has all the attributes of the
poppy drug, and I certainly know that after a
week's chewing of pitchery the swab is just about
as useful for work as three-eighths of a corpse.
Lake George, which faded off the map of
X S. Males 20 years ago, is putting in a reappear-
ance. Twenty-five .years back it was a shallow
puddle about 25 miles long and eight broad, teeming
with fish and game. Then the inflow failed to make
up for the loss through evaporation and soakage, and
the area grew less and loss. While this was taking
place great hauls of fish were made; they only had
to bo scooped up at last, so closely were they packed
in the shrinking - waterholes. Finally, as the mois-
ture showed no signs of returning-, the Lands Depart-
ment leased the basin, which became a fine pastur-
age. Now the water is spreading rapidly, and the
Fisheries Department is thinking of getting to work
to restock the pool. After the previous holiday it
was nature that did the restocking, but it was a bit
of a dawdler.
* * * *
“Tiger ’: Can any Abo. tell me a profes-
sion with more aliases than the swagman's? Here
are a few: “Waltzin’ Matilda,” “paddin’ the hoof,”
“travellin’,” “humpin’ the bluey,” “on the wallaby,”
“paddlin’ the canoe,” “on the track,” “chasin’ the
sunset, “followin’ the luck,” “carryin’ the swag.”
“on the camel-track,” “huntin’ the mirage,” “hoof-
in’ it,” “doin’ the circuit” (this of the blokes who
turn up regularly every three months or so), “cart-
in’ the sausage-roll,” “humpin’ the plunder,”
“trampin’,” “lookin’ for work” (this bloke is often
clever at avoiding it), “leavin' the dead hopes,”
playin’ a lone hand,” “followin’ the sheds.” Onlv
met one of the profesh who added the final “g” to
his words, and he declared his sister was wife of
the Earl of Beauchamp: “Ai am transpoarting mai
dunnaige across the fields looking for a—er —suitable
resting-place.” lie found it, too —or two years of
it, anyhow.
* * * *
“Lou” : A neat little sbrub of the West
Queensland downs country is one the niggers call
the emu apple-tree. It is a small affair, about 4ft.
tall, with a clean stem and a small mushroom-
shaped tuft of dark green foliage. It is evergreen,
and in summer bears a pink-and-white-slcinned berry’
about the size of a large cherry, the flesh white.
I’ve seen the “flatnoses” eating this when very
thirsty. I sampled it myself once; it tasted like
quinine.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Over the mountain and valley and plain,
Warmed by the sunshine or soaked by the rain,
Moving on slowly by dark or by day,
Some dressed in black, but most of 'em "ray,
Heels in the air, with their heads hanging down,
Our friends the wild rabbits are going to town.
Cursed in the past as a virtueless pest,
Hailed now by many as meat of the best;
trapped in their wide-spreading country domain,
Dragged to the city by hurrying train ;
Heels in the air, with their heads hanging down,
Our friends the wild rabbits arc going to town.
Looked at with scorn by the trappers outback,
Hailed as a blessing by many who lack
t ash to buy scarcer and higher-priced meat,
Off to be hawked through each alley and street;
Heels in the air, with their heads hanging down,
Our friends the wild rabbits are going to town.
Taken by science and stiffened with cold,
Down in the liner, close packed in her hold,
Leaving the land where they ate tip the grass.
Out through the Heads in their thousands they pass.
Heels in the air, with their heads hanging down,
Our friends the wild rabbits are going to town.
Over the seas to a far-away shore,
Millions may come, but they'll want many more ;
Passed by inspectors, and branded as good,
Who there despises friend Bunny as food! •
lleels in the air. with their heads hanging down.
Our friends the wild rabbits are welcomed in town.
And off to the trenches, where bay'not and gun
fence oft the shocked world from the murderous
Hun.
To help in a humble but practical way
The hungry brown soldiers who strive in the fray—
Hats oft when you see, with their heads hanging down,
Our friends the wild rabbits a-going to town!
