Miss May Matthews, a pleasant, soft-
voiced Labor organiser who talks politics
with a straight hat, is one of the four
women who intend to have a try for the
three Senate seats that will need filling in
N. S. Wales shortly. In addition to the
intrepid four, 30 Labor men have nomi-
nated ; and David Watson, who is one of
the three retiring senators, will probably be
available again ; so it’s a long way to Tip-
perary and an uncompleted journey for
most of the starters. Miss Matthews is a
member of the central executive, and has
been doing organising work for the Federal
find State parties for about nine years—
wljich is a fairly long apprenticeship. Once
at Molong, back in 1910, she acted as
chairlady for Attorney-General Hughes. She
has only once been asked to leave a hall,
aqd that was for contradicting a whirling
Liberal sister who ventured to tell a hand-
fed audience that the Labor party was
bursting to give lunatics votes! It wasn't
worth while staying in a meeting like that,
anyhow.
“Trot” on a neglected industry :
AVhy is the writing of bellowdramas not a more
flourishing Australian industry ? Look at the good
stuff going to waste every day ! Here is a case re-
ported in the newspapers of a man so distracted by
receiving one of Holman’s “letters to young men”
about the duty of killing the Turk that he imme-
diately killed himself. There is a theme for a really
Australian drama, even the title ready-made—“ Dri-
ving a Youth to Destruction.”
Or, if not for a drama, consider the possibilities of
the thing as a movie! Why, there must be at least
a fiver in it.
Act I. : Shirker, aged 67, preparing to go to work.
Shirks all day in a mine with a pick. Home-coming.
Reception by truculent landlady who places before
him a roast turkey and a tray of mince pies, telling
him that if he is not satisfied with that he had
better go to the Front. Distended shirker goes to
mantelpiece, and is in the act of lighting a cigar
when he sees a letter addressed to him. Opens it,
reads —and, striking his forehead with a gesture of
despair, hurriedly swallows a sob and rushes from the
house.
Act II.: Holman composing letters to young men.
Enter attendant with telegram. This announces that
Scaddan refuses to lend any more money. Holman,
seized by anguish, writes seven more letters to young
men asking them to lend .the Government 2s. 6d.
Enter Secretary with message from shirker asking
whether in lieu of going to the Front he could not
get' his expenses paid as an advocate of prompt
enlistment by the inhabitants of Liberal constitu-
encies? Holman looks hurriedly in the cash-box,
whispers to himself: “My God, has it come to this
that I must refuse to help the Empire!” and sinks
into his chair with a loud, mocking laugh of victory.
Act III.: Grey-haired shirker smoking cigar de-
scends from a motor-car at the Treasury buildings,
and forcing Holman’s. door with his pick encounters
the Premier. Lays before him the letter to a young
man. Holman mistakes him for the serang of the
Norton Griffiths combine coming with an agreement
to put up barbed-wire entanglements along the banks
of the Murray to prevent the escape of foodstuffs.
Finding his mistake lie (ells the man that discretion
is the better part of valor, and that he will submit
the matter to caucus. Shirker refuses to be repulsed,
and declares that if he enlists he will be breaking
faith with his union, which had arranged to go on
strike. Holman says that alters the situation en-
tirely. The visitor, folding himself in his dust-coat,
murmurs, “Et tu Brutus,” and falls on his pick.
Final scene: Holman explaining to the reporters
that the incident was quite uncalled for, and nothing
further need be said about it.
Why, the thing would run for months and months.
Tuft”: He had been drinking in the bar;
and that is what the law says a bar is for.
He had, perhaps, drunk too much ; but his
mate was helping him along. His mate was
in khaki; so wws he. He was rather argu-
mentative. The John Hop whose job it was
to arrest drunks was waiting at the most
likely place to capture them —outside the
place where the material for getting drunk
on was sold. There was a brief argument
about moving on, and then there was a
prismatic blending of dark blue with khaki,
out of which emerged a big blue Hop hold-
ing a khaki bantam. And the procession
moved off. The crowd lingered, feeling that
something had gone wrong. Apparently it
never occurred to the J. Hop to arrest'the
hotel. It hasn’t occurred to the Caucus,
either.
One of the first acts of Tory politicians
if ever they attain power again should be
to abolish figures. Joe Cook’s party called
the maternity bonus an insult to mother-
hood, and said that it would be resented by
right-thinking women; yet in 1914 . there
were 137,983 births in the Commonwealth,
and 139,159 bonuses paid. The difference
represents the rush of women who wanted
to be insulted even before they committed
a live offence.
William Hutchinson, M.L.A. for Borung
(Vic.), who has lately considered it neces-
sary to spend some time explaining his
administration of the Department of Agri-
culture, is a bulky Rechabite who com-
. menced his farm-expert’s career as a watch-
maker at Warracknabeal, in the Wimmera
district. He kept the old shop going for
some time after Borung sent him to Parlia-
ment, but in the end sold out and became a
politician entirely. Hutchinson, senior, was
a highly-respected bookseller at Warrackna-
beal, and brother Jack, who owns a profit-
able general-store in the same town, is a
lieutenant-colonel at the Front. 11. V.
McKay (the harvester man) and Minister
Hutchinson are cousins, through their
descent from the squatting Shaw family, of
Victoria and N. S. Wales.
Arthur Bourchier, of the London Garrick,
announces that his next play will be one
by William Archer, the ex-Queenslander who
is better known as dramatic critic than as
playwright. In his book on “Play-Making”
Archer modestly asks: “How comes it that
so many people, and I among the number,
who could not write a play to save their
lives, are eager to tell others how to do it?”
Rut that is a little hit of spoofifig self-depre-
ciation. Archer was only 25 and fresh from
rounding up cattle on his father's station
near Mount Morgan when he went to Lon-
don with the manuscript of “The Rush-
rangers” in his trunks. It was produced
on Easter Monday, 1881, at a long-popular
East End theatre, the Grecian, which was
soon afterwards acquired by General Rooth
and converted into a Salv’army citadel. A
melodramatically sensational piece, largely
built on the doings of Ned Kelly, it suited
the tastes of the East Enders and enjoyed
a fairly good run. One of its incidents was
the abduction of a squatter’s wife by an old
lover turned bushranger. Archer did not
turn out any more Australian plays, but he
translated the works of Ibsen and other
foreign dramatists into English. He got
himself called to the English Rar, but never
handled briefs, lie started dramatic criti-
cism on the Figaro, hut soon transferred to
the World, in whose columns he reviewed
the London playhouses for many years,
becoming second only to Clement Scott in
power and influence. Under the title of
“The Dramatic Year,” there are several
annual volumes of his critiques and impres-
sions. _ Latterly he has been bitten by the
phonetic craze, and as secretary of the
London Simplified Spelin Sosieti has been
trying to get people to write as they talk
and drop all unnecessary letters in the
words they use.
Listen to the thud of the Rrummagem joss
as its nose strikes the floor:
They must put their fiscal theories aside and look
at the condition of foreign exchange. They must dis-
courage imports, and have strict regard for the
necessity of reducing consumption thereof.
It is the Kritish Chancellor of the Ex-
chequer who is talking—talking not lightly
(if he ever does talk that way), but in a
deeply-considered Rudget speech. There
seems no help for it. We shall hate to lose
him; but we shall have to send Rruce
Horatius Smith to keep the Freetrade
bridge. And he will have to hurry or he
won’t be able to find even the necessary two
to stand beside him.
“Jean Sibi” : Finlayson, the dour,
courageous “anti” of the House of Reps.,
cherishes a great admiration for Harry
Lauder. In fact, the politician’s nearest
approach to a misspent life is the singing
o_f Lauder’s more rollicking ditties to select
circles of friends. He has a warm, round
bai’itone of unusual quality, and when he
lifted it up lately in a “Wee Deocli an’
Doris” at a Parliamentary dinner, the song
sounded none the less jolly because Finlay-
son would inevitably interpret its message
in terms of cocoa or tea. The songster,
by the way, has a deadly rival in artist
David Low, who will break out in “She’s
Ma. Daisy” or “Tobermory” on great provo-
cation. Low, it is darkly rumored, takes
his warbling verra seriously, and diets freely
on parritch and haggis to get the “local
color” into his voice.
A lady with a past blow into Port Jack-
son one day lately. This was the old French
barque Jean Bart. In 1912 she was wrecked
near Adelaide, and was sold with her wheat
cargo for a song. She was furbished up and
sold again to a German firm for 20 times the
wreck price. The insult of the squarehead
flag was wiped out in 1914 by a British
cruiser, which captured her off the South
African coast. Condemned by the Prize
Court, she was purchased by a British buyer
and re-cliristened Tridonia.
“Gus Gosh” proposes that it be made a
contract job :
The dreadful frequency with which Premiers do
not earn their salaries is becoming monotonous.
Why not introduce the payment-by-results principle
into public life? Take, for instance, the ease of
Holman, who engaged to carry into effect a certain
platform drawn up by the people who put him in
the way of a pay envelope. One of his jobs was to
see that the superfluous and extravagant Upper
House and State Governor were wiped out. Under
the proposal a percentage of his screw would he
docked for the omission to attend to these car-
buncles. It would of course be necessary for Pre-
miers, like other contractors, in submitting their
tenders to specify the time in which the job would
be done. Thus after the first month or so of office
(during which full screw would be paid) it would
be quite easy to determine what the Ministers should
forfeit as penalties for non-fulfilment. ■
“S.E.”: Pulled down at Christchurch
(M.L.) recently, the old Gaiety Theatre,
the first place of entertainment in the flat
place. It was erected in 1862, and for many
years has served as quarters for the waiters
and people connected with a neighboring
pub. But it saw some great times in its
youth. In March, 1871, it housed William
Hoskins and Florence Colville, who pro-
duced “The School for Scandal,” a comedi-
etta “A Morning Call,” and an extravaganza
“Nothing to Wear,” the last written by
Charles Matthews and presented to Hoskins.
Bland Holt’s first appearance in Christ-
church was at the Gaiety, on Sept. 1, ’79,
when he appeared with the G. D. Chaplin-
Rose Osborne company, and was seen as the
first Gravedigger in “Hamlet.”
The population of N. S. Wales at the
1911 census was 1,646,734. Of these 725,000
were packed into Sydney. The area of the
State is 198,054,420 acres; the number of
persons engaged in agricultural, pastoral
and other primary-producing occupations
was 204,014. These figures make it easy
to understand how great is the difficulty of
finding land for returned soldiers to settle
on. The place is getting absolutely con-
gested. Apart from holdings of from one
to 500 acres, the alienated land, amounting
to 14,000,000 acres, has actually been
divided up into 17,000 holdings. There
won’t be room to breathe directly.

Look to Your Teeth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort, and good
health, artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible b.\
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.

V\ estralia, whose mortality experience
among miners has become so shocking, is to
ha\ e workmen inspectors of mines at last—
"check inspectors.” as they are called in
VS. Wales and elsewhere. It would have
had them two years ago, when the last
amending Bill was before Parliament, had
not Labor Minister Collier rubbed the Cham-
ber of Mines .up the wrong way. and asked
for abolition of contract, reduction in heights
of stopes and a lot of other very debatable
things. Now. as a result of friendly con-
ferences between Collier and the mineowners’
1 epresentatives, the Rill is to be amended
principally in the direction of taking away
from the proposed inspectors the right to
initiate prosecutions, though, in order to
bluff extremists on both sides, a sham fight
is being put up in Parliament over other
features of the measure.

IN TOPIIET.
New South Wales is at present making careful
inquiries, both in London and America, as to the
possibility of securing loan money. —News item.
PREMIER HOLMAN: “ Excuse me, old chap,
any chance of securing a loan?”
The futility of Jokoolt as Leader of the
Opposition is becoming apparent even to
former supporters of the waspish little man,
and an attempt will be made to drop him as
gently as possible before the Referenda cam-
paign. In obtaining the leadership Cook
beat Forrest by a narrow margin, and the
supporters of Bigjohn reckon that his forth-
coming “jubilee” should be celebrated by
giving him the captaincy. Pushful Willy
Watt is also mentioned as a possible leader,
and also Irvine. But Irvine is not favored.
The Icecream, one of the l-est Nationalists
of the whole Parliamentary bunch, has more
than once exposed the fraudulency of the
politics of his own party, and is therefore
not “safe.”
The negotiations between the political
parties in Tas. for keeping in out of the wet
(otherwise termed “co-operating during the
period of the war”) have ended in the Labor
Government introducing a Bill to extend
the life of the present Assembly till March
31: 191”, thus putting off the evil day for
over .12 months. The Liberal Opposition
has appointed a committee of three who are
to sit with the Ministry in Cabinet and dis-
cuss and deliberate on- “all questions of
finance, legislation and administration.”
Cither party can terminate the agreement
on July do. 11)1(5, if it gives the leader of
the other party at least three weeks’ notice
in writing. The idea seems to be that if
the parties can get along till next July
without biting each other they can keep the
peace till March, 1017. There is a pious
addendum to the effect that the agreement
is not to affect the right of any member to
exercise his full freedom of speech and
action. So far the co-operative idea is going
beautifully, but when the little Bill for ex-
tending the life of Parliament comes before
the House of Dodery it is more than likely
i- will either die or emerge seriously
mangled.
“Everything the Education Department:
does is just,” Griffith, its Minister in X. S.
A\ ales, told the Assembly the other evening.
Just, perhaps, but very quaint. For in-
stance, it puts the failures to examine the
successes. There is more than one inspector
of recent appointment who couldn’t get a
first-class school, yet has not only to examine
first-class schools but also to recommend the
headmasters for promotion or otherwise. On
the whole, it isn’t much wonder that some
teachers in Griffith's Department reckon
that it pays better there, to be a failure than
a success.
A valuable Bill, originating in the X.S.W.
Leg. Council—J. D. Fitzgerald is its sponsor
—will, if it completes its journey, put a stop
to the ancient liberty of testators to leave
their widows and families to charity or the
washtub. It not only provides for the ad-
equate maintenance of testator’s dependents,
hut also adjusts the law regarding the guar-
dianship of infants. The limitation of the
power of vindictive people to wreak a post-
mortem vengeance lias long been wanted,
and N. S. Wales is one of the last places in
the world where legislation on the subject
has been withheld.
Joseph Cook raced the minors in his
maiden constituency ;it Lithgow (N.S.W.)
on a recruiting jaunt the oilier evening. The
net result was one recruit! Well, three
cheers for the one, anyhow.
N. S. Wales Coverninent, which fixed the
price of milling wheat at ss. per bushel, and
is ready to prosecute any farmer who dares
to sell it for more, has just sent out its bills
to those drought-stricken farmers whom it
supplied with seed wheat —inferior stuff
which no self-respecting cocky sowed with-
out first winnowing. The price is Os. Of
course the answer of the Government is
that it couldn’t afford to sell for the ss. fixed
by law for milling wheat. Neither could
the farmer; but a little thing like that didn’l
matter when it was a case of getting cheap
food for the bloated city at the impoverished
country’s expense.
Section 152 of the Customs Act provides
that any increase in duties on goods supplied
on contract may be recovered by the con-
tractor. This fair thing has been unques-
tioned for 14 years. The other day, however,
a Victorian firm had to sue a co-operative
co. for the amount of duty paid on gunny-
bags under an increased tariff. The claim
was resisted, and the County Court gave the
defendants a verdict, which was subsequently
sustained by the Victorian Full Court. The
snag is Section 55 of the Constitution Act,
which provides that “laws imposing taxation
shall deal only with the imposition of taxa-
tion, and any provision dealing with any
other matter shall be of no effect.” anil
that laws imposing duties of Customs shall
deal with duties of Customs only. Under
that provision Section 152 was held to be
invalid; and now any commercial person
who has contracted to supply imported goods
without providing for any increase in tariff
rates will hare to pay the duties himself—
unless the High Court upends the view of
the Victorian Full Court. If it doesn't,
Parliament will have to settle the matter
by a special Act. To make the contractor
liable will be to introduce more speculation
into a business that needs to be made as little
speculative as possible: and every time
a tender is put in. something will have to
be added to cover this contingency.
There has been dead silence from David
Ilall lately about the profits made by that
eminent Attorney-General through specu-
lating on behalf of the Mu State in wheat
and farm produce. At present David sees
chaff in his sleep—bags and tons and ship-
loads ol: it. lie heard (even an Attorney-
General may hear things) that X. S. Wales
was short of fodder; so he sent off to the
U.S.A. for 20.0(10 tons or so. to arrive just
when the new season’s hay would be coming
in. David’s chaff has lately been passing
through a mill at Homebush. There it is
being shaken and sieved to get rid of some of
the tons of dust and rubbish with which it
is loaded; and the job is so dreadful that
the men have to wear respirators. By the
time the dust that Hall paid for as chaff is
taken out and the grain that should have
been in the chaff but wasn’t has been nut
in, the stuff will cost £32 per ton : and you
can buy thousands of tons of far better
stuff in the country now for £3. Some of
the imported stuff has actually been
sold for £3 17s. Gd. It is a great
thing to have your produce business
managed for you by a lawyer. It is almost
as satisfactory as it would be to give a
tinsmith the job of Chief Justice!

Ihe Victorian Education Department has
discovered that “Agricultural High Schools”
arc expensive futilities, and at Eehuca the
other day Minister Livingston wept tears
o'er the. wasted cash and vanished dream.
At one of these places, which costs £l4OO
a year and occupies 20 acres of land, there
appear to he two pupils, who arrive en masse
each morning. Inquiry into what has be-
come of those who have passed through the
schools discloses that, instead of growing
iiops, they are decorating office stools and
driving delivery carts. Livingston, looking
legietfulJy nt the hole in the cashbox, man-
,l. ' decided “not to open any more.” If
Livingston tracked the pupils of the ordi-
nary agricultural colleges lie would also find
astonishingly few who have stuck to farm-
mg. I lie t rouble is that Australian papers
and politicians have got into the habit of
talking in an airy way about “going on the
land, and youngsters have gone into schools
and colleges to learn about silos and Berk-
shire ducks, and then the old man has
startl'd to nose round for particulars of the
cost of getting on the land, and has mostly
discovered that to merely equip a decent-
sized farm and hang on to it till it produces
something costs as much as it does to equip
a youngster for a profession

THE STEPPING STONE.
There are 10,000 good arguments in favor
of the lieferenda proposals, so there was no
need to again wheel out this busted Free-
trade superstition:—■
Think wliat high prices mean to the wives of those
at the front, who freely risk their lives that we may
be free, and to the widows and mothers of the men
who have fallen.
Having thought on this, suppose the Aus-
tralian citizeii has a think about what low
prices would mean to the same parties. Also,
as the argument declares that “high prices
impair national efficiency,” let him also think
very hard on that point. Having done so
lie will see that if low prices are such good
things they can be at once secured by (1)
cutting down wages, and (2) drawing a pen
through the tariff. There is really no diffi-
culty about producing low prices if this Gov-
ernment thinks they are a good thing in-
stead of the curse they really are.
The Peacock Government, being fully
seized of the serious financial position, has
already begun to retrench. It has reduced
the country train services, with much justi-
fication in some cases; also it has reduced
the working staffs at small stations or abol-
ished them altogether, to the great loss and
discomfort of country people who were well-
loaded with losses and discomforts already.

THE GREAT FISHER MYSTERY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
i> . that is the whole point,” said the
1 nine Minister, smiling mysteriously. —Daily paper.
The smile of Andrew Fisher is a lovely thing to see,
When mysteriously it flutters from his eyes to his
moustache,
And a very sphinx for caution and obscurity is he;
Oh, his bitterest opponents never speak of him as
rash.
There are men of keener intellect and wider vision
too,
Of more brilliant parts and subtle, you can meet with
any day,
But for keeping dark a secret there are reallv veri-
few
Wiio can tantalise the public in his Scotch and
cheerful way.
Xow will Fisher go to London or remain in office
here ?
lis the riddle of the moment men are asking far
and wide.
And the daily papers argue till they make it almost
clear
That his bag is packed and ready to he wafted o'er
the tide.
Oh, the mystery appals us, and the ripple of his
smile
Is a blend of serpent folly with the cunning of the
lamb,
But for all his fine evasion, and despite his gentle
guile,
He can go or stay and welcome and we do not care
X.S.W.

DOES YOUR CHILD SUFFER FROM
WORMS?
Stomach and intestinal worms are the
cause of nine-tenths of the crying, fretful-
ness and irritability of children. The mere
mention of worms makes one feel uncomfort-
able, and their presence in the system must
assuredly cause such torture and agony that
there is'little wonder at the irritable dispo-
sition which is so often developed, not only
in children, but frequently in adults. Worms
require a specific medicine, and there is no
better remedy than Comstock’s “Dead Shot’
Worm Pellets. .
They are a purely vegetable medicinal
preparation for the eradication of worms in
children and adults. For sale by all chem-
ists and storekeepers, price 3s. Gd. per
packet, or will be sent, post paid, at the
same price by The W. H. Comstock Co-
Ltd., 23 Lang Street, Church Hill, Sydney.*
SPORTING
NOTIONS
A joyous item brightened New Plymouth
(M.L.) the other clay. In one of the draper’s shop
windows was displayed a leathern waistcoat bearing
a ticket with the information that 5000 of the gar-
ments were wanted for the men at Gallipoli. The small
town was much interested in the thing the first day ;
but on the second day of display the window was
nearly burst in by the excited populace. The ticket
had by that time fallen from the waistcoat on to a
selection of lace-trimmed —er —well, never mind.
“Five thousand of these wanted for our boys at the
Front!” It was a great day.
* * * *
The honors of the just-concluded A..J.C.
meeting were with St. Carwyne, Reputation, Cetigne
and Carlita. The two firsts (Metropolitan and Craven
Plate), a dead-heat in the Spring Stakes and third in
the Randwick Plate put St. CaTwvne into the first
flight of Australian prads. Reputation, who dead-
heated with him in the Stakes, and was second and
third in the Plate and the Metropolitan, turned out
to be a little dandy who appropriated most of the
ill-luck that happened to be lying round at Rand-
wick during the week, and was additionally unfor-
tunate in meeting a horse that had, with good
reason, been regarded as unreliable even if brilliant
just when the animal happened to be at the very top
of his form. There can be little doubt that the
public saw St. Carwyne do the best he is callable 01 ;
but Reputation will probably do better things yet.
( etio-ne’s place among the three-year-olds is not alto-
gether clear, but he won both the Derby and Clibborn
Stakes in such good style as to give him a strong
claim to consideration as the best of his year.
The best performance of the meeting was put up
by Carlita, whose win in the Randwick Plate, with
Sst. 91b., stamps her as an exceptionally good mare.
She won' from end to end, and without being
pressed ran the two miles in 3min. 27 (min. from a
field which included Uncle Sam, Ulva’s Isle and St.
Carwyne. Carlita is weighted at Bst. 51b. for the
Melbourne Cup, and on this running must be counted
as one of the most dangerous among the top-weights.
At the finish she was leaving the others at every
stride. Of the beaten division Uncle Sam was doing
best over the last furlong. St. Carwyne’s perform-
ance in this event was such as to establish his
strong dislike for a two-mile journey. Put under
pressure against Cagou for second money he turned
it, up at the critical moment, running away from the
whip with a celerity that would have carried him
right across the course had Cagou not been there to
block the way. The difference between St. Carwyne
and Woorak merely seems to be that the latter gets
sooner tired of being bustled. The five-year-old will
certainly see a mile and a half out to the bitter end
(vide the Metropolitan), but a mile is quite enough
for Woorak, and then onlv if nothing collars him.
In the Craven Plate (1-i mile) he started favorite at
7 to 4 on, and spread joy through the Land of Judah
by promptly shutting up when asked to shift himself
more rapidly over the last furlong. What on earth
impels people to back a horse like this is one of the
turfs .manv mysteries. The writer of this paragraph
wouldn’t trust him with tuppence in a sprint over
50 yards.
Of course, the demented tribe who go a-punting
got all the worst of it on the meeting. The first
day was not particularly profitable to the Bar Yons,
but they managed to get a crust on the second, some
butter on the third, and on the fourth a case of fizz
and a box of cigars. Saturday’s events ended accord-
ing to the prophets of woe and desolation, the
favorite going down with a heavy thud five times
out of six, and in the solitary race where the animal
won he was a mighty poor favorite. Two races were
won by the rankest of outsiders —Scoutmaster the
Waverlev Handicap, and Schoches the Dowling
Steeplechase. The first of these had been in the
habit recently of winning under welter weights at
northern provincial meetings, and probably wondered
what had happened when he found himself with a
midget of 6st. 71b. in the saddle for the Metropoli-
tan. Anyhow, he was so much surprised that he ran
nearlv last. But bv Saturday he had got over his
astonishment, and then fled over a mile and three-
quarters of ground in such a hurry that he snatched
what looked like a certainty from Sea Pink in the
last few strides and set up a fresh Australian record
for the distance. Schoches, who got away with the
Steeple, is an unprepossessing-looking customer with
a record of wins in the show ring as a buggy horse
and jumper. He is said to have changed hands not
long ago at the enormous price of £l7. If any people
expected him to win they didn’t go near the books,
for his name was scarcely written. Yet he jumped
and stayed so well as to suggest that somebody has
missed a rare opportunity of throwing in for a
hatful over something bigger than Saturday’s event.
The day after Mountain Knight’s poor
showing in the Spring Stakes lie was reported to be
very sick, which in a way of speaking evened things
up a bit to the punters who were feeling ill about
laying odds on him winning. There was talk of the
Knight having been doped by an enemy. Maybe.
Anyhow, he was scratched for Caulfield, and that
made a lot more people feel ill. Since then the
horse is reported to be doing as well as can be
expected, and has consequently been backed by
someone or other for the Melbourne Cup. As he has
9st. to carry on that trip it looks just as likely as
not that there will be more sickness about next
month. Better horses than Mountain Knight haven’t
been in front with that weight before to-day.
Cheerful news for purchasers of high-
priced yearlings: Cetigne cost his owner 200 guineas,
St. Carwyne 100 and Carlita 125. The winnings of
the three at Randwick were £13,793. Wolaroi,
winner of the Breeders’ Plate, was bred by his owner;
Eulacre, winner of the Gimcrack Stakes, was sold
as a yearling for 75 guineas. They won £3868_.
Sister Satellite, second in each event, cost 1125
guineas. i
The win of a Sydney medical man over
the A.J.C. meeting ran into five figures, most of it
landed when St. Carwyne got the second leg of the
double in with the Metropolitan. The winner re-
joiced exceedingly and was glad in public places.
Looking on at the Metropolitan there was another
man who won the best end of a competency a few
years ago. lie walked away in misery and gloom;
for when St. Carwyne snatched the stake which
William the Silent should have won, the five figures
he would have called round to collect were blown
bubbles. The luck of the game!

“Devilfish”: Fishermen spin awful yarns
about the number of fish they catch, but can any
of them beat this?
Janies Lumsden de-
posed in evidence
before a Royal
Commission that
fishing with rod
and line, he once
jerked 100 dozen
freshwater herring
or cucumber mullet
out of the Derwent
near New Norfolk
(Tas.) in eight
hours. That means
five fish every two
minutes. Another
angler, W. Crooke,
avers that with a
bush sapling, a bit
of string and a
white fly his father
once hauled nearly
]OO dozen of the
same fish, averag-
ing about £lb.
apiece, out of the
Huon River in a
few hours. It would
take a smart man
to catch one cu-
cumber mullet in
either Huon or
Derwent now.
JIMMY
CLABBY,
Pug.
“0.K.”: Once in a way the inveterate
punters who follow form and measure chances by tne
odds they can get have a win thrust upon them by
the betting market. St. Carwyne, for the Craven
Plate last week, was a case in point. The handsome
son of St. Alwyne started at 7’s, 8 s and 10 s to 1
(the last the standard price) in a field of five yn
form he was far and away the most reasonable in-
vestment at the price, and his backers won their
monev. Ten to 1 St. Carwyne in a w.f.a. race five
starters! On the previous Saturday he had caught
Reputation at the end of a 12-furlongs w.f.a. race,
giving 51b. to the Maoriland horse. Mountain Knight,
Cagou, Ulva’s Isle, Carlita and others were behind
the dead-heaters. On the Monday the Metropolitan
proved the truth of Saturday’s running, and sug-
gested that St. Carwyne was improving, for this
time, when he met Reputation and the others on
101 b. better terms than before, he beat them very
handsomely. Oil the Wednesday he had to give
41b. to Reputation, whilst he was meeting Cagou on
practically the same terms as in the Saturday race.
But this' time the distance was only 10 furlongs,
and for no conceivable reason, save weight of money,
Reputation and Cagou started at shorter odds than
St. Carwyne, whilst Woorak, the brilliant miler, was
backed down to 6 to 4. This was where the fol-
lowers of form came in. They remembered that St.
Carwyne, the 12-furlongs champion of to-day, had
seemed to have more speed than stamina last year.
When he finished third for the Metropolitan in 1914
he was swerving as though tired. Again in the
Caulfield Cup, with a light weight up, he tired
after being in the lead. But in the Melbourne
Stakes (10 furlongs) St. Carwyne had beaten Ale-
conner, Cagou, Posinatus and others on w.f.a. terms.
If the St. Carwyne of 1914 could win the Melbourne
Stakes with a brilliant rush, what reasonable punter
could forget to back the St. Carwyne of last week
for a w.f.a. race over the Melbourne Cup distance,
seeing that the price was 10 to 1 in a field of five?
Only once in a blue moon does Ikev Mo offer so
tempting an investment on the course.
* * * *
One of the best “good things” that ever
didn’t come off for the Metropolitan was the im-
ported Positano in 1897. Positano, like St. Car-
wvne last year, won the Spring Stakes on A.J.C.
Derby Day quite unexpectedly, and like St. Carwyne
last "year lie was thrown into the Metrop. at a light
weigiit. A son of St. Simon, and of goodly appear-
ance, a five-year-old with a nrevious win to his credit,
the English nag looked the best of “certs.” for the
Metrop. All the old occasional punters, who finger
a £5-note a long time before putting it on a
racing “moral,” rolled up with their subscription
for Positano in the Metrop. They wired their fivers
to the leading books of the time. They walked or
cabbed to the offices on the Monday morning. They
went to Randwick to see the certainty come off. But
it didn’t come off. Positano finished nowhere. He
was an uncertain-tempered animal, already in the
stallion stage of life, and after one or two more
failures he was put to the stud. A fine lot of
children have resulted. Poseidon, Lord Nolan,
Piastre and Posinatus were four that won the Mel-
bourne Cup. # #
As a matter of course, William the Silent
and Reputation are worth backing for the Caulfield
and Melbourne Cups, because they were placed in
the Metropolitan, which for the past nine years has
been the surest of handicap tests. Only once (in
1909) have the placed horses in the Metrop. failed
to provide a winner or a placed horse in one or
other of the Cups. Last year St. Carwyne s third
in the Metrop. was confirmed by a third in the Oaul-
field and a fourth in the Melbourne Cup. In four
previous instances the second for the Metrop. scored
a win at Caulfield or Flemington, or both. And in
three other cases the third in the Metrop. won the
Caulfield Cup. All within nine years ihese are
figures that talk. The actual winner ot the Metrop.
is usually stopped by his penalty it he goes for the
big handicaps in Victoria, but the two gees that
came next to him are always a dangerous pair. A
gambler might fairly take 6 to 4 in thousands that
William the Silent and Reputation will account tor
one or other of the Cups between them.
* * * *
The Caulfield Cup winner is commonly
said to want a lot of finding, but the fact remains
that the winner for the last five years has not
averaged more than 6 to 1. He or she has been
among the favorites at the start. This prophet
prefers Patrobas, William the Silent, Reputation
Burrabadeen, Lavendo (all of them short-priced) and
the questionable Cyklon to St. Carwyne Garlin,
Aurifer, Prince Bardolph, Golden Wire and the rest
of the field. They can’t all win or get placed;
Say William the Silent in a loud tone of voice. lie
has an undeniably great chance on paper, and at
time of writing there is nothing against him save
the allegation that he seemed a bit sore after the
trip from Sydney. William will have G. Meddielc
instead of little Dempsey on his back when he runs
at Caulfield, which may or may not be a help to the
little gee that lost the Metropolitan so unluckily.
No matter how they may perform in the Caul-
field event, the Melbourne Cup chances of Lavendo,
Patrobas, Burrabadeen and Reputation will look good
if the nags continue to thrive. Patrobas has vet to
be promoted to the same three-year-old “class ’ as
Cetigne and Wallace Isinglass, but he is likely to
get there after the V.R.C. Derby if all goes well
with him this next fortnight. Anyhow, he’s a good
sort of Wallace colt to be in the Melbourne Cup at
6st. 71b.
* * * *
Guineas Day at Caulfield brought forth no
evidence as to the strength of the coming N.S.W.
contingent, except that Flash of Steel ran nowhere m
the Toorak Handicap, whereas E. J. Watt’s pre-
cocious Maori Mat had slipped away with the
Debutant Stakes. Patrobas won the Guineas decis-
ively, after a rough passage, and Traquette got the
better of Cyklon in the Caulfield Stakes, which is
a nine-furlongs race. At a mile it looked as though
the imported horse (who was giving 91b.) would
certainly overtake the flying mare, but she kept
going whilst he failed for want of condition, appar-
ently. A mighty fine gentleman is Cyklon, but not
a Melbourne Cup horse, according to this scribe’s
ideas concerning the sort pf animal that is best oyer
a long distance. Nor did he seem likely to be ripe
for the Caulfield Cup struggle on Saturday. In the
meantime, however, Cyklon has probably run in the
Eclipse Stakes on Wednesday, and if he has won the
11 furlongs race, or gone very near it, he will have
a second-to-none chance for the big handicap on
Saturday. Presuming that William the Silent also
has had the colors up for the Eclipse Stakes,
punters can draw their own comparisons between the
two English horses —at the weights.
Two previous winners during the same afternoon
—King Marco and Traquette—were saddled again for
the Toorak Handicap, the last race on the card.
This was an unprecedented happening. One winner
is sometimes brought out for a second go at a big
meeting, but two is a record. The pony King
Marco, who had won the Welter race four hours
earlier, started at 12 to 1 aud finished a good third,
only 1-J lengths behind the winner. Traquette was
backed down to 7 to 4, but her tussle with Cyklon
only two hours ago had taken too much steam
out of the mare. Besides she was humping 9st. 31b.,
and the pace was exceptionally hot. The Toorak this
year was the fastest mile ever run on the Caulfield
course.
“Snowy” : It was tbe annual race-meeting
at Conargo (N.S.W.), and down on Billabong Flat
shearers and station-men had gathered for a day’s
sport. After five favorites had gone down matters
looked serious for the punters, and comment concern-
ing the judge, who was mounted In a dray, was of
an aspersive and sweeping character. As the field
turned into the home straight in the last race it was
seen that the favorite was running like a cow. Some-
thing had to be done, and like a flash an inspiration
seized the bunch. With a whoop they ran that
tip-dray headlong for the billabong; and when the
judge had climbed out and emptied his eyes of green
weed and mud the mokes were coming back to
unsaddle.
* * * *
A ticket-snatcher lias been given 32
months’ easy life for following his profession at
Moonee Valley (Vic.). It sounds a fair “stretch”;
but no doubt the Court considered the long list of
unpunished snatchings, and hardened its heart accord-
ingly. Precious few punters take a record of their
ticket- numbers, and the crime is therefore difficult to
sheet home once the snatcher has departed. It is
a petty business, but it beats bank-breaking for safe
profits.
* * * *
Somebody in the Cold Country has dis-
covered that an Act passed in the time of Henry
the Mormon requires the Archbishop of Canterbury
to keep a stud for the breeding of “swift horses.”
William Allison, of London Sportsman, reckons this
shows that in the time of the historical Fatman the
quality of the nag was a matter of national concern,
and suggests that his Gaiters should he haled before
the Beak and invited to explain wily he should not
pay a fine or hand over his job to Richard Marsh or
Dick Wootton. If a common informer succeeded
in an action against the shovel-hatted gent, he (the
informer) would receive half the fines, which would
amount to £l4O.
* * * *
As is customary, many small punters failed to meet
their obligations. Fielders regard such happenings
as inevitable. — Lachrymose par. in Sydney journal.
Fred Dyer, otherwise known as “the
singing Welshman,” and Les Darcy climbed into the
ring at Sydney Stadium on Saturday, and the subse-
quent proceedings were just plain farce. Dyer hadn’t
a thousand to one chance, and before the first round
had ended was a sore and sorry pug. Darcy had
hard work keeping up with the retreating enemy, but
when they happened to come together smote him
with direful emphasis. Once he was cautioned for
hitting low, but the occurrence was really the result
of Dyer leaping upwards and taking the glove on
a part of himself that wasn’t there before. A man
only needs to leap high enough at the right moment
to make any blow fall below the plimsoll. At the
end of the fifth lesson Wales was a bleeding wreck,
and early in the sixth the towel intercepted further
execution. When this pair met before Dyer saw
the journey through. This time Darcy made him
look like a novice.
* * * *
Although the contour of Harry Reeves
amidships suggested a pneumatic pup, he managed
to account for Joe Bonds at Melbourne Stadium on
Saturday night. He got the verdict at the end of
do rounds, mainly by being somewhere else when
Bonds started to hurl himself to the front. The big
Canadian was burdened with a useless right, damaged
on Jack Darcy the previous Saturday, and with a
rib dented by Beeves in round two. In consequence
his reliance was on a left hook, his right being sent
over only about half a dozen times, once in the
twelfth round welting Reeves down for four. Reeves's
ducking and dodging made the performance look like
a contortion act, with Bonds as the comic assistant.
When he smote, however, it was with both hands,
and the approval of Providence and the referee was
upon him. Bonds took home an over-size hand, a
shattered rib, a thick ear, and, by the look of the
house, about three half-crowns.
* * * *
“Rag”: Uid Charon received a splendid
oar for his ferry-boat when George Upward died
at Sydney last week. Few, if any, have figured so
actively in Australian rowing since he made his inter-
colonial debut as stroke of the triumphant Victorian
Eight in 1880. He sat in the same seat for several
years, with all but unbroken success, and
then devoted himself to the sport as coach.
In the matter of styles “G.E.,” whilst adhering in
fundamentals to the English style (as per Woodgate),
introduced modifications of his own, which eventu-
ally developed into the present Victorian system. I
hope the tide will be with him down the Styx.
For a crust he toiled as a clerk, first, ill the Vic-
torian Assembly, and then in the Commonwealth
Parliament; and his kindliness amounted to genius.
Sydney sportsmen’s battalion holds a fine-
record. On the word of hon. sec. Frank Iredale, only
one recruit was turned down for physical deficiency
in the first batch of SOO!
* * * *
The “possibilities” forecasted a month ago
about the lingering football Finals in Adelaide were
all there. To begin with, the third on the list,
West Adelaide, beat the first, Port. Then the fourth,
Sturt, beat the second, South Adelaide, by some
miraculous straight-kicking (nine goals six behinds)
in a sideways gale which sent South’s more numer-
ous shots a trifle wide of the posts. In the so-called
final, Sturt beat West again by straight shooting,
seven goals three behinds against two goals 13
behinds; and, on Port Adelaide exercising their
right of challenge, Sturt beat them also, by two clear
goals. The Sturt 18 are a young and keen lot, ami
the club had never before won the premiership, while
Port have been either first or second for seven years
past. Sturt only got into the “last four” by a
scramble, but put up a great performance in beating
the other three in turn within as many weeks.
* * * *
The question of what would happen in
Adelaide football if the “fairest and most brilliant
player” of the season happened to be two individuals
is still unsettled ; but the Magarey medal which
goes to that player has this year been awarded to
Barry, of the South Adelaides, as being both. The
referee in each first-class match registers his decision
as soon as it is over, and the votes are counted at
the end of the season. This time there was a dead-
heat of three, and the referees had to come together
to make the award. Barry is now training to kick
Turks.
* * * *
“D.F.” : The football season blew out at
Hobart the other Saturday with Lefroy on top.
Financially it was the worst season the League ever
struck, and when it has paid the Cricket Association
for the ground there will not he enough left to buy
bootlaces. The cricketers are moving warwards, too,
and the districts have to be cut down from five to
four. Even so there will be a job to rake teams
together. A lot of the best men are at the Front.
At the annual meeting a proposal was made to drop
the district matches altogether and play only scratch
games, but though it met with a good deal of sup-
port it did not go through. Financially the cricket
outlook is hazy, apart from the war; for the foot-
ballers find most of the boodle to keep the Associa-
tion’s ground going, and now the City Council is
making a new sports ground and is pretty sure to
get the League as a tenant.
Sydney University A grade cricket team
has lost its first match handsomely. The ’Varsity
has been one of the strongest elevens during the last
few seasons, yet on the opening day of 1915-6 the
club was represented by hardly any players wlv:
had ever shown in first grade. Practically the whole
of last year’s team enlisted, and many oi them have
seen active service. Another elub to do magnifi-
cently is Central Cumberland. Owing to enlist-
ments it has shut down temporarily.
Manly (N.S.W.) Municipal Council felt
a flutter in its chest last week when an application
was made for the payment of £5 15s. —money and
valuables “lifted” while the owner was in the surf.
The Council hires out a costume and a dressing
cubicle for 10d., and as somebody opened the cubicle
and took the valuables the owner opined that lie
had a claim on the Council. The Council naturally
decided that he hadn’t. Some day, however, a can-
tankerous person will argue the matter, and the
discussion won’t do Manly any good. The Sydney
Municipal (Domain) baths give a guarantee against
loss for the payment of one penny, and bathers at
Manly should get something for six times that
charge. The thief scourge is one of the drawbacks
in surfing, and the councils’ unconcern is almost
conspicuous enough to make an outsider believe that
the municipalities call tenders for the job of looting
their customers.
*. * * *
“Retlaw” : Fishing is a gay old sport
sometimes, but you’ll get something specially lively
if you go after the “stingarees” on the mud flats
on ‘the Clarence River (N.S.W.). But it’s no game
for a lone hand. The paraphernalia required is good
strong shark lines, offal for bait (the louder the
better), and a supply of saplings or fence rails about
10ft. long. If luck is in, the stingrays bite very
quicldv. All hands tail on to the line and haul.
Then the saplings are brought into use. The lively
time consists in trying to break the beast s back
without" being caught by the switching tail. To bit!
a wriggling stingray with a heavy long pole while
steering clear of his sting and keeping your feet
on a greasy bank takes some doing. I’ve seen the
beast hauled out 3ft. across and with a tail 6ft. tc
Bft. long.
But perhaps the best sport with stingrays in Aus-
tralia can be had in the American River, Kangaroo
Island (S.A.). They are there by the thousand.
The local plan is to harpoon them as you drift in a
dinghy.
AFTER THE MATCH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Now from the close-clipt field the players come,
Silent and white across the restful green ;
No bugle brings them respite, and no drum
Rolls as night comes to shroud the peaceful scene ;
No trench has scarred, no grave has heaped their
turf !
On other shores beats war’s tumultuous surf.
To some past age that we had half-forgot
Belongs this flannelled pageant, done with play,
That from the pitch comes in with pleasure hot,
While dies in sweetness one more summer’s day ;
The scent of fresh-crushed grass clings to the ball;
And yet—there’s something ghostly in it all!
O peaceful summer days, how faint and far
You seem to us who scarce can see for tears!
O 'happy nights, when ev’ry thoughtful star
Shone down ori lovers planning for the years!
Is this indeed the same round Earth you knew,
Ere on the world the envious Prussian drew?
How many comrades that we knew of old
Have played their last in Death’s terrific game?
Ilow many lovers, too, their last tale told,
Now dreams alone in fields whose crops are flame?
Ah, you who lounge beneath the bordering trees,
What joy find you in games, remembering these?
The crowd is gone. The night comes on and lays
Its shadow fingers on your battle-ground.
Who wears the tinsel crown, the paper bays,
For deeds that rang the painted pickets round?
Play if you must, but vision, in God’s name,
The hedge of steel that guards your tiny game!
N.S.W.
GALLIPOLI.
“Some punters failed to pay!” they cry;
As though all hostile views it squelches.
Still, there’s a chance we yet may try;
The Tote —that neither scales nor welshes !
N.S.W.
E. N. M.

from an experienced Boxer. But you don’t admire his awkwardness,
or the way lie leaves himself open, or how he punches the air;
and wears a blackened eye that advertises his downfall for days
afterwards.
I Teach Boxing by Post
in such a manner that you will rapidly develop your natural
ability.
The Instruction is simple and explicit—based on
my own personal experience and connection with the
world’s champions—both amateur and professional.
HUNDREDS of pupils throughout Australasia and

my postal course. Here is evidence: —
“I had a spar with a big chap last week,
and my hand seemed to work like a machine;
and he said after that he could not keep my
left out of his face.”
There are no loopholes for any misunderstanding.

This State office was brought to life 10 years
ago, chiefly to serve as a brake upon the
insurance cos. in the fire business, which
had pushed their rates up so high that they
were regarded as over the odds. Good evi-
dence that they were over the odds was soon
forthcoming: even before this office could get
to work the rates were lowered. Two years
ago the general manager reckoned that the
public had been thus saved altogether about
a million and a half in premiums. It often
happens that the competitor who is respon-
sible for cheaper goods is allowed to go to the
Avail; and after the funeral A r ery good
reasons are usually found for putting prices
back to Avhere they ivere before, or a bit
above it. In this case the office, though it
hasn’t had all the support it should have
got. has no cause to complain ; and, as the
table shoAvs, an increasing number of people
see the Avisdom of keeping it alive and
vigorous.
In one respect it is just as AA r ell that the
groAvth of annual premium income has been
gradual: it has alloived the office to maintain
reserves fairly commensurate Avith the risks,
which it couldn't have done if the premiums
had come in A'ery much more rapidly. In
that case, no doubt, it could have draAvn
upon the State for the £lOO,OOO of capital
Avhich the Treasury is authorised to pro-
vide. But the aim of the office has always
been to live on its oavii resources; and
1 except for £2OOO which it got to start Avitli
and subsequently repaid) it has easily been
able to do that. In the long run the policy-
holders Avill get the advantage of the saving
of interest. Sooner or later, when the
reserves have been further strengthened, and
provided that things go along as they have
gone hitherto, a reduction of rates is cer-
tain. During the past four years the
premiums have paid all claims and expenses,
and 2S p.c. of the income has been left
over; and though for the present it is neces-
sary for safety’s sake to go on accumulating
such profits against the risk of conflagration
and other hazards and contingencies, the
time will come Avhen it will be recognised
that it is not necessary to ask the public
for £lOO when £72 is enough—or say £75,
alloAving for necessary additions to the cur-
rent risks reserve. It isn’t a pleasant thing
for private cos. to contemplate; but even
private cos. Avon’t gain anything by burying
their heads in the sand and refusing to see
the inevitable.

Mining at Westonia (W.A.) has resulted
in the diseoA-ery of undreamt-of Avater. The
granite outcrops of the West have always
been known to hold supplies of more or less
consequence gathered from rainfall, but
nothing like the supplies struck underground
at Westonia Avere ever imagined. In one
instance a rock drilling party ivas practi-
cally SAvept aAvay as the steel bored into one
of these reservoirs, and lately in the Edna
May there has been a pressure of 701 b. to
the inch in the Aoav from the holes in the
face. Plant to cope with 100,000 gallons is
being provided to combat the stream. Un-
fortunately the water is mineralised, other-
Avise some of the holdings surrounding the
mine would haA-e a chance of saving them-
selves from inevitable collapse by blossoming
into irrigation concerns.
John De'ason, of Moliagul (Vic.). avlio
died the other day, always stuck to it that
he and his mate found the famous Welcome
Stranger nugget by simply striking a long
pick into the surface. A lot of the rich gold
thereabouts Avas, he said, got that ivay. If
the pick struck nothing the sampler tried
again further on; if the pick-point struck
something likely, he dug. Often, of course,
it Avas stone or wood, but occasionally it
was gold. Deason believed that the “Wel-
come Stranger” had been shed from a large
“bloAv” of quartz just beloAV Avhere it was
found. Tlis theory Avas that here and there
in this "buck” body Avas a patch of gold,
and that the big nugget had been one. He
believed that if a tunnel Avere put in. and
the bloAv shattered by an enormous charge
of explosives, other patches might be found :
but he never managed to get a syndicate to
test the idea. Deason’s battery at Moliagul
is one of the quaint old-timers in Victoria,
but it is still quite capable of crushing
quartz. Its trouble is to get quartz Avorth
crushing.
"O’Phrmerty": A mighty dismal place is
Kelly Basin, on Macquarie Harbor (Tas.).
Tt Avas to have been the port for the Lyell
field: but that Avas before the big amalga-
mation Avhich resulted in the present Mount
Lyell Co. Hotels, stores and houses Avere
built on a lavish scale. A railway ran some
miles up the Linda Valley to connect Avith
the mine. The rolling stock included five
locos, and hundreds of ore trucks, and a
station was made at the Kelly Basin end
big enough to serve a large town. Just as
the place seemed launched on a career of
prosperity, iicavs came through of the amal-
gamation and that Strahan Avould be the
port of the ueiv co. Kelly Basin died in
less than a Aveek. That Avas years ago, and
noAv the place is the pathetic sarcophagus
of hope. The only sign of life visible is
Avhen the Mount Lyell Co. runs a train over
the line noAV and then in order to hold its
rights.

THE DAY OF THE EXPERT. To-day is the day of the expert —the Ilian who knows every detail of his work
- -who is thorough, efficient and up to date. The engineer who desires to reach the top of the tree in his profession must
he conversant with everv engineering* method which makes for speed, complete craftsmanship, and for economy.
THE ENGINEER WHO DOES NOT KNOW of the Oxy-Acetylene process is not abreast of the times; he is
at big* disadvantage, and leaves himself open to the charge of incompetence.
Throughout the world to-day the Oxv-Acetylene process is the latest scientific addition to every engineering
workshop, "which, fitted with this process, has an advantage over the opposition impossible to estimate. It is simple,
vet wonderfully effective: its price is trifling, and the plant is portable. AV hither the broken parts require to be cut
or welded at the bench or b,v the roadside is of no consequence.
Any work of Iron or Steel welding or cutting- can be carried out with Ox.v-Acetylene. It is revolutionising
f the Engineering trade. Broken parts that once were useless can now be successfully welded and made new
again. This, too. in the matter of minutes. Cost is a matter of pence as compared with the expenditure
of pounds in the old method of buying new parts and rna king new drawings and c astings.
All Engineers should at once write us for free catalogues and illustrations showing- the remark-
able variety of work that can be done with Oxy-Acetylene. Following are a few of the broken
and worn machinery parts our process will make new again:
Cracked or broken Aluminium Crank or Gear Cases, Differential Boxes, Steering- Wheels, Pipes,
Cylinders, Piston's, Cranks, Clutches, Cardan Shafts, Band Brakes, Worn Key ways in Shafts.
Pieces welded to broken Gears and New Teeth Cut, etc.
Broken Machinery of any kind can. be welded, including- Cast Iron, Wrought Iron. Mild
Steel, Brass, Copper,* Gun Metal, etc., which cannot be repaired by any other process.
The latest authoritative work on the subject, “A Practical Manual of
Autogenous Welding,” published by Charles Griffin & Co., London, states:
“Within the last few years welding by oxy-hvdrogen and oxy-acetylcne blow-
pipes has been applied in thousands of workshops.” Those workshops which arc
rapidly adding the process to their equipment include manufacturers, con-
tractors, machine shops, garages, blacksmiths, boilermakers, repair shops,
foundries, steel mills and railroad shops.

ANSWERS TO CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on the MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted: and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
Iluby: “Benevolent neutrality” is the art

lives to the nation. It regarded it chiefly as an
illustration of the amazing toughness of the hide of
the present generation of N. S. Wales politicians. . . .

the Empire, and we are rather grateful that they
can make that pretence while interfering so little
with u5....C. Reader: Don’t know what the Queens-

A.L.R.: Thin little drawings and old little jokes.
....Finlayson: Same trouble, apparently... .K.K.:
You’re very young, aren’t you?... .Woodicus: Afraid

Sam Mauger, president of the Vic. Alli-
ance, the body which assisted with all its
might to introduce the early closing of Mel-
bourne pubs, admits failure. “We have a
temporary measure,” said Samuel, at the
annual meeting, “but, notwithstanding this,
those who are making the greatest profits
out of the war expenditure in this country
are those associated with the liquor inter-
ests.” The Alliance consists mostly of one-
eyed citizens who scream at the very idea
of State management, and the time now
seems ripe for it to either propose a prac-
ticable alternative scheme or get out of the
reform business.
J. C. Watson’s scheme for securing em-
ployment for returned soldiers is disfigured
by the wretched device of land purchase.
Why Watson should try to make the Labor
party again identify itself with this make-
shift is incomprehensible. The very terms
in which it is suggested that the system
should be carried on —“it is expected that
payment could be arranged by long-dated
bonds” —is enough to condemn it. Has Aus-
tralia not a big enough load of bonds round
her neck already? In any case, if Australia
could go on stuffing Cohen’s pocket with
bonds from now till Doomsday, land pur-
chase for settlement purposes is a trumpery
process with which Conservatism should
alone be associated. It is not Labor policy
to ransom back the country in order that
its sons may find emnloyment. But it is
Labor policy to demand that the landlords
shall yield back to society that immense in-
crement their lands have acquired through
the expenditure of public money.
“The Pariah,” who knows his Asia: —
The German did more than send stuff to Australia;
he sent stuff to other countries—and why shouldn’t
we try to collar some of that trade? There is no
need to labor the advantage of our position as re-
gards at least the south of China, where the Hun
had the bulk of his business; but there is great need
for us to ask what our representative is doing to
keep our business men up to date with information.
When I was last in Hongkong an Australian bet me
that I would not be able to find the name of the
aforesaid rep. in any newspaper office either in that
particular oven or in Shanghai. Of seven papers
only one knew of his existence, and it had the
name wrong. That was a few years ago, but if the
position is any better now we ought to hear about it.
Yankee firms arc getting in and scoring new busi-
ness which should come to us. Things are done
differently by Japan. We have already had a well
informed and capable committee from that quarter,
and the firstfruits are already with us—mostly to
our disadvantage. Can’t we get busy, too —on some-
thing to our advantage?

. I’anaualaiid’s revenue for the quarter to
OAM of Se l )tember showed an increase of
iJAUOO compared with the corresponding
Jhiee months of 1914, while the expenditure
I™® I** 1 ** frilly ahead to the tune of
t.lO /,000. There is a rocky time coming for
somebody. Most likely it will be the kid who

Bill Hamilton, Minister for Mines, has
a discovery which he reckons worth
v i- • or so - • It happened this way.
Application had been made bv one of the
coal cos. at Ipswich for certain rights, and
in the course of inquiry it was found that
tie Crown had years ago reserved to itself
the minerals under a racecourse. In the
process of time the racecourse club got per-
mission to cut up the land and sell it. It
is calculated that there are four million
tons of coal under these lands. As the
Crown has been looking about for a mine of
its own—all the other coal-bearing lands in
the vicinity were grabbed by syndicates a
couple of generations ago—the value of the
discovery will be appreciated.
“ E .N ; M." ; The Light Horse Camp at
i hermside is a disgrace to the Defence De-
partment. A recent visit convinced me
that it must have been chosen by a major in
the horse-marines. Actually there is no
pioper training-ground, and no provision
>’ oi ; a Jttexnpt at, drilling the men with
horses. The place is divided into small
paddocks, in one of which the unfortunate
beasts, exposed to sun, wind and dust,
wander round disconsolately. The camp
itself is a poor affair at best, and quite un-
worthy of Queensland. The cooking
arrangements are conducive to waste, especi-
ally of meat, which is getting dearer and
dearer, and though the quality and quantity
of the food are all that they should be, one
undei stands, after a meal or two in camp,
the full force of the old Dutch proverb about
tiod sending meat and the Devil sending
cooks. A few ovens, such as those used
by country bakers, cheaply and easily con-
structed, are needed to replace the existing
system. One can hardly expect a tired De-
partment to tackle this subject, but the
whole camp-question really needs grappling
with. Instead of purchasing, or leasing, a
few hundred acres here and there at the end
of a tram-line, or near some suburban rail-
way station, the Defence Department should
secure several thousand acres of suitable
land, 15 or 20 miles at least from the cities —
land where there would be grass for stock,
where vegetables and fruit could be grown
for the men, and where there would be room
for a rifle-range, big drill and exercise
grounds, and sufficient variety of contour to
give men and horses a training. The only
people who profit by the existing system
seem to be trippers, tram cos. and publicans.
Had there been such an area available near
Brisbane during the past year we should not
be sending to. the Front men painfully short
of rifle-practice and mounted drill. The
Department might make a note of it that
Australia will need a good system after the
war quite as much as she needed it before
the war.

Max Meldrum is a strong candidate for
the Art Directorship of the State’s Technical
Schools system. It will be a good revolu-
tion for Queensland to pioneer if an Aus-
tralian—-and an Australian of the first flight
—is Selected to direct art in an Australian
ptate. Up to date Australian politicians
ha\e been struck on mostly semi-competents
with a certificate from stodgy South Ken-
sington—the sort of thing the real artist
y.-lio has had early study in London loses at
the age of 16. Happily the Queensland Gov-
ernment is too young to be hypnotised bv
.jj u P ers titi°o of the South Kensington
(certificate, and the prejudices are all in
favor of an Australian.

a* it spins along. By. this plan much coun-

“D.”: The Opposition recent stonewall in
the Bananaland Assembly revealed the utter
weakness and hopelessness of Tolmie as a
fighting leader and the unexpected grit of
young Corser. Though the latter took the
outing more as a joke than anything else, he
displayed tenacity, versatility and endur-
ance. It was no jest to deliver about 20
speeches during the 15 hours’ sitting, even
though the subject was the familiar \>ne of
electoral principles.
Bundaberg is a proud town when men-
tion is made of April 25 and the cliffs of
Gaba Tepe. In the first boat that left the
transports to rush the beach four Bunda-
berg lads were seated —Ilefferan, Monag-
han, Mitchell and Cox. Of the 60 in
the boat only these four set foot on Turkish
ground. Mitchell, who at the landing car-
ried a machine-gun ashore through water
waist deep, and subsequently used it to
good effect, is back in his native town with

If any Bananalander is desirous of send-
ing a parcel to a member of the 2nd Light
Horse, forward it to Mrs. J. S. P. Bourne.
Uayhekl, and her energy and system will
do the rest. Her son, Major Bourne, has
been in trenches for some weeks with his
dorsemen, and each mail finds him more
enthusiastic about his charges.
Nurse Norma Mowbray, daughter of the
d i M -> who returned to Queens-
land last Monday from the Front, says that
the nurses always can tell an Australian
oidier by his outstanding pluck when
wounded. No matter what he has to go
thiough, he rarely even winces.
Women have to suffer a lot in war time,
as you know, Thargomindah, but this seems
more than a fair share. One of our dailv
newspapers headed a report of a meeting the
other night this way: “The Country’s
Danger: National Council of Women.” And
the poor ladies were really trying to arouse
tiie country to a true sense of its duty.
The Soldiers’ Comforts Fund, which ivas
originally intended merely to provide Christ-
mas goodies” for Billjim in the trenches,
las extended its scope, and now is in the
generally useful” business, working in peace
and harmony with the Red Cross Society,
i eanwlule, the Jimbillycan workers are
packing some 12,000 of the national williams
with varied gifts, and Mrs. Spencer Browne
feels as proud about it as she ought to be.
Yesterday was Blossom Day. About £2OO
was gathered, and will go into another fund
tor supplying Christmas gifts for soldiers
and nurses.

The fortune which has been waiting for
for the man who can devise a means
of settling Queensland’s prickly-pear pro-
blem is still going begging. Experiments
canied out at Dulacca have an appearance
of success, but the Prickly Pear Selectors’
Union, an organisation formed in the inter-
ests of the men who obtain land on the
undertaking that they will clear it of the
pest, is doubtful. It points out that land
trom which the pear appears to have been
eradicated blossoms with it again even after
other crops have been reaped. The union
contends that nothing but closer settlement
will stop the spread of prickly pear, and it
has therefore appealed to the Government
to give to “pear” selectors - first choice of
pear frontages. This would leave the
heavily cursed areas for the amusement of
experimenters, and make the “small” man’s
burden lighter.

BEFORE THE MIRROR.
By “JEANNETTE.”
“Jeannette,” the well-known writer on Beauty Culture and author of “The Book of Beauty,”
will be pleased to help and advise any reader dn matters of the toilet. Queries should be accom-
panied by a stamped addressed envelope, and directed to “Jeannette,” c/o “Before the Mirror,”.
43 Gray’s Inn Read, London, W.C., England.
These are undoubtedly days of exceptional nervous strain for one and all of
us. Anxiety, with its beauty-destroying influence, is casting its shadow over the
lives of many of us women, and while, perhaps, a few —the more stoical among
us —can avoid worrying over past or prospective events, we may all, with a little
care and forethought, succeed in preventing the result of worry from being notice-
able in our appearance. To these fortunate few this little chat will, however, be
as interesting and instructive as to those who stand in real need of help in toilet
matters, and may be the means of bringing to their notice some hitherto unknown
method of retaining, or regaining, that beauty of face and figure which is the birth-
right of every woman. Soma of the ingredients mentioned below are at present
not generally known to the public, but any good chemist will usually be found to
have a small quantity in stock.
Freckles aiul Hoiv to Cure Them. —lt is always
those with the finest and fairest skins who
freckle most easily, but this knowledge is poor
comfort when the face is disfigured with these
ugly little blemishes. To prevent the forma-
tion of freckles, a wash should be applied to
the face which will form a shield from the
rays of the sun. A simple and inexpensive
one can be made up at home by dissolving an
ounce of cleminite in four tablespoonfuls of
hot water. Shake the bottle, and when cold
apply to the face and allow to dry on the
skin. If this is done every day, renewing the
application whenever the face is washed, and a
Little mercolised wax rubbed into the skin
every night, freckles will not make their
appearance, no matter how hot the sun. To
remove freckles which have already formed,
apply the wax every night, rub it well into
the skin of the face and neck and leave it on
till the morning. Then wash off, using Pilenta
soap and warm water.
The Hair—To Increase the Growth. —To arrest
an undue falling of the hair and increase the
growth, scalp massage and the application of
a good tonic is absolutely necessary. Part the
hair in the centre, and starting at the forehead
massage for at least ten minutes. Then apply
a tonic. A simple and inexpensive one can be
made up at home by mixing one ounce of
boranium (which can be bought at the chem-
ist’s) with a quarter of a pint of bay rum.
Add the boranium to the bay rum, allow to
stand for half an hour, then strain and add
sufficient water to make half a pint. Dab
amongst the roots of the hair with a soft
sponge.
Blackheads, Enlarged Pores, etc. —Blackheads
are unusually troublesome things during the
summer months to those bothered in that way.
The extra warmth makes the skin “give,” and
it looks more greasy than usual, and conse-
quently the blackheads are more noticeable.
To remove them and close up the pores of the
skin dissolve a stymol tablet in a glass of hot
water, allow the effervescence to subside, then
dip a soft sponge into the liquid and bathe
the face. After a few minutes, dry the face
with a soft towel, and the blackheads will be
instantly removed.
To Reduce a Double Chin. —To reduce a double
chin, give hard, firm massage every night with
mercolised wax, stroking the flesh firmly back-
wards from the chin towards the lower portion
of the neck. Do this every night, and in the.
morning bathe with cold water and rub into
the skin a little parsidium jelly. J his treat-
ment will strengthen the flabby tissues anci
restore the contour of the face. Occasionally
a double chin is due to stooping over books or
work, but generally it is the result of putting
on weight. When the latter is the case a levs
clynol berries, eaten regularly every day, will
greatlv assist in reducing the superfluous
flesh. ' A glass of hot water should be sipped
after each meal.
Superfluous Hair. —Dark-complexioned women
are far more frequently troubled with a
growth of superfluous hair than those with
fair skins, and at the first sign of these objec-
tionable hairs steps should be taken to remove
them, or tliev will develop into a strong
growth which will take a very long time to
destrov. A little powdered plieminol applied
to this growth will remove the unsightly blem
ish, destroying it permanently if the hairs be
but few. Two or more applications may be
necessary if the growth be unusually strong,
but about one ounce of plieminol should be
sufficient for the most stubborn case, riiemi-
nol can be obtained at the chemist’s, and a
little powdered alum should be obtained at the
same time for dusting the skin before applying
the plieminol.
Grey or Fading Hair.—To restore the color to
grey or fading hair add one ounce of concen-
trate of tammalite to four ounces "of bay rum.
Apply to the roots with a soft sponge every
night until the desired result is obtained, then
use the lotion two or three times a week. After
three or four days a decided improvement will
be noticed, the hair turning gradually to its
original color. An iron tonic will be found
most beneficial when the gre'yness is caused by
general debility.
Beautiful Hair—How to Shampoo.—Whether
the liair is luxuriant and glossy, or thin and
lanky depends very largely upon the care
bestowed upon it. To keep the hair in good
condition it should be shampooed once in
every two or three weeks. Before shampooing,
massage a little pure olive oil or almond oil
into the scalp, then mix one teaspoonful of
stallax granules .411 a cup of hot water, fill a
basin with warm water, into which the ends
of the hair can fall, and shampoo the stallax
; mixture into the scalp in the usual way.
Rinse, and dry by fanning with a palm fan, or
! in the open air, if the weather permits. Should
j the hair be naturally very greasy, the oil
1 massage should be emitted. " [3

The Inky Way.
“Rosna” : The Honk, that gay little
regimental rag edited by Corporal Phil
Harris —“Philo,” of The Bulletin —and
now of the Australian Divisional Ammuni-
tion Park, “somewhere in France,” has
made its first appearance on the actual field
of slaughter. Former numbers were pub-
lished on the transport coining over. But
the British Censor —the D.A.P. is now with
a British division —won’t allow the present
issue to go outside the war zone. Presum-
ably it contains priceless secrets about the
number of sausages the British army can
consume when put to it. Of the “subs” of
former issues two, Lieut. Imlay and Private
A. L. Brewer, were’ wounded in the early
days at the Dardanelles, so Corpl. Phil has
had to seek a new staff.
* * * *
Stanley Hall stepped out of the S. M.
Herald office the other day after over dO
years’ service there. He did nothing aston-
ishingly brilliant; most of the years in
which he might have done something strik-
ing he spent, instead, in a hard grind so
that daughter Elsie, the pianist, might do
the brilliant things. Heaven only knows
Whether it was worth the cheerful struggle:
she performed before a few kings, taught a
few princelings and then married a man in
the middle of Africa. Before joining the
Herald the very dependable Hall ran a
paper in Fiji and had something to do with
the British annexation of the island.
“J.F.” : Succumbed with startling sudden-
ness, after an operation, Walter Emanuel,
the sprightly young Jew who for a dozen
years has written the first page of London
Punch. His weekly collection of whim-
sical comments on passing events was the
only thing in Punch that many people
looked at —excepting always the paper’s
excellent drawings. Emanuel kept at his
whimsies up to the hour when he submitted
himself to the knife, and his page appeared
as usual on the day of his death. He was
not a member of the staff, but a London
solicitor who preferred to hold on to the
tidy practice he had pulled together before
he began this press work. Since the out-
break of the war he had also been a special
constable, and a chill thus contracted did
the mischief.
* * * *
The Argus in its fiery youth of 1854 and
thereabouts was a regular devil, and pub-
lished things like this :
Ave! it must come, and the tyrant s throne
Is crumbling with our hot tears rusted....
Ye robber rulers, pause no longer ;
Ye cannot stay the opening day,
The world goes on, the light grows stronger ;
The people’s advent’s coming.
After that Hotspur grew aweary and became
a suburban mayor and a J.P.
* * * *
“S.T.S.” : One of the smallest books of
verse on record (5| x 3|, and only eight
pages) comes from Egypt, where it has
been produced by Signaller Skeyhill—for-
merly known as a successful elocutionist in
the Ballarat competitions. As a parodist
he is well enough ; but in serious work he
is—well, he writes things like this :
I’ve often passed those little mounds
Where the deadly bullets meow :
And the air was full of shrapnel—
’Trs called Shrapnel Gully now.
What is selling the booklet (and it lias had
a tremendous sale among the Australians in
Egypt) is a savage summing-up, i'h the
manner of Dennis’s Sentimental Bloke, of
the stay-at-home brigade: —
’E reads ther Mobnin" ’Eralb,
Ah’ sees thcr Turks is on ther run..
Then ’e brags about Australia,
An’ wot ’er boys ’ave done.
’E shines before ther barmaids,
’E’s gpod at beery skitin’,
But round ther corner of ther street
Is where ’e does ’is fig’ntin’!
The unfortunate bard has lost: the sight of
both eyes—only temporarily, it is hoped.
* * * *
Vance Palmer, the Queenslander wlna has
planted many short stories with American
magazines and political articles with Eng-
lish reviews, is returning to Australia
shortly. Since he left these parts he has
run 300 short yarns through his battery, and
is now putting a selection of' them into a
book.

“Jink”: The Lyttelton Times (M.L.)
is nothing if not. realistic in its war news.
Its latest was to announce that “issy Smith
is the first Jew to vin (lie Victoria Cross.”
