Ilenry Lawson foresaw Gallipoli. This
is from “A Hero ip Dingo Scrubs,” pub-
lished in 1902: —
These lanky, sawny bushmen will do things in an
easy-going way some day that'll mgke the old world
sit up and think hard.
Lawson, by the. way, is getting himself
braced up in the Walker Convalescent Hos-
pital. and finding “heaps of material” in a
Corner where he never looked for it before.
* * * *.
Rescued from fire in a boot factory at
Invercargill (M.L.) lately’ was a copy of a
sheet called the Nokomai Herald, pub-
lished in Invercargill in 1871, at Is. per
head. The edition is a very youthful one
(No. 2), and, in the absence of printing
presses, the four pages had been laboriously
written out with pen and ink. It is now
on its way to, the Otago, Early Settlers'
Museum.
* * * *
Since so many of Maoriland's telegraphists
have left for the Dardanelles country editors
have been having a wild time. The press
messages are now mostly transmitted with
a hammer and saw, and in the middle of
observations about meat they are liable to
fly off into unintelligible remarks, and the
harassed country rag can never be sure
whether it’s merely another error or the
name of a new town that the Russian Bear
has called at.

HANDY WORKED
A COMBINATION OF SEVERAL
USEFUL TOOLS IN ONE.
Just the thing for the Home Workshop,
Farm Workshop. Store or Garage.
If- you do your own repair- work in home
workshop, store or garage, here is a device
which you absolutely need. It will greatly
simplify and improve your work.
All you have to do is, holt it to table or
working bench and it is ready for work.
There is a strong, powerful vice with a
jaw opening of 4J inches. The jaws are 4
inches in width and will hold any wood or
metal article firmly and securely.
There is a pipe vice which will grip pipe
up to 11 inches in diameter.
There is an anvil, strongly and substan-
tially made, and a steel cutting hardie for cutt-
ing wire or steel.
There is a drill press which can be operated
at two speeds. There are two drills and a
rest to hold the job to the drill.
There is a carborundum grinding wheel
which will keep the edges of your wood or
metal-cutting tools in tip-top shape. It has
three speeds : direct, 4 to 1 and 16 to 1.
The materials used are ol first quality, so
that you can count upon the Stewart Handy
Worker lasting a lifetime. The gearing is
cut from solid steel bar ; so are the two cutting
drills furnished. Taken all in all, this wonder-
ful tool has no equal in quality, workmanship
and utility.
Here is a workshop equipment in itself
which, with all the features and attachments
above enumerated, you can buy from your
Storekeeper for 15 S . Complete.

E. A. (“Nulla”) Roberts, killed at Galli-
poli, besides being one of the most popular
of Sydney solicitors, was a conspicuously
fine athlete. As a youngster at King’s
School, Parramatta, he was a topnotcher;
then he played Rugby Union football,
and took rep. honors; later on the golf
microbe got in the blood. Arriving at the
Front he had barely time to look round
before a bullet found him.
The One Big Union idea, which was given
a jolt when the N. S. Wales Registrar
stopped the amalgamation of the Railway
Workers and the A.W.TJ. on the ground of
an ineffective ballot, is getting into its stride
again. The executives of the two concerns
are conferring, and have already agreed that
tickets for one are to be recognised by the
other. The movement ought to be blessed,
(if course; but the blessing is withheld pend-
ing proof that the Big Union proposes to obey
the law and not flout it. So far, the bigger
the union the less disposed has it been to
give Arbitration a chance. Which has been
cruel to the poor little under-dog unions, for
Arbitration is the only hope they have.
This is insisted on with the emphasis of
large type in the official argument justifying
the amendment of the Constitution :
We are at war, but the money-lender insists upon
his interest. The cost of living has risen. Employ-
ment is becoming scarce.
Now it is to be hoped someone will tell the
politicians that the way to dodge interest is
not to sign the p.n.; that employment is
scarce because the politicians have neglected
the tariff; that the cost of living in Aus-
tralia is not half as big a scandal as the
cost of seven Parliaments, cocked hats, re-
freshment rooms and Ushers of the Black
Rod. Now what do they propose to do
about those things? Or, rather, when are
they going to do something about those
things? They have proposed to do some-
thing too long already.
That ponderous periodical, N.S.W. Gov-
ernment Gazette, published on October 3
the usual statement of the General Loan
Account. Recent expenditure totals
£1,631,788 3s. lOd. To show the deadly
earnestness with which N. S. Wales loads
the bill on to our stand-by, posterity, wit-
ness items such as these: —
Water supply, Nepean River .. .. £1 1 0
Bridge over Sydney Harbor (towards) 2 19 7
Tort Kcmbla improvements . . ..154
Cottage homes, invalid children .. 15 7
Galong to Burrowa Railway . . ..171
Sydney Harbor Bridge (further sum) 2 9 0
We’ve got to that stage, apparently, when
we can’t pay 255. out of revenue even for
the sick kid.

MuUengandra, Via Albury. N. S. W.—
“For twelve months 1 suffered a great deal
with a rash. At first there appeared on my
face an itchy red patch which caused mo
much pain and it disfigured my face greatly.
At first I thought that it was only a rash
but after a time the trouble became so irri-
tating that I was advised to try some oint-
ment. This I did but without success.

Cuticura Ointment and applied them as
directed and almost immediately after
using the Cuticura Soap and Ointment my

using them both and soon I was completely
cured of this nasty rash.” (Signed) Miss

Business, Robbery, etc.
“War”: I was shown last week by a
manufacturer a few miles from Sydney
samples of spokes and pick- and hammer-
handies made from spotted gum and other
local timbers. They were well finished and
seemed in every respect equal to the im-
ported Yankee article, and could be sold at
about half the money. City wholesalers
had, nevertheless, turned them down be-
cause they could not be bothered to make a
market for them. Wonderful are the ways
of patriotism ! Now, here’s a job for a per-
manent Made-in-Australia committee an
organisation to which a little man lpight go
for advice as to how to get his product to
the buyers. The little man usually comes
a cropper because he doesn t know the right
people to go to. # ,
“Warre” : Mention of the shocking lack of
co-operative spirit among the cookies of
X. S. Wales reminds me of the fate or an
inland co-operative butter factory a few
years ago. The president was the boss
cocky of the district, also the largest sup-
plier of milk. Suddenly his cans ceased
journeying to the co-op. and started going
to a private factory in another town, which
had fished for him with an extra tuppence
a gallon and hooked him. The explosion
which followed was so terrific that the
remains of the co-op. factory have not yet
come to earth. I met a wheat cocky the
other day, an erstwhile advocate of co-
operation. who denounced the principle as
all wrong. Had he not bought a bag of
sugar from the storekeeper 6d. cheaper than
he could get it from the co-op. society in
which he had a couple of shares'?
* * * *
“D.F.”: All the financiers don’t go into
Parliament. A while ago the Horton Koad
Trust, a minute public body in the back-
blocks of Stanley (Tas.), evolved a simple
scheme fox* carrying but public works, -the
Trust saved up a bit out of. revenue from
road rates, and after that, if any of the
constituents wanted a road, the Trust lent
him or them the necessary money, charging
5 per cent, interest. Once the scheme got
going the public works policy of that pai-
ticular body was self-supporting, and the
Trust was never worried by doubts about
whether its public works were reproductive
or not; it knew they were. The thing
worked well for a while; and then someone
pointed out that it was illegal or something,
and the pearl of an idea was dropped.
* * *
By way of protest against the unfulfilled
prediction of a water famine which circu-
lated a year ago, Melbourne Board ot
Works has put forth an illustrated book
showing the three water-supply systems
whereon the city -relies for its daily wash.
The book contains reassuring letterpress as
well as pleasing pictures ; also, the announce-
ment that a volume dealing with the sewage
system will be published later on.. It seems
that the much-abused Board, since taking
control in 1891, has spent £1,316,000 on
construction work; and an explanation of
what the Board has to show for all that
money seems, all things considered, a per-
missible extravagance. # #
A calculation has been made in London
of the cost of a reconstructed man. A pair
of artificial legs costs about £3O and a pair
of arms about £2O. Ears, all shapes, with
good, serviceable drums, cost £lo each.
Eyes, all colors, are quoted at £6 a pair.
Without heart, brains or bowels a man is
worth about £lOO. # ,

THE PHARISEE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He prayed, as did that Pharisee of old
In solemn strain to listening Deity:
“I thank Thee, (tod, I am as other men,
No better and no worse. I sometimes lie;
L pilfer in a strictly legal way ;
I take the unearned increment where’er
And whensoe’er I see I safely can ;
I spill no mortal's blood, although men shed
Their daily sweat to build my lordly pile ;
And here and there I do a kindly act.
I cherish all my children, love my wife,
And hanker not for Naboth’s little plot
Or brave Uriah’s frail and beauteous bride.
Of Envy, Hatred, Malice and the like
I own I have my tidy mortal share.
But as for boasting I am not as others,
But built of some superior clod or clay
I’m not so deadly foolish.
Thou, O Lord,
Hast made me as I am, and Thou must bear
The burden of my follies and my virtues.
That Hebrew ass who brayed of self-made goodness,
Within that Jewish Temple fair of old,
Relieved Thee of a heavy load, 0 Maker,
But 1 am not so kind.”
Then rose
That other wight, as did the Publican.
•‘Behold in me,” he softly said, “O Lord,
A worm who walks in dust east-down and vile;
Who knows himself the chief of hapless sinners.
And therefore seeks Thine aid. Beyond my fellows
How deeply have I fallen! In the dirt
Long have I grovelled low, and were it not
Thou didst extend a special helping Hand
To drag me forth o’ the mire, there lay 1 yet,
A mate for maggot comrades. Chosen now
1 stand at Thy right hand to show how great
Hath been Thy saving grace! - ’
Then said the Lord:
“O Voice Abject! Thy proud humility
Hath solved a mighty problem troubling Me,
And rid the lleav'ns of grave perplexity;
For, till thy speech had closed its vaunting span,
In maze and doubt the Mind Immortal ran
Which was the Pharisee, the Publican?”
•QUEENSLAND.

Miss Marian Marcus-Clarke, the favorite Actress, is the youngest daughter of Marcus Clarke,
whose thrilling novel, “For the Term of His Natural Life,” has made him world famous. Miss
Marcus-Clarke made her name in America as “Mrs. Whipple’’ in Jack Mason’s success, “The
M itching Hour,” and as “Martha” in “The Holy Citv.” Returning to her native land,
Australia, she played all the principal character parts with the Fred Niblo-Josephine Cohan Co.
Miss Marcus-Clarke writes to us from the Criterion Theatre:—
I am dropping you a line to tell you how good I have found Rexona Soap. As a
skin beautifier and as a shampoo for the hair I have never found anything nearly so
good, and it is delightfully refreshing. Everyone I have recommended it to has been
so pleased with it. I have also used Rexona Ointment. It is excellent, and I think
there is nothing on the market that in any way approaches the Rexona Remedies.
I wish yon much success with them. Yours faithfully,
(Sgd.) MARIAN MARCUS-CLARKE.

The Gods Who Are Gone and
the Gods Who Remain.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A PERFECT night, no wind, no cloud ;
Dee]) purple skies decked out with
stars—
The funeral pall to gods allowed
Whom Earth once knew as avatars;
The cool, clear silence all around
Prims to the world's remotest bound.
No man could look on such a night
Unmoved by its surpassing peace
And glory. ’Tis as though the might
Of God had ordered Strife to cease
And all His worlds from East to West
From North to South —to be at rest!
la yon unfathomable well
Of crystal ether, gemmed with suns,
Wherein vast constellations dwell
And the superb Galaxy runs —
Beyond the reach of mortal gaze
Reign all the Gods of Elder Days.
Great Jove, the King of Heaven, is there;
His feebler thunders left behind ;
A starry realm, s.> vast, so fair
Delights him to his inmost mind;
Freed from all greed and lust and war,
Jove holds high state for evermore!
Athene, beautiful and wise,
Rules a more rare and radiant sphere ;
With laughter in her frank grey eyes
She laid aside her shield and spear
Long, long ago ! The endless hours
For her are filled with feast and flowers.
Poseid m, Foul of Earth’s small seas,
Who drove his dolphins through the foam,
Enjoys eternities of ease
In his celestial mountain-home;
Where stormy waters never roar
He prunes star-clusters by the score!
Apollo, ever fair and young,
Best-loved by all the sons of men,
Since he left Earth has never sung,
And vows he will not sing again ;
His lyre and bow lie idle ; he
Is shepherding young Nebulae !
Majestic Juno, Heaven’s proud Queen —
Voluptuous in Olympian years—
Dwells in a distant star serene
Where never jealous hate appears,
And passes countless peaceful hours
Amid her golden bees and flowers!
And Mercury, Jove’s herald bright,
The graceful, smiling, roguish god,
Is seen no more in helm of light.
Now with his winged sandals shod.
In lilac glades where fountains play
He dreams Eternity away!
And Venus —ah. divine, supreme.
Immortal! —what deal- star doth hold?
The constellated orbs that gleam
From pole to pole in burnished gold
Know not if she doth reign, or live.
Or is on Earth a fugitive.
Of all the glorious company
That gathered round Imperial Jove
To rule, or feast with ecstasy
In the Olympian halls above,
But two passed not beyond the stars —
Fair, wandering Venus, and fierce Mars !
N.s.w.
O. C. CABOT.
The Shirker.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
OH no, it's not the war. It’s a story
that points a moral: Never be a
gentleman to a lady unless you in-
tend to marry her.
Five years ago I met an innocent, lily-
white flower from the country. Her virtue
would have made a nun’s look cheap. For
three successive nights I made a pretence
of being a gentleman, and I actually made
her think I teas a gentleman. That’s how I
fell. To-day I lie enmeshed —hopelessly
enmeshed.
It happened so simply. It always does.
What I then thought was her innocence I
now think was her crudity. It was never
her cunning, for she had no artistry about
her. But it's all the same now.
In those three nights I kissed her only 33
times. She never kissed me once. Out-
wardly she was quite passive. I tired. She
didn’t. I went away. That was all. At
least I’m almost sure that was all.
For five years I never saw her, though
she Avrote frequently. I never replied. No,
there was nothing brazen about her. She
simply trusted me implicitly, and pardoned
or overlooked my neglect. Being a cour-
teous man I sent her five printed Christmas
cards, one each year. While I was sending
her the Christmas cards 1 got hopelessly en-
gaged to another, and I’m most happy in
my hopelessness even unto the present time.
That’s the position.
Three days ago she called on me. I
gulped—and smiled. An iceberg would
have been frozen. I afternoon-tea’d her and
got away as soon as I could, weakly excus-
ing myself and mumbling that I hoped I
Avould see her soon again.
She called again this morning—just
looked at me and Avaited for me to do some-
thing. I frantically seized my hat and
vaguely hurried her street-Avards. Then I
paused to try to think. I recollected an
urgent appointment with a man who had
forged some cheques. She continued look-
ing but didn’t say a word. I was con-
quered, and I walked her any old hoAv into
and out of lanes and byAvays and highways.
At last she commenced talking. I merely
had to listen and interpose a few “Yeses”
and “Noes” and “Oh reallys,” and think of
Avays and means of getting free.
I Avas brought to when she Avas in the
middle of some patter about the recent trans-
gressions of an elder brother. It appeared
that he had just been married to a clergy-
man’s daughter of whom his family did not
approve. AnyhoAV, that’s about the last I
remember before the final bell Avent
“And mother sent her best love to you”
(as Heaven is my witness I’ve never seen
the woman). "They—they often ask me at
home if we’re really engaged, and,” shyly, "I
don’t know what to tell them exactly.”
(Don’t laugh, you silly fools, don’t laugh
—it’s a major tragedy. Can’t you see there’s
no escape for me?)
I pulled my face together and made a
miserable, utterly-futile attempt to look
grave and deadly earnest and secretly sor-
rowful. 1 told her 1 was already married
to a certain extent elsewhere, and. more-
over, that my reputation was so muddy that
I was unfit to kiss her feet. Then, fool of
fools, I laughed !
The laugh was the laugh of sheer hysteria
and utter despair; but I saw she thought I
was trying to be funny or something.
Tersely, my burning words had cut no ice.
As I turned to flee under some horrible
pretence she said she would tell them at
home how happy I was.
I shall have to murder her. It will be
better for my future wife and for me. And
for her —she was made to mix only with
angels.
D. V.
Bail.
[Fob Tub Bulletin.]
IIALMERS swung his solitary way
J around the prison yard. He gazed
before him with stony aloofness, in
his eyes a frozen contempt for the other
occupants of the yard. Two of these walked
with dragging steps and down-bent heads.
They were defaulting bank clerks. Another
slouched stolidly on his way with expres-
sionless eyes; lie was committed for in-
decent assault. And one walked with
greedy, unblinking eye’s turned to the little
patch of blue sky that canopied the place
of shame. This was a slim, strong French
Canadian with a round face and clear eyes
as blue as the sky he watched. He was in
for murder of the woman he had loved.
Once again Chalmers was going over the
details of his flight and his arrest. If
that wharf-laborer had not recognised him
he would have been safe now on his way to
Norway. If he had told Jupp twelve hours
sooner of the state of his affairs, Jupp could
have raised the money. God ! If only he
had sold out when he found he was losing
ground ! Forgery ! They could give a man
life for that. And the Raw Society had
got hold of the deed, lie wondered what
Jack ilaliam had thought, sitting there,
with his thin shoulders hunched. Had he
thought of the nights they had swatted
together, or of how Chalmers had bucked
him up and kept him to scratch before their
final ? Struck from the rolls after all his
hard work! Five years at least —and
struck from the rolls ! Why hadn’t he been
content to stick to serving behind a counterV
His family had put him to it. lie remem-
bered his apprenticeship days—how tall and
raw-boned he had been, his wrists always
too long for the ready-to-wear suits old
Maclean stocked. Douglas had been at the
next counter, and how he had admired
Douglas ! Heaven! how he had loathed the
shop. He used to watch the boys and girls
on tlieir way to the High Schools, and he
envied them. Then one day he had to go to
court to give evidence concerning some
theft, and at once he had known that he
would be a lawyer.
Thank Heaven, he'd be out of this to-
morrow. Douglas and Howden would bail
him. How lie loved Howden and admired
Douglas! Yes, sir; there were two men
for you. Friendsf Well, he should say
so! He threw back his shoulders and
swung round with a sudden jauntiness that
drew from the others looks of envy. He’d
be bailed out to-morrow for certain. The
warders said so quite openly. That sort
always had moneyed friends who stuck to
them. One of the bank clerks looked around
wearily. He wondered how Jo and the kid
were managing. Two sureties of £5O each,
and himself in a hundred ! God ! Nobody’d
do it for him! He’d like to see the kid
again—and Jo. He wondered if she’d stick
to him. She was so pretty, and had such
a dashing way with her. A warder called
to him, and he was taken from the yard—
not to be seen again by the others till the
day of his trial, when his wife —tear-
stained, followed every motion of the Judge
with such pitiful intensity that twelve
months’ hard was commuted to six months
without.
By the next afternoon the other bank
clerk had been taken away by friends. Now
Chalmers walked in company with a mur-
derer, and the something infinitely worse
than a murderer. Howden would come in
the afternoon train, he told himself, and
no doubt Douglas was waiting for him to
arrive. Wouldn’t he be glad to get out!
One of his well-cut Oxfords was scraped
by the jagged stone in the right-hand corner
near the wall. “In Debtors’ Yard the
stones are hard,” he quoted grimly, eyeing
the bruised leather. In the distance a train
whistled—the 4.10. Howden would be in
that. There was a man for you—such charm
—such breeding! A perfect specimen of
the output of an English Public School!
His code of honor! Why, it did a man good
to know Howden —and then, what a play-
fellow he was! Come to think of it, he’d
never known how to play till he’d met Ilow-
den. He’d been such a serious, ambitious
Prig.
The next afternoon, and the next, and
still the next, Chalmers walked the prison
yard. His eyes had lost some of their con-
fidence, and the warders saw the ugly thing
called Fear lurking in their depths. Pacing
slowly up and down there flashed before him
the crowds of “Good fellows” who had drunk
his whisky and won his money at cards.
He thought of the women—smiling, alluring
with whom he had flirted, and now not a
sign from one of them. Even the thing
unspeakable had had friends to come for-
ward and stand by it. But he who had set
out with such hopes and high ambition was
walled in, beneath a square of God’s bine
sky, in company with a murderer.
John Egan.

For when diseased the kidneys fail to purify the blood, so that uric acid
and waste matter collecff in the system, causing rheumatism, stone, dropsy,
gravel, sciatica, lumbago and other complications.
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills are a tonic for the kidneys, which they

Pies and Passion.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ml' friend Miss Simpson told me the
story of Maudie’s love affair in
nightly instalments, while I ate late
dinners in the upstairs room at Smithwick’s.
Bveryone knows Smithwick's, where one
gets a large, nourishing pie, a pot of tea
and a deafening for sixpence. But few
know it when the crash of cutlery on marble
slabs, the scraping of chairs, the shrill
shrieks of Pie and tea one !” are over for
the day, and only a yawning cashier and a
weary waitress lurk in shadowy corners of
the big empty room. Pew, too, have soft-
ened the hearts of Smithwick’s girls, who
are, for the most part, cold and distant.
( ertainly they always serve men before
women, hut in a manner that suggests
obedience _to some law of nature rather
than the indulgence of a preference.
I am a mild, inoffensive person, and new
acquaintances treat me with confidence or
indifference, according to their individual
n ? e J s - Realising that, with a long period
dinners looming ahead, indifference
noni Miss Simpson would mean starvation,
1 laid myself out to win her confidence, and
1 am happy to say succeeded, though on the
lirst night The frail barque of my hopes
was nearly wrecked on a misunderstanding.
I ordered sw r eet corn. Miss Simpson
hi ought me pork and beans. I protested
mildly.
That is sweet corn." said Miss Simpson,
gazing fixedly over my head.
"Pardon me, but it appears to me to be
pork and beans, a dish I dislike exceedingly,”
I said.
"It is sweet corn,” said Miss Simpson,
with marked coldness.
I saw ruin and disaster and an endless
succession of tepid dinners. I hate cold
plates, and I surrendered. I apologised
abjectly. And then I asked for pork and
beans, and Miss Simpson forgave me and
brought me sweet corn, and all was well.
A fcwv nights later I wanted sweet corn
again. So I ordered pork and beans, and
to my horror .Miss Simpson brought them,
her eyes challenging mine defiantly. Mv
fate trembled in the balance, but a call from
the kitchen saved me. Miss Simpson dis-
appeared. Reside me was an open window.
Below stretched the verandah roof. When
Miss Simpson returned the dish w-as empty.
I noticed some plumbers working at flic
spouting next day, but it wanted cleaning
out in any case.
After that we avoided sweet corn bv
mutual consent, and Miss Simpson brought
me steaming dinners on red-hot plates, and
gave me minute details of the character
and family history of herself and the other
girls.
One night she rushed up with no trace of
her usual queenly calm. and. holding the
menu upside down two inches from my
glasses, said: “What do you think? Maudie
has a boy!”
I disentangled ray eyes, which had be-
come crossed, and replied, “You don’t say
so !” I was sparring for time. Smithwick’s
girls ai’e as undistinguishable to me sepa-
rately as dress-baskets to a luggage porter.
But it would never have done to say so.
(I hate cold plates.) I knew that if the
incident was to be considered as humorous,
Miss Simpson would explosively ejaculate
“Laugh!” at frequent intervals —remini-
scently, and not as a command. I had found
the habit very helpful.
“Yes,” she said. “Fancy old Maudie!
Laugh ! My word! While there’s life
there’s hope, as they say. Mind you, though,
I’m glad.”
“Maudie?” I said thoughtfully.
“Oh, you know Maudie !” said' Miss Simp-
son with a touch of impatience. “The long
slab of a girl with a nice, kind face, like a
cow, that waited on you on my night off.
Not that faces are everything.” (Miss
Simpson is not pretty.) “But she has no
figger or anything. She’s like an ineing
board.”
Miss Simpson ran an approving glance
down the generous curve of her own apron
bib.
“Oh. ah, yes. Of course, I know now.
But I was wondering where you had gone,
and hoping there was nothing wrong, so I
didn’t notice her very closely.”
Ordinarily Miss Simpson would have in-
quired if I wanted to borrow half a crown.
She might even have been alarmed. She
has frequently assured me that a girl can’t
be too careful. But she ignored the per-
sonal application and hurried on :
“Yes. Well, fancy her with a boy 1”
“Surely ‘her days are in the sere and
yellow leaf,’ ’’ I ventured.
Miss Simpson playfully offered to take
two tickets on that one, and requested me
to come to earth. I obeyed, and suggested
that Miss Maud was getting a bit old for
that sort of thing. Miss Simpson agreed
heartily, and estimated Maudie’s age at
about a hundred not out.
“Mind you. though, sometimes they get it
worse when they are old. I’ve often' noticed
that.”
“Henry .Tames lias written a book. ‘The
Dangerous Age.’ ” I said. “That”
.“Perhaps he has,” said Miss Simpson.
“But what I say is wliat’s the use of sticking
inside seven or eight nights reading a book
when you can* go to the pitchers and see it
all done in a couple of hours, and music and
all? Anyway, I noticed there was some-
thing uji about Maudie. Slic’d keep edging
up to the glass and giving her old hair a
poke, and wiping away at the corner table,
and watching the door”
At this stage Miss Simpson interrupted
herself to ask what I was going to have.
Being unable to read upside-down, I did not
know, but suggested thick soup. Miss
Simpson brought me clear, and, ordering me
not to eat that old bread, brought a fresh
loaf from the next table. I hate fresh
bread.
“Well. I've been watching, out, and sure
enough there is a chap coming to her table
by himself every day. He’s—oo—ever so
much younger than her. But he’s a very
gentleman’,y young feller, and awfully nice
to Maudie. and ’pon me word I begin to
wonder if there’s something doing. Of
course in our business you never know. A
feller may like that table because it’s out
of the draught, or there’s a good light to
read his paper, or anything; and if he finds
that the girl’ll fall over herself to bring him
his lunch before anyone else it’s only
natural that he’ll keep on coming. Oo well,
I’ll tell you how it goes.”
“I can’t quite make him out.” said Miss
Simpson a few nights later. “He’s as nice
as pie to Maudie, and he’s got her so she’ll
eat out of his hand. We do give her a time
about him!”
“How unkind of you !” I said.
Miss Simpson was quite surprised. “Why,
if you don’t jolly a girl a bit when she’s got
a boy she’d think you was jealous. Maudie’s
as pleased as a little dog with two tails if
she can get anyone to tease her about
George.”
“And why do you say you can’t under-
stand him?”
“Well. I don’t know. The way he goes
on you’d think he had tickets on Maudie,
but you never know. He may be one of
these fellers that go on like that just be-
cause they like to see people jolly round
them.”
“A bit of an altruist,” I suggested.
“Oh no, I don’t think he’s a wowser,”
said Miss Simpson.
About a week later she rushed up, seeth-
ing with excitement and suppressed news.
“My word, there’s been doings here to-
day! What are you going to have?”
“Beef,” I answered promptly. An in-
stant’s pause to consult the menu would
have been fatal. I think I mentioned before
that I hate cold plates. Miss Simpson sped
like an arrow from the kitchen and slammed
down the beef.
“My word, yes! 'You should have been
here ! You know Em’ly and I used to pull
Maudie’s leg and say ‘Oo ! George has just
come in with such a nice girl!’ and Maudie’d
laugh and say, ‘Oh yes. I know you!’ Beall y
she was getting a bit over the odds the way
she hung around him, and I suppose he
thought so, too. Anyway I was inside get-
ting coffee-cake, and Em’ly rushes in and
she says, ‘Maudie,’ she says, ‘George has just
come in. and lie’s got a girl with him.
Straight! I’m not pulling your leg.’ Maudie
says, ‘I don’t believe you,’ and she went
down to take his order.
“We all started dodging down to have a
look, and sure enough there was a girl. Oh
she was a bit o’ style ! She had a new, full
skirt, and one of those little hats, and bits
of hair over her ears, and her lips all red.
and a silver bag. Maudie went up, and if
you’d heard the way this Bit o’ Style talked
to her. ‘Waitress,’ she says, ‘bring me the
menu.’ ‘There’s no menu here,’ says
Maudie. ‘lt's just light refreshments on this
floor.’ ‘Oh deah !’ says the Bit o’ Style, ‘wlmt
a queer place ! Why did you bring me lieah,
George?’ Then she goes off a treat about
everything. The forks were dirty, and the
tea was cold, and the pie was ‘greasay.’ She
talked to Maudie something awful, I call it.
“George looked as if he’d like to crawl
under the table, though it’s my opinion he’d
put her up to it. So, as it just happened
that the boss was out, I got all the girls to
line up at the door when they were going
out—Maudie too. I held on to her apron,
and I says, ‘Just you have a bit o’ spirit.’
We all looks at them like this,” and Miss
Simpson looked down her nose with ineffable
scorn.
“The Bit o’ Style walks out pulling at her
veil and talking to George, and all of a
sudden she looks round and sees us all stand-
ing there, and what do think she did?”
“Tell me!” I said breathlessly,
“She turned round, and she stuck out her
tongue at us! Not a little bit like that”—
anc j Miss Simpson showed a demure red tij
between her teeth. “But all of it, like this’
__ anc ] Miss Simpson hurled her tongue out
0 f her mouth clear down to the root, like a
chameleon catching a fly.
j dropped my knife and fork on the floor,
and groped for them frantically. It seemed
unbearably indelicate to let my gaze linger
on a portion of Miss Simpson’s anatomy
hitherto decently hidden from the light of
(j a y.
“Good gracious!” I faltered. “What a
shock you must have got!”
“Shock? You could have knocked us
down with a stick, as the sayin’ is. We’d
a n thought she was the real thing, but it
just seemed to put Maudie on her feet again,
j didn’t think she had it in her.
u » s h e says, ‘you’ll oblige me by
not herring to George again. A feller that
resorts with persons of that kind is no gentle-
man’”

ELAINE MACDONALD.
His Home Coming.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HIS land; the land he knew so well
add loved so much
And learned its secrets as but lovers
may—
There seemed in all its quietness a touch
Of hidden troubling. Xocturned in the grey
Of smothering nightfall on a glad day spent,
The kindred things of earth seemed pained
to say
Fond words of solace to the discontent
That brooded in them. “Would lie come, or
stay,
Now lie is dead,” they asked, “and far
away?”
Was it just now the wind lie loved that
stirred
The leaves to rustling welcome? Was the
change
From blackened skies to jubilance of light
But play of fancy? What I saw and heard
It was not these, but something wondrous
strange....
Tt was his spirit homing'in its flight.
MAORILANJ).
BERNARD INGLEBY.
